They were neighbours and comrades. Luk\xc3\xa1shka was sitting on the\ngrass cross-legged like a Tartar, adjusting his nets.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know whose it was\xe2\x80\x94yours, I expect.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas it beyond the pit by the plane tree? Then it is mine! I set the\nnets last night.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka rose and examined the captured pheasant. After stroking the\ndark burnished head of the bird, which rolled its eyes and stretched\nout its neck in terror, Luk\xc3\xa1shka took the pheasant in his hands.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll have it in a pilau tonight. You go and kill and pluck it.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd shall we eat it ourselves or give it to the corporal?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has plenty!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like killing them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Naz\xc3\xa1rka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive it here!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka drew a little knife from under his dagger and gave it a swift\njerk. The bird fluttered, but before it could spread its wings the\nbleeding head bent and quivered.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s how one should do it!\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka, throwing down the\npheasant. \xe2\x80\x9cIt will make a fat pilau.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nNaz\xc3\xa1rka shuddered as he looked at the bird.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, Luk\xc3\xa1shka, that fiend will be sending us to the ambush again\ntonight,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, taking up the bird. (He was alluding to the\ncorporal.) \xe2\x80\x9cHe has sent F\xc3\xb3mushkin to get wine, and it ought to be his\nturn. He always puts it on us.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka went whistling along the cordon.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake the string with you,\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted.\n\nNazirka obeyed.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll give him a bit of my mind today, I really will,\xe2\x80\x9d continued\nNaz\xc3\xa1rka. \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s say we won\xe2\x80\x99t go; we\xe2\x80\x99re tired out and there\xe2\x80\x99s an end of\nit! No, really, you tell him, he\xe2\x80\x99ll listen to you. It\xe2\x80\x99s too bad!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGet along with you! What a thing to make a fuss about!\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka,\nevidently thinking of something else. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat bosh! If he made us turn\nout of the village at night now, that would be annoying: there one can\nhave some fun, but here what is there? It\xe2\x80\x99s all one whether we\xe2\x80\x99re in\nthe cordon or in ambush. What a fellow you are!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd are you going to the village?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll go for the holidays.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cG\xc3\xbarka says your Dun\xc3\xa1yka is carrying on with F\xc3\xb3mushkin,\xe2\x80\x9d said Naz\xc3\xa1rka\nsuddenly.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, let her go to the devil,\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka, showing his regular\nwhite teeth, though he did not laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cAs if I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t find another!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cG\xc3\xbarka says he went to her house. Her husband was out and there was\nF\xc3\xb3mushkin sitting and eating pie. G\xc3\xbarka stopped awhile and then went\naway, and passing by the window he heard her say, \xe2\x80\x98He\xe2\x80\x99s gone, the\nfiend.... Why don\xe2\x80\x99t you eat your pie, my own? You needn\xe2\x80\x99t go home for\nthe night,\xe2\x80\x99 she says. And G\xc3\xbarka under the window says to himself,\n\xe2\x80\x98That\xe2\x80\x99s fine!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re making it up.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, quite true, by Heaven!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, if she\xe2\x80\x99s found another let her go to the devil,\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka,\nafter a pause. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s no lack of girls and I was sick of her anyway.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, see what a devil you are!\xe2\x80\x9d said Naz\xc3\xa1rka. \xe2\x80\x9cYou should make up to\nthe cornet\xe2\x80\x99s girl, Mary\xc3\xa1nka. Why doesn\xe2\x80\x99t she walk out with any one?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka frowned. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat of Mary\xc3\xa1nka? They\xe2\x80\x99re all alike,\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you just try...\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you think? Are girls so scarce in the village?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAnd Luk\xc3\xa1shka recommenced whistling, and went along the cordon pulling\nleaves and branches from the bushes as he went. Suddenly, catching\nsight of a smooth sapling, he drew the knife from the handle of his\ndagger and cut it down. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat a ramrod it will make,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, swinging\nthe sapling till it whistled through the air.\n\nThe Cossacks were sitting round a low Tartar table on the earthen floor\nof the clay-plastered outer room of the hut, when the question of whose\nturn it was to lie in ambush was raised. \xe2\x80\x9cWho is to go tonight?\xe2\x80\x9d\nshouted one of the Cossacks through the open door to the corporal in\nthe next room.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is to go?\xe2\x80\x9d the corporal shouted back. \xe2\x80\x9cUncle Burl\xc3\xa1k has been and\nF\xc3\xb3mushkin too,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, not quite confidently. \xe2\x80\x9cYou two had better go,\nyou and Naz\xc3\xa1rka,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, addressing Luk\xc3\xa1shka. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd Ergush\xc3\xb3v must\ngo too; surely he has slept it off?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t sleep it off yourself so why should he?\xe2\x80\x9d said Naz\xc3\xa1rka in a\nsubdued voice.\n\nThe Cossacks laughed.\n\nErgush\xc3\xb3v was the Cossack who had been lying drunk and asleep near the\nhut. He had only that moment staggered into the room rubbing his eyes.\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka had already risen and was getting his gun ready.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe quick and go! Finish your supper and go!\xe2\x80\x9d said the corporal; and\nwithout waiting for an expression of consent he shut the door,\nevidently not expecting the Cossack to obey. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course,\xe2\x80\x9d thought he,\n\xe2\x80\x9cif I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been ordered to I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t send anyone, but an officer\nmight turn up at any moment. As it is, they say eight _abreks_ have\ncrossed over.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I suppose I must go,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Ergush\xc3\xb3v, \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s the regulation.\nCan\xe2\x80\x99t be helped! The times are such. I say, we must go.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nMeanwhile Luk\xc3\xa1shka, holding a big piece of pheasant to his mouth with\nboth hands and glancing now at Naz\xc3\xa1rka, now at Ergush\xc3\xb3v, seemed quite\nindifferent to what passed and only laughed at them both. Before the\nCossacks were ready to go into ambush. Uncle Er\xc3\xb3shka, who had been\nvainly waiting under the plane tree till night fell, entered the dark\nouter room.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, lads,\xe2\x80\x9d his loud bass resounded through the low-roofed room\ndrowning all the other voices, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going with you. You\xe2\x80\x99ll watch for\nCh\xc3\xa9chens and I for boars!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\n\n Chapter VIII\n\n\nIt was quite dark when Uncle Er\xc3\xb3shka and the three Cossacks, in their\ncloaks and shouldering their guns, left the cordon and went towards the\nplace on the T\xc3\xa9rek where they were to lie in ambush. Naz\xc3\xa1rka did not\nwant to go at all, but Luk\xc3\xa1shka shouted at him and they soon started.\nAfter they had gone a few steps in silence the Cossacks turned aside\nfrom the ditch and went along a path almost hidden by reeds till they\nreached the river. On its bank lay a thick black log cast up by the\nwater. The reeds around it had been recently beaten down.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall we lie here?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Naz\xc3\xa1rka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not?\xe2\x80\x9d answered Luk\xc3\xa1shka. \xe2\x80\x9cSit down here and I\xe2\x80\x99ll be back in a\nminute. I\xe2\x80\x99ll only show Daddy where to go.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is the best place; here we can see and not be seen,\xe2\x80\x9d said\nErgush\xc3\xb3v, \xe2\x80\x9cso it\xe2\x80\x99s here we\xe2\x80\x99ll lie. It\xe2\x80\x99s a first-rate place!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nNaz\xc3\xa1rka and Ergush\xc3\xb3v spread out their cloaks and settled down behind\nthe log, while Luk\xc3\xa1shka went on with Uncle Er\xc3\xb3shka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s not far from here. Daddy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka, stepping softly in\nfront of the old man; \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll show you where they\xe2\x80\x99ve been\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m the only\none that knows, Daddy.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cShow me! You\xe2\x80\x99re a fine fellow, a regular Snatcher!\xe2\x80\x9d replied the old\nman, also whispering.\n\nHaving gone a few steps Luk\xc3\xa1shka stopped, stooped down over a puddle,\nand whistled. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s where they come to drink, d\xe2\x80\x99you see?\xe2\x80\x9d He spoke in\na scarcely audible voice, pointing to fresh hoof-prints.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cChrist bless you,\xe2\x80\x9d answered the old man. \xe2\x80\x9cThe boar will be in the\nhollow beyond the ditch,\xe2\x80\x9d he added. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll watch, and you can go.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka pulled his cloak up higher and walked back alone, throwing\nswift glances now to the left at the wall of reeds, now to the T\xc3\xa9rek\nrushing by below the bank. \xe2\x80\x9cI daresay he\xe2\x80\x99s watching or creeping along\nsomewhere,\xe2\x80\x9d thought he of a possible Ch\xc3\xa9chen hillsman. Suddenly a loud\nrustling and a splash in the water made him start and seize his musket.\nFrom under the bank a boar leapt up\xe2\x80\x94his dark outline showing for a\nmoment against the glassy surface of the water and then disappearing\namong the reeds. Luk\xc3\xa1shka pulled out his gun and aimed, but before he\ncould fire the boar had disappeared in the thicket. Luk\xc3\xa1shka spat with\nvexation and went on. On approaching the ambuscade he halted again and\nwhistled softly. His whistle was answered and he stepped up to his\ncomrades.\n\nNaz\xc3\xa1rka, all curled up, was already asleep. Ergush\xc3\xb3v sat with his legs\ncrossed and moved slightly to make room for Luk\xc3\xa1shka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow jolly it is to sit here! It\xe2\x80\x99s really a good place,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cDid\nyou take him there?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cShowed him where,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Luk\xc3\xa1shka, spreading out his cloak. \xe2\x80\x9cBut\nwhat a big boar I roused just now close to the water! I expect it was\nthe very one! You must have heard the crash?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did hear a beast crashing through. I knew at once it was a beast. I\nthought to myself: \xe2\x80\x98Luk\xc3\xa1shka has roused a beast,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d Ergush\xc3\xb3v said,\nwrapping himself up in his cloak. \xe2\x80\x9cNow I\xe2\x80\x99ll go to sleep,\xe2\x80\x9d he added.\n\xe2\x80\x9cWake me when the cocks crow. We must have discipline. I\xe2\x80\x99ll lie down\nand have a nap, and then you will have a nap and I\xe2\x80\x99ll watch\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s the\nway.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cLuckily I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to sleep,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Luk\xc3\xa1shka.\n\nThe night was dark, warm, and still. Only on one side of the sky the\nstars were shining, the other and greater part was overcast by one huge\ncloud stretching from the mountaintops. The black cloud, blending in\nthe absence of any wind with the mountains, moved slowly onwards, its\ncurved edges sharply defined against the deep starry sky. Only in front\nof him could the Cossack discern the T\xc3\xa9rek and the distance beyond.\nBehind and on both sides he was surrounded by a wall of reeds.\nOccasionally the reeds would sway and rustle against one another\napparently without cause. Seen from down below, against the clear part\nof the sky, their waving tufts looked like the feathery branches of\ntrees. Close in front at his very feet was the bank, and at its base\nthe rushing torrent. A little farther on was the moving mass of glassy\nbrown water which eddied rhythmically along the bank and round the\nshallows. Farther still, water, banks, and cloud all merged together in\nimpenetrable gloom. Along the surface of the water floated black\nshadows, in which the experienced eyes of the Cossack detected trees\ncarried down by the current. Only very rarely sheet-lightning, mirrored\nin the water as in a black glass, disclosed the sloping bank opposite.\nThe rhythmic sounds of night\xe2\x80\x94the rustling of the reeds, the snoring of\nthe Cossacks, the hum of mosquitoes, and the rushing water, were every\nnow and then broken by a shot fired in the distance, or by the gurgling\nof water when a piece of bank slipped down, the splash of a big fish,\nor the crashing of an animal breaking through the thick undergrowth in\nthe wood. Once an owl flew past along the T\xc3\xa9rek, flapping one wing\nagainst the other rhythmically at every second beat. Just above the\nCossack\xe2\x80\x99s head it turned towards the wood and then, striking its wings\nno longer after every other flap but at every flap, it flew to an old\nplane tree where it rustled about for a long time before settling down\namong the branches. At every one of these unexpected sounds the\nwatching Cossack listened intently, straining his hearing, and screwing\nup his eyes while he deliberately felt for his musket.\n\nThe greater part of the night was past. The black cloud that had moved\nwestward revealed the clear starry sky from under its torn edge, and\nthe golden upturned crescent of the moon shone above the mountains with\na reddish light. The cold began to be penetrating. Naz\xc3\xa1rka awoke, spoke\na little, and fell asleep again. Luk\xc3\xa1shka feeling bored got up, drew\nthe knife from his dagger-handle and began to fashion his stick into a\nramrod. His head was full of the Ch\xc3\xa9chens who lived over there in the\nmountains, and of how their brave lads came across and were not afraid\nof the Cossacks, and might even now be crossing the river at some other\nspot. He thrust himself out of his hiding-place and looked along the\nriver but could see nothing. And as he continued looking out at\nintervals upon the river and at the opposite bank, now dimly\ndistinguishable from the water in the faint moonlight, he no longer\nthought about the Ch\xc3\xa9chens but only of when it would be time to wake\nhis comrades, and of going home to the village. In the village he\nimagined Dun\xc3\xa1yka, his \xe2\x80\x9clittle soul\xe2\x80\x9d, as the Cossacks call a man\xe2\x80\x99s\nmistress, and thought of her with vexation. Silvery mists, a sign of\ncoming morning, glittered white above the water, and not far from him\nyoung eagles were whistling and flapping their wings. At last the\ncrowing of a cock reached him from the distant village, followed by the\nlong-sustained note of another, which was again answered by yet other\nvoices.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cTime to wake them,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Luk\xc3\xa1shka, who had finished his ramrod and\nfelt his eyes growing heavy. Turning to his comrades he managed to make\nout which pair of legs belonged to whom, when it suddenly seemed to him\nthat he heard something splash on the other side of the T\xc3\xa9rek. He\nturned again towards the horizon beyond the hills, where day was\nbreaking under the upturned crescent, glanced at the outline of the\nopposite bank, at the T\xc3\xa9rek, and at the now distinctly visible\ndriftwood upon it. For one instant it seemed to him that he was moving\nand that the T\xc3\xa9rek with the drifting wood remained stationary. Again he\npeered out. One large black log with a branch particularly attracted\nhis attention. The tree was floating in a strange way right down the\nmiddle of the stream, neither rocking nor whirling. It even appeared\nnot to be floating altogether with the current, but to be crossing it\nin the direction of the shallows. Luk\xc3\xa1shka stretching out his neck\nwatched it intently. The tree floated to the shallows, stopped, and\nshifted in a peculiar manner. Luk\xc3\xa1shka thought he saw an arm stretched\nout from beneath the tree.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSupposing I killed an _abrek_ all by myself!\xe2\x80\x9d he thought, and seized\nhis gun with a swift, unhurried movement, putting up his gun-rest,\nplacing the gun upon it, and holding it noiselessly in position.\nCocking the trigger, with bated breath he took aim, still peering out\nintently.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t wake them,\xe2\x80\x9d he thought. But his heart began beating so fast\nthat he remained motionless, listening. Suddenly the trunk gave a\nplunge and again began to float across the stream towards our bank.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuppose I miss?...\xe2\x80\x9d thought he, and now by the faint light of the moon\nhe caught a glimpse of a Tartar\xe2\x80\x99s head in front of the floating wood.\nHe aimed straight at the head which appeared to be quite near\xe2\x80\x94just at\nthe end of his rifle\xe2\x80\x99s barrel. He glanced cross. \xe2\x80\x9cRight enough it is an\n_abrek!_\xe2\x80\x9d he thought joyfully, and suddenly rising to his knees he\nagain took aim. Having found the sight, barely visible at the end of\nthe long gun, he said: \xe2\x80\x9cIn the name of the Father and of the Son,\xe2\x80\x9d in\nthe Cossack way learnt in his childhood, and pulled the trigger. A\nflash of lightning lit up for an instant the reeds and the water, and\nthe sharp, abrupt report of the shot was carried across the river,\nchanging into a prolonged roll somewhere in the far distance. The piece\nof driftwood now floated not across, but with the current, rocking and\nwhirling.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cStop, I say!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Ergush\xc3\xb3v, seizing his musket and raising\nhimself behind the log near which he was lying.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cShut up, you devil!\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Luk\xc3\xa1shka, grinding his teeth.\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Abreks!_\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhom have you shot?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Naz\xc3\xa1rka. \xe2\x80\x9cWho was it, Luk\xc3\xa1shka?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka did not answer. He was reloading his gun and watching the\nfloating wood. A little way off it stopped on a sand-bank, and from\nbehind it something large that rocked in the water came into view.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did you shoot? Why don\xe2\x80\x99t you speak?\xe2\x80\x9d insisted the Cossacks.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Abreks_, I tell you!\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t humbug! Did the gun go off? ...\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve killed an _abrek_, that\xe2\x80\x99s what I fired at,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Luk\xc3\xa1shka in\na voice choked by emotion, as he jumped to his feet. \xe2\x80\x9cA man was\nswimming...\xe2\x80\x9d he said, pointing to the sandbank. \xe2\x80\x9cI killed him. Just\nlook there.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave done with your humbugging!\xe2\x80\x9d said Ergush\xc3\xb3v again, rubbing his\neyes.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave done with what? Look there,\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka, seizing him by the\nshoulders and pulling him with such force that Ergush\xc3\xb3v groaned.\n\nHe looked in the direction in which Luk\xc3\xa1shka pointed, and discerning a\nbody immediately changed his tone.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cO Lord! But I say, more will come! I tell you the truth,\xe2\x80\x9d said he\nsoftly, and began examining his musket. \xe2\x80\x9cThat was a scout swimming\nacross: either the others are here already or are not far off on the\nother side\xe2\x80\x94I tell you for sure!\xe2\x80\x9d Luk\xc3\xa1shka was unfastening his belt and\ntaking off his Circassian coat.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you up to, you idiot?\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Ergush\xc3\xb3v. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly show\nyourself and you\xe2\x80\x99ve lost all for nothing, I tell you true! If you\xe2\x80\x99ve\nkilled him he won\xe2\x80\x99t escape. Let me have a little powder for my\nmusket-pan\xe2\x80\x94you have some? Naz\xc3\xa1rka, you go back to the cordon and look\nalive; but don\xe2\x80\x99t go along the bank or you\xe2\x80\x99ll be killed\xe2\x80\x94I tell you\ntrue.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cCatch me going alone! Go yourself!\xe2\x80\x9d said Naz\xc3\xa1rka angrily.\n\nHaving taken off his coat, Luk\xc3\xa1shka went down to the bank.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t go in, I tell you!\xe2\x80\x9d said Ergush\xc3\xb3v, putting some powder on the\npan. \xe2\x80\x9cLook, he\xe2\x80\x99s not moving. I can see. It\xe2\x80\x99s nearly morning; wait till\nthey come from the cordon. You go, Naz\xc3\xa1rka. You\xe2\x80\x99re afraid! Don\xe2\x80\x99t be\nafraid, I tell you.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cLuke, I say, Luk\xc3\xa1shka! Tell us how you did it!\xe2\x80\x9d said Naz\xc3\xa1rka.\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka changed his mind about going into the water just then. \xe2\x80\x9cGo\nquick to the cordon and I will watch. Tell the Cossacks to send out the\npatrol. If the _abreks_ are on this side they must be caught,\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what I say. They\xe2\x80\x99ll get off,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ergush\xc3\xb3v, rising. \xe2\x80\x9cTrue,\nthey must be caught!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nErgush\xc3\xb3v and Naz\xc3\xa1rka rose and, crossing themselves, started off for the\ncordon\xe2\x80\x94not along the riverbank but breaking their way through the\nbrambles to reach a path in the wood.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow mind, Luk\xc3\xa1shka\xe2\x80\x94they may cut you down here, so you\xe2\x80\x99d best keep a\nsharp look-out, I tell you!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo along; I know,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Luk\xc3\xa1shka; and having examined his gun\nagain he sat down behind the log.\n\nHe remained alone and sat gazing at the shallows and listening for the\nCossacks; but it was some distance to the cordon and he was tormented\nby impatience. He kept thinking that the other _abreks_ who were with\nthe one he had killed would escape. He was vexed with the _abreks_ who\nwere going to escape just as he had been with the boar that had escaped\nthe evening before. He glanced round and at the opposite bank,\nexpecting every moment to see a man, and having arranged his gun-rest\nhe was ready to fire. The idea that he might himself be killed never\nentered his head.\n\n\n\n\n Chapter IX\n\n\nIt was growing light. The Ch\xc3\xa9chen\xe2\x80\x99s body which was gently rocking in\nthe shallow water was now clearly visible. Suddenly the reeds rustled\nnot far from Luke and he heard steps and saw the feathery tops of the\nreeds moving. He set his gun at full cock and muttered: \xe2\x80\x9cIn the name of\nthe Father and of the Son,\xe2\x80\x9d but when the cock clicked the sound of\nsteps ceased.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHallo, Cossacks! Don\xe2\x80\x99t kill your Daddy!\xe2\x80\x9d said a deep bass voice\ncalmly; and moving the reeds apart Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka came up close to Luke.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI very nearly killed you, by God I did!\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat have you shot?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the old man.\n\nHis sonorous voice resounded through the wood and downward along the\nriver, suddenly dispelling the mysterious quiet of night around the\nCossack. It was as if everything had suddenly become lighter and more\ndistinct.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere now. Uncle, you have not seen anything, but I\xe2\x80\x99ve killed a\nbeast,\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka, uncocking his gun and getting up with unnatural\ncalmness.\n\nThe old man was staring intently at the white back, now clearly\nvisible, against which the T\xc3\xa9rek rippled.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was swimming with a log on his back. I spied him out! ... Look\nthere. There! He\xe2\x80\x99s got blue trousers, and a gun I think.... Do you\nsee?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Luke.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can one help seeing?\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man angrily, and a serious and\nstern expression appeared on his face. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve killed a brave,\xe2\x80\x9d he\nsaid, apparently with regret.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I sat here and suddenly saw something dark on the other side. I\nspied him when he was still over there. It was as if a man had come\nthere and fallen in. Strange! And a piece of driftwood, a good-sized\npiece, comes floating, not with the stream but across it; and what do I\nsee but a head appearing from under it! Strange! I stretched out of the\nreeds but could see nothing; then I rose and he must have heard, the\nbeast, and crept out into the shallow and looked about. \xe2\x80\x98No, you\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x99 I said, as soon as he landed and looked round, \xe2\x80\x98you won\xe2\x80\x99t get\naway!\xe2\x80\x99 Oh, there was something choking me! I got my gun ready but did\nnot stir, and looked out. He waited a little and then swam out again;\nand when he came into the moonlight I could see his whole back. \xe2\x80\x98In the\nname of the Father and of the Son and of the Holy Ghost\xe2\x80\x99... and through\nthe smoke I see him struggling. He moaned, or so it seemed to me. \xe2\x80\x98Ah,\xe2\x80\x99\nI thought, \xe2\x80\x98the Lord be thanked, I\xe2\x80\x99ve killed him!\xe2\x80\x99 And when he drifted\nonto the sand-bank I could see him distinctly: he tried to get up but\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t. He struggled a bit and then lay down. Everything could be\nseen. Look, he does not move\xe2\x80\x94he must be dead! The Cossacks have gone\nback to the cordon in case there should be any more of them.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd so you got him!\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is far away now, my lad!\n...\xe2\x80\x9d And again he shook his head sadly.\n\nJust then the sound reached them of breaking bushes and the loud voices\nof Cossacks approaching along the bank on horseback and on foot. \xe2\x80\x9cAre\nyou bringing the skiff?\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Luk\xc3\xa1shka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re a trump, Luke! Lug it to the bank!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted one of the\nCossacks.\n\nWithout waiting for the skiff Luk\xc3\xa1shka began to undress, keeping an eye\nall the while on his prey.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait a bit, Naz\xc3\xa1rka is bringing the skiff,\xe2\x80\x9d shouted the corporal.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou fool! Maybe he is alive and only pretending! Take your dagger with\nyou!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted another Cossack.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGet along,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Luke, pulling off his trousers. He quickly undressed\nand, crossing himself, jumped, plunging with a splash into the river.\nThen with long strokes of his white arms, lifting his back high out of\nthe water and breathing deeply, he swam across the current of the T\xc3\xa9rek\ntowards the shallows. A crowd of Cossacks stood on the bank talking\nloudly. Three horsemen rode off to patrol. The skiff appeared round a\nbend. Luk\xc3\xa1shka stood up on the sandbank, leaned over the body, and gave\nit a couple of shakes.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite dead!\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted in a shrill voice.\n\nThe Ch\xc3\xa9chen had been shot in the head. He had on a pair of blue\ntrousers, a shirt, and a Circassian coat, and a gun and dagger were\ntied to his back. Above all these a large branch was tied, and it was\nthis which at first had misled Luk\xc3\xa1shka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a carp you\xe2\x80\x99ve landed!\xe2\x80\x9d cried one of the Cossacks who had\nassembled in a circle, as the body, lifted out of the skiff, was laid\non the bank, pressing down the grass.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow yellow he is!\xe2\x80\x9d said another.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere have our fellows gone to search? I expect the rest of them are\non the other bank. If this one had not been a scout he would not have\nswum that way. Why else should he swim alone?\xe2\x80\x9d said a third.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMust have been a smart one to offer himself before the others; a\nregular brave!\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka mockingly, shivering as he wrung out his\nclothes that had got wet on the bank.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis beard is dyed and cropped.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd he has tied a bag with a coat in it to his back.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat would make it easier for him to swim,\xe2\x80\x9d said some one.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, Luk\xc3\xa1shka,\xe2\x80\x9d said the corporal, who was holding the dagger and\ngun taken from the dead man. \xe2\x80\x9cKeep the dagger for yourself and the coat\ntoo; but I\xe2\x80\x99ll give you three rubles for the gun. You see it has a hole\nin it,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, blowing into the muzzle. \xe2\x80\x9cI want it just for a\nsouvenir.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka did not answer. Evidently this sort of begging vexed him but\nhe knew it could not be avoided.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee, what a devil!\xe2\x80\x9d said he, frowning and throwing down the Ch\xc3\xa9chen\xe2\x80\x99s\ncoat. \xe2\x80\x9cIf at least it were a good coat, but it\xe2\x80\x99s a mere rag.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99ll do to fetch firewood in,\xe2\x80\x9d said one of the Cossacks.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cM\xc3\xb3sev, I\xe2\x80\x99ll go home,\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka, evidently forgetting his vexation\nand wishing to get some advantage out of having to give a present to\nhis superior.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, you may go!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake the body beyond the cordon, lads,\xe2\x80\x9d said the corporal, still\nexamining the gun, \xe2\x80\x9cand put a shelter over him from the sun. Perhaps\nthey\xe2\x80\x99ll send from the mountains to ransom it.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt isn\xe2\x80\x99t hot yet,\xe2\x80\x9d said someone.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd supposing a jackal tears him? Would that be well?\xe2\x80\x9d remarked\nanother Cossack.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll set a watch; if they should come to ransom him it won\xe2\x80\x99t do for\nhim to have been torn.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Luk\xc3\xa1shka, whatever you do you must stand a pail of vodka for the\nlads,\xe2\x80\x9d said the corporal gaily.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course! That\xe2\x80\x99s the custom,\xe2\x80\x9d chimed in the Cossacks. \xe2\x80\x9cSee what luck\nGod has sent you! Without ever having seen anything of the kind before,\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ve killed a brave!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBuy the dagger and coat and don\xe2\x80\x99t be stingy, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll let you have the\ntrousers too,\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka. \xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re too tight for me; he was a thin\ndevil.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nOne Cossack bought the coat for a ruble and another gave the price of\ntwo pails of vodka for the dagger.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDrink, lads! I\xe2\x80\x99ll stand you a pail!\xe2\x80\x9d said Luke. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll bring it myself\nfrom the village.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd cut up the trousers into kerchiefs for the girls!\xe2\x80\x9d said Naz\xc3\xa1rka.\n\nThe Cossacks burst out laughing.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave done laughing!\xe2\x80\x9d said the corporal. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd take the body away. Why\nhave you put the nasty thing by the hut?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you standing there for? Haul him along, lads!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka in a commanding voice to the Cossacks, who reluctantly took\nhold of the body, obeying him as though he were their chief. After\ndragging the body along for a few steps the Cossacks let fall the legs,\nwhich dropped with a lifeless jerk, and stepping apart they then stood\nsilent for a few moments. Naz\xc3\xa1rka came up and straightened the head,\nwhich was turned to one side so that the round wound above the temple\nand the whole of the dead man\xe2\x80\x99s face were visible. \xe2\x80\x9cSee what a mark he\nhas made right in the brain,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cHe won\xe2\x80\x99t get lost. His owners\nwill always know him!\xe2\x80\x9d No one answered, and again the Angel of Silence\nflew over the Cossacks.\n\nThe sun had risen high and its diverging beams were lighting up the\ndewy grass. Near by, the T\xc3\xa9rek murmured in the awakened wood and,\ngreeting the morning, the pheasants called to one another. The Cossacks\nstood still and silent around the dead man, gazing at him. The brown\nbody, with nothing on but the wet blue trousers held by a girdle over\nthe sunken stomach, was well shaped and handsome. The muscular arms lay\nstretched straight out by his sides; the blue, freshly shaven, round\nhead with the clotted wound on one side of it was thrown back. The\nsmooth tanned forehead contrasted sharply with the shaven part of the\nhead. The open glassy eyes with lowered pupils stared upwards, seeming\nto gaze past everything. Under the red trimmed moustache the fine lips,\ndrawn at the corners, seemed stiffened into a smile of good-natured\nsubtle raillery. The fingers of the small hands covered with red hairs\nwere bent inward, and the nails were dyed red.\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka had not yet dressed. He was wet. His neck was redder and his\neyes brighter than usual, his broad jaws twitched, and from his healthy\nbody a hardly perceptible steam rose in the fresh morning air.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe too was a man!\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered, evidently admiring the corpse.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, if you had fallen into his hands you would have had short\nshrift,\xe2\x80\x9d said one of the Cossacks.\n\nThe Angel of Silence had taken wing. The Cossacks began bustling about\nand talking. Two of them went to cut brushwood for a shelter, others\nstrolled towards the cordon. Luke and Naz\xc3\xa1rka ran to get ready to go to\nthe village.\n\nHalf an hour later they were both on their way homewards, talking\nincessantly and almost running through the dense woods which separated\nthe T\xc3\xa9rek from the village.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMind, don\xe2\x80\x99t tell her I sent you, but just go and find out if her\nhusband is at home,\xe2\x80\x9d Luke was saying in his shrill voice.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I\xe2\x80\x99ll go round to Y\xc3\xa1mka too,\xe2\x80\x9d said the devoted Naz\xc3\xa1rka. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll have\na spree, shall we?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen should we have one if not today?\xe2\x80\x9d replied Luke.\n\nWhen they reached the village the two Cossacks drank, and lay down to\nsleep till evening.\n\n\n\n\n Chapter X\n\n\nOn the third day after the events above described, two companies of a\nCaucasian infantry regiment arrived at the Cossack village of\nNovoml\xc3\xadnsk. The horses had been unharnessed and the companies\xe2\x80\x99 wagons\nwere standing in the square. The cooks had dug a pit, and with logs\ngathered from various yards (where they had not been sufficiently\nsecurely stored) were now cooking the food; the pay-sergeants were\nsettling accounts with the soldiers. The Service Corps men were driving\npiles in the ground to which to tie the horses, and the quartermasters\nwere going about the streets just as if they were at home, showing\nofficers and men to their quarters. Here were green ammunition boxes in\na line, the company\xe2\x80\x99s carts, horses, and cauldrons in which buckwheat\nporridge was being cooked.\n\nHere were the captain and the lieutenant and the sergeant-major, Onisim\nMikh\xc3\xa1ylovich, and all this was in the Cossack village where it was\nreported that the companies were ordered to take up their quarters:\ntherefore they were at home here.\n\nBut why they were stationed there, who the Cossacks were, and whether\nthey wanted the troops to be there, and whether they were Old Believers\nor not\xe2\x80\x94was all quite immaterial. Having received their pay and been\ndismissed, tired out and covered with dust, the soldiers noisily and in\ndisorder, like a swarm of bees about to settle, spread over the squares\nand streets.\n\nQuite regardless of the Cossacks\xe2\x80\x99 ill will, chattering merrily and with\ntheir muskets clinking, by twos and threes they entered the huts and\nhung up their accoutrements, unpacked their bags, and bantered the\nwomen. At their favourite spot, round the porridge-cauldrons, a large\ngroup of soldiers assembled and with little pipes between their teeth\nthey gazed, now at the smoke which rose into the hot sky, becoming\nvisible when it thickened into white clouds as it rose, and now at the\ncamp fires which were quivering in the pure air like molten glass, and\nbantered and made fun of the Cossack men and women because they do not\nlive at all like Russians. In all the yards one could see soldiers and\nhear their laughter and the exasperated and shrill cries of Cossack\nwomen defending their houses and refusing to give the soldiers water or\ncooking utensils. Little boys and girls, clinging to their mothers and\nto each other, followed all the movements of the troopers (never before\nseen by them) with frightened curiosity, or ran after them at a\nrespectful distance.\n\nThe old Cossacks came out silently and dismally and sat on the earthen\nembankments of their huts, and watched the soldiers\xe2\x80\x99 activity with an\nair of leaving it all to the will of God without understanding what\nwould come of it.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin, who had joined the Caucasian Army as a cadet three months\nbefore, was quartered in one of the best houses in the village, the\nhouse of the cornet, Elias Vas\xc3\xadlich\xe2\x80\x94that is to say at Granny Ul\xc3\xadtka\xe2\x80\x99s.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGoodness knows what it will be like, Dm\xc3\xadtri Andr\xc3\xa9ich,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\npanting Vany\xc3\xbasha to Ol\xc3\xa9nin, who, dressed in a Circassian coat and\nmounted on a Kabard\xc3\xa1 horse which he had bought in Gr\xc3\xb3znoe, was after a\nfive-hours\xe2\x80\x99 march gaily entering the yard of the quarters assigned to\nhim.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, what\xe2\x80\x99s the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, caressing his horse and looking\nmerrily at the perspiring, dishevelled, and worried Vany\xc3\xbasha, who had\narrived with the baggage wagons and was unpacking.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin looked quite a different man. In place of his clean-shaven lips\nand chin he had a youthful moustache and a small beard. Instead of a\nsallow complexion, the result of nights turned into day, his cheeks,\nhis forehead, and the skin behind his ears were now red with healthy\nsunburn. In place of a clean new black suit he wore a dirty white\nCircassian coat with a deeply pleated skirt, and he bore arms. Instead\nof a freshly starched collar, his neck was tightly clasped by the red\nband of his silk _beshmet_. He wore Circassian dress but did not wear\nit well, and anyone would have known him for a Russian and not a Tartar\nbrave. It was the thing\xe2\x80\x94but not the real thing. But for all that, his\nwhole person breathed health, joy, and satisfaction.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it seems funny to you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Vany\xc3\xbasha, \xe2\x80\x9cbut just try to talk to\nthese people yourself: they set themselves against one and there\xe2\x80\x99s an\nend of it. You can\xe2\x80\x99t get as much as a word out of them.\xe2\x80\x9d Vany\xc3\xbasha\nangrily threw down a pail on the threshold. \xe2\x80\x9cSomehow they don\xe2\x80\x99t seem\nlike Russians.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou should speak to the Chief of the Village!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I don\xe2\x80\x99t know where he lives,\xe2\x80\x9d said Vany\xc3\xbasha in an offended tone.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho has upset you so?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ol\xc3\xa9nin, looking round.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe devil only knows. Faugh! There is no real master here. They say he\nhas gone to some kind of _kriga_, and the old woman is a real devil.\nGod preserve us!\xe2\x80\x9d answered Vany\xc3\xbasha, putting his hands to his head.\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow we shall live here I don\xe2\x80\x99t know. They are worse than Tartars, I do\ndeclare\xe2\x80\x94though they consider themselves Christians! A Tartar is bad\nenough, but all the same he is more noble. Gone to the _kriga_ indeed!\nWhat this _kriga_ they have invented is, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know!\xe2\x80\x9d concluded\nVany\xc3\xbasha, and turned aside.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s not as it is in the serfs\xe2\x80\x99 quarters at home, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d chaffed Ol\xc3\xa9nin\nwithout dismounting.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease sir, may I have your horse?\xe2\x80\x9d said Vany\xc3\xbasha, evidently perplexed\nby this new order of things but resigning himself to his fate.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo a Tartar is more noble, eh, Vany\xc3\xbasha?\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Ol\xc3\xa9nin, dismounting\nand slapping the saddle.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you\xe2\x80\x99re laughing! You think it funny,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Vany\xc3\xbasha angrily.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, don\xe2\x80\x99t be angry, Vany\xc3\xbasha,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Ol\xc3\xa9nin, still smiling. \xe2\x80\x9cWait\na minute, I\xe2\x80\x99ll go and speak to the people of the house; you\xe2\x80\x99ll see I\nshall arrange everything. You don\xe2\x80\x99t know what a jolly life we shall\nhave here. Only don\xe2\x80\x99t get upset.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nVany\xc3\xbasha did not answer. Screwing up his eyes he looked contemptuously\nafter his master, and shook his head. Vany\xc3\xbasha regarded Ol\xc3\xa9nin as only\nhis master, and Ol\xc3\xa9nin regarded Vany\xc3\xbasha as only his servant; and they\nwould both have been much surprised if anyone had told them that they\nwere friends, as they really were without knowing it themselves.\nVany\xc3\xbasha had been taken into his proprietor\xe2\x80\x99s house when he was only\neleven and when Ol\xc3\xa9nin was the same age. When Ol\xc3\xa9nin was fifteen he\ngave Vany\xc3\xbasha lessons for a time and taught him to read French, of\nwhich the latter was inordinately proud; and when in specially good\nspirits he still let off French words, always laughing stupidly when he\ndid so.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin ran up the steps of the porch and pushed open the door of the\nhut. Mary\xc3\xa1nka, wearing nothing but a pink smock, as all Cossack women\ndo in the house, jumped away from the door, frightened, and pressing\nherself against the wall covered the lower part of her face with the\nbroad sleeve of her Tartar smock. Having opened the door wider, Ol\xc3\xa9nin\nin the semi-darkness of the passage saw the whole tall, shapely figure\nof the young Cossack girl. With the quick and eager curiosity of youth\nhe involuntarily noticed the firm maidenly form revealed by the fine\nprint smock, and the beautiful black eyes fixed on him with childlike\nterror and wild curiosity.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is _she_,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Ol\xc3\xa9nin. \xe2\x80\x9cBut there will be many others like\nher\xe2\x80\x9d came at once into his head, and he opened the inner door.\n\nOld Granny Ul\xc3\xadtka, also dressed only in a smock, was stooping with her\nback turned to him, sweeping the floor.\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-day to you. Mother! I\xe2\x80\x99ve come about my lodgings,\xe2\x80\x9d he began.\n\nThe Cossack woman, without unbending, turned her severe but still\nhandsome face towards him.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat have you come here for? Want to mock at us, eh? I\xe2\x80\x99ll teach you to\nmock; may the black plague seize you!\xe2\x80\x9d she shouted, looking askance\nfrom under her frowning brow at the new-comer.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin had at first imagined that the way-worn, gallant Caucasian Army\n(of which he was a member) would be everywhere received joyfully, and\nespecially by the Cossacks, our comrades in the war; and he therefore\nfelt perplexed by this reception. Without losing presence of mind\nhowever he tried to explain that he meant to pay for his lodgings, but\nthe old woman would not give him a hearing.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat have you come for? Who wants a pest like you, with your scraped\nface? You just wait a bit; when the master returns he\xe2\x80\x99ll show you your\nplace. I don\xe2\x80\x99t want your dirty money! A likely thing\xe2\x80\x94just as if we had\nnever seen any! You\xe2\x80\x99ll stink the house out with your beastly tobacco\nand want to put it right with money! Think we\xe2\x80\x99ve never seen a pest! May\nyou be shot in your bowels and your heart!\xe2\x80\x9d shrieked the old woman in a\npiercing voice, interrupting Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems Vany\xc3\xbasha was right!\xe2\x80\x9d thought Ol\xc3\xa9nin. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98A Tartar would be\nnobler\xe2\x80\x99,\xe2\x80\x9d and followed by Granny Ul\xc3\xadtka\xe2\x80\x99s abuse he went out of the hut.\nAs he was leaving, Mary\xc3\xa1nka, still wearing only her pink smock, but\nwith her forehead covered down to her eyes by a white kerchief,\nsuddenly slipped out from the passage past him. Pattering rapidly down\nthe steps with her bare feet she ran from the porch, stopped, and\nlooking round hastily with laughing eyes at the young man, vanished\nround the corner of the hut.\n\nHer firm youthful step, the untamed look of the eyes glistening from\nunder the white kerchief, and the firm stately build of the young\nbeauty, struck Ol\xc3\xa9nin even more powerfully than before. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, it must\nbe _she_,\xe2\x80\x9d he thought, and troubling his head still less about the\nlodgings, he kept looking round at Mary\xc3\xa1nka as he approached Vany\xc3\xbasha.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere you see, the girl too is quite savage, just like a wild filly!\xe2\x80\x9d\nsaid Vany\xc3\xbasha, who though still busy with the luggage wagon had now\ncheered up a bit. \xe2\x80\x9c_La fame!_\xe2\x80\x9d he added in a loud triumphant voice and\nburst out laughing.\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XI\n\n\nTowards evening the master of the house returned from his fishing, and\nhaving learnt that the cadet would pay for the lodging, pacified the\nold woman and satisfied Vany\xc3\xbasha\xe2\x80\x99s demands.\n\nEverything was arranged in the new quarters. Their hosts moved into the\nwinter hut and let their summer hut to the cadet for three rubles a\nmonth. Ol\xc3\xa9nin had something to eat and went to sleep. Towards evening\nhe woke up, washed and made himself tidy, dined, and having lit a\ncigarette sat down by the window that looked onto the street. It was\ncooler. The slanting shadow of the hut with its ornamental gables fell\nacross the dusty road and even bent upwards at the base of the wall of\nthe house opposite. The steep reed-thatched roof of that house shone in\nthe rays of the setting sun. The air grew fresher. Everything was\npeaceful in the village. The soldiers had settled down and become\nquiet. The herds had not yet been driven home and the people had not\nreturned from their work.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin\xe2\x80\x99s lodging was situated almost at the end of the village. At rare\nintervals, from somewhere far beyond the T\xc3\xa9rek in those parts whence\nOl\xc3\xa9nin had just come (the Ch\xc3\xa9chen or the Kum\xc3\xbdtsk plain), came muffled\nsounds of firing. Ol\xc3\xa9nin was feeling very well contented after three\nmonths of bivouac life. His newly washed face was fresh and his\npowerful body clean (an unaccustomed sensation after the campaign) and\nin all his rested limbs he was conscious of a feeling of tranquillity\nand strength. His mind, too, felt fresh and clear. He thought of the\ncampaign and of past dangers. He remembered that he had faced them no\nworse than other men, and that he was accepted as a comrade among\nvaliant Caucasians. His Moscow recollections were left behind Heaven\nknows how far! The old life was wiped out and a quite new life had\nbegun in which there were as yet no mistakes. Here as a new man among\nnew men he could gain a new and good reputation. He was conscious of a\nyouthful and unreasoning joy of life. Looking now out of the window at\nthe boys spinning their tops in the shadow of the house, now round his\nneat new lodging, he thought how pleasantly he would settle down to\nthis new Cossack village life. Now and then he glanced at the mountains\nand the blue sky, and an appreciation of the solemn grandeur of nature\nmingled with his reminiscences and dreams. His new life had begun, not\nas he imagined it would when he left Moscow, but unexpectedly well.\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe mountains, the mountains, the mountains!\xe2\x80\x9d they permeated all his\nthoughts and feelings.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s kissed his dog and licked the jug! ... Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka has kissed\nhis dog!\xe2\x80\x9d suddenly the little Cossacks who had been spinning their tops\nunder the window shouted, looking towards the side street. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s drunk\nhis bitch, and his dagger!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted the boys, crowding together and\nstepping backwards.\n\nThese shouts were addressed to Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka, who with his gun on his\nshoulder and some pheasants hanging at his girdle was returning from\nhis shooting expedition.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have done wrong, lads, I have!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, vigorously swinging his\narms and looking up at the windows on both sides of the street. \xe2\x80\x9cI have\ndrunk the bitch; it was wrong,\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated, evidently vexed but\npretending not to care.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin was surprised by the boys\xe2\x80\x99 behavior towards the old hunter, but\nwas still more struck by the expressive, intelligent face and the\npowerful build of the man whom they called Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere Daddy, here Cossack!\xe2\x80\x9d he called. \xe2\x80\x9cCome here!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe old man looked into the window and stopped.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood evening, good man,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, lifting his little cap off his\ncropped head.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood evening, good man,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Ol\xc3\xa9nin. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it the youngsters\nare shouting at you?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nDaddy Er\xc3\xb3shka came up to the window. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, they\xe2\x80\x99re teasing the old man.\nNo matter, I like it. Let them joke about their old daddy,\xe2\x80\x9d he said\nwith those firm musical intonations with which old and venerable people\nspeak. \xe2\x80\x9cAre you an army commander?\xe2\x80\x9d he added.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I am a cadet. But where did you kill those pheasants?\xe2\x80\x9d asked\nOl\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI dispatched these three hens in the forest,\xe2\x80\x9d answered the old man,\nturning his broad back towards the window to show the hen pheasants\nwhich were hanging with their heads tucked into his belt and staining\nhis coat with blood. \xe2\x80\x9cHaven\xe2\x80\x99t you seen any?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked. \xe2\x80\x9cTake a brace if\nyou like! Here you are,\xe2\x80\x9d and he handed two of the pheasants in at the\nwindow. \xe2\x80\x9cAre you a sportsman yourself?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am. During the campaign I killed four myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cFour? What a lot!\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man sarcastically. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd are you a\ndrinker? Do you drink _chikhir?_\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not? I like a drink.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, I see you are a trump! We shall be _kunaks_, you and I,\xe2\x80\x9d said\nDaddy Er\xc3\xb3shka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cStep in,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ol\xc3\xa9nin. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll have a drop of _chikhir_.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI might as well,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man, \xe2\x80\x9cbut take the pheasants.\xe2\x80\x9d The old\nman\xe2\x80\x99s face showed that he liked the cadet. He had seen at once that he\ncould get free drinks from him, and that therefore it would be all\nright to give him a brace of pheasants.\n\nSoon Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka\xe2\x80\x99s figure appeared in the doorway of the hut, and it\nwas only then that Ol\xc3\xa9nin became fully conscious of the enormous size\nand sturdy build of this man, whose red-brown face with its perfectly\nwhite broad beard was all furrowed by deep lines produced by age and\ntoil. For an old man, the muscles of his legs, arms, and shoulders were\nquite exceptionally large and prominent. There were deep scars on his\nhead under the short-cropped hair. His thick sinewy neck was covered\nwith deep intersecting folds like a bull\xe2\x80\x99s. His horny hands were\nbruised and scratched. He stepped lightly and easily over the\nthreshold, unslung his gun and placed it in a corner, and casting a\nrapid glance round the room noted the value of the goods and chattels\ndeposited in the hut, and with out-turned toes stepped softly, in his\nsandals of raw hide, into the middle of the room. He brought with him a\npenetrating but not unpleasant smell of _chikhir_ wine, vodka,\ngunpowder, and congealed blood.\n\nDaddy Er\xc3\xb3shka bowed down before the icons, smoothed his beard, and\napproaching Ol\xc3\xa9nin held out his thick brown hand. \xe2\x80\x9c_Koshkildy_,\xe2\x80\x9d said\nhe; \xe2\x80\x9cThat is Tartar for \xe2\x80\x98Good-day\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98Peace be unto you,\xe2\x80\x99 it means in\ntheir tongue.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Koshkildy_, I know,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Ol\xc3\xa9nin, shaking hands.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh, but you don\xe2\x80\x99t, you won\xe2\x80\x99t know the right order! Fool!\xe2\x80\x9d said Daddy\nEr\xc3\xb3shka, shaking his head reproachfully. \xe2\x80\x9cIf anyone says \xe2\x80\x98_Koshkildy_\xe2\x80\x99\nto you, you must say \xe2\x80\x98_Allah rasi bo sun_,\xe2\x80\x99 that is, \xe2\x80\x98God save you.\xe2\x80\x99\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s the way, my dear fellow, and not \xe2\x80\x98_Koshkildy_.\xe2\x80\x99 But I\xe2\x80\x99ll teach\nyou all about it. We had a fellow here, Elias M\xc3\xb3sevich, one of your\nRussians, he and I were _kunaks_. He was a trump, a drunkard, a thief,\na sportsman\xe2\x80\x94and what a sportsman! I taught him everything.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what will you teach me?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ol\xc3\xa9nin, who was becoming more and\nmore interested in the old man.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll take you hunting and teach you to fish. I\xe2\x80\x99ll show you Ch\xc3\xa9chens\nand find a girl for you, if you like\xe2\x80\x94even that! That\xe2\x80\x99s the sort I am!\nI\xe2\x80\x99m a wag!\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94and the old man laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll sit down. I\xe2\x80\x99m tired.\n_Karga?_\xe2\x80\x9d he added inquiringly.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what does \xe2\x80\x98_Karga_\xe2\x80\x99 mean?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, that means \xe2\x80\x98All right\xe2\x80\x99 in Georgian. But I say it just so. It is a\nway I have, it\xe2\x80\x99s my favourite word. _Karga_, _Karga_. I say it just so;\nin fun I mean. Well, lad, won\xe2\x80\x99t you order the _chikhir?_ You\xe2\x80\x99ve got an\norderly, haven\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHey, Iv\xc3\xa1n!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted the old man. \xe2\x80\x9cAll your soldiers are Iv\xc3\xa1ns. Is\nyours Iv\xc3\xa1n?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue enough, his name is Iv\xc3\xa1n\xe2\x80\x94Vany\xc3\xbasha. Here Vany\xc3\xbasha! Please get some\n_chikhir_ from our landlady and bring it here.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIv\xc3\xa1n or Vany\xc3\xbasha, that\xe2\x80\x99s all one. Why are all your soldiers Iv\xc3\xa1ns?\nIv\xc3\xa1n, old fellow,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man, \xe2\x80\x9cyou tell them to give you some\nfrom the barrel they have begun. They have the best _chikhir_ in the\nvillage. But don\xe2\x80\x99t give more than thirty kopeks for the quart, mind,\nbecause that witch would be only too glad.... Our people are anathema\npeople; stupid people,\xe2\x80\x9d Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka continued in a confidential tone\nafter Vany\xc3\xbasha had gone out. \xe2\x80\x9cThey do not look upon you as on men, you\nare worse than a Tartar in their eyes. \xe2\x80\x98Worldly Russians\xe2\x80\x99 they say. But\nas for me, though you are a soldier you are still a man, and have a\nsoul in you. Isn\xe2\x80\x99t that right? Elias M\xc3\xb3sevich was a soldier, yet what a\ntreasure of a man he was! Isn\xe2\x80\x99t that so, my dear fellow? That\xe2\x80\x99s why our\npeople don\xe2\x80\x99t like me; but I don\xe2\x80\x99t care! I\xe2\x80\x99m a merry fellow, and I like\neverybody. I\xe2\x80\x99m Er\xc3\xb3shka; yes, my dear fellow.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAnd the old Cossack patted the young man affectionately on the\nshoulder.\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XII\n\n\nVany\xc3\xbasha, who meanwhile had finished his housekeeping arrangements and\nhad even been shaved by the company\xe2\x80\x99s barber and had pulled his\ntrousers out of his high boots as a sign that the company was stationed\nin comfortable quarters, was in excellent spirits. He looked\nattentively but not benevolently at Er\xc3\xb3shka, as at a wild beast he had\nnever seen before, shook his head at the floor which the old man had\ndirtied and, having taken two bottles from under a bench, went to the\nlandlady.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood evening, kind people,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, having made up his mind to be\nvery gentle. \xe2\x80\x9cMy master has sent me to get some _chikhir_. Will you\ndraw some for me, good folk?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe old woman gave no answer. The girl, who was arranging the kerchief\non her head before a little Tartar mirror, looked round at Vany\xc3\xbasha in\nsilence.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll pay money for it, honoured people,\xe2\x80\x9d said Vany\xc3\xbasha, jingling the\ncoppers in his pocket. \xe2\x80\x9cBe kind to us and we, too will be kind to you,\xe2\x80\x9d\nhe added.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow much?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the old woman abruptly. \xe2\x80\x9cA quart.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo, my own, draw some for them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Granny Ul\xc3\xadtka to her daughter.\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake it from the cask that\xe2\x80\x99s begun, my precious.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe girl took the keys and a decanter and went out of the hut with\nVany\xc3\xbasha.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell me, who is that young woman?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ol\xc3\xa9nin, pointing to Mary\xc3\xa1nka,\nwho was passing the window. The old man winked and nudged the young man\nwith his elbow.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait a bit,\xe2\x80\x9d said he and reached out of the window. \xe2\x80\x9cKhm,\xe2\x80\x9d he coughed,\nand bellowed, \xe2\x80\x9cMary\xc3\xa1nka dear. Hallo, Mary\xc3\xa1nka, my girlie, won\xe2\x80\x99t you\nlove me, darling? I\xe2\x80\x99m a wag,\xe2\x80\x9d he added in a whisper to Ol\xc3\xa9nin. The\ngirl, not turning her head and swinging her arms regularly and\nvigorously, passed the window with the peculiarly smart and bold gait\nof a Cossack woman and only turned her dark shaded eyes slowly towards\nthe old man.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cLove me and you\xe2\x80\x99ll be happy,\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Er\xc3\xb3shka, winking, and he looked\nquestioningly at the cadet.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m a fine fellow, I\xe2\x80\x99m a wag!\xe2\x80\x9d he added. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s a regular queen, that\ngirl. Eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is lovely,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ol\xc3\xa9nin. \xe2\x80\x9cCall her here!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man. \xe2\x80\x9cFor that one a match is being arranged\nwith Luk\xc3\xa1shka, Luke, a fine Cossack, a brave, who killed an _abrek_ the\nother day. I\xe2\x80\x99ll find you a better one. I\xe2\x80\x99ll find you one that will be\nall dressed up in silk and silver. Once I\xe2\x80\x99ve said it I\xe2\x80\x99ll do it. I\xe2\x80\x99ll\nget you a regular beauty!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou, an old man\xe2\x80\x94and say such things,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Ol\xc3\xa9nin. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it\xe2\x80\x99s a\nsin!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cA sin? Where\xe2\x80\x99s the sin?\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man emphatically. \xe2\x80\x9cA sin to look\nat a nice girl? A sin to have some fun with her? Or is it a sin to love\nher? Is that so in your parts? ... No, my dear fellow, it\xe2\x80\x99s not a sin,\nit\xe2\x80\x99s salvation! God made you and God made the girl too. He made it all;\nso it is no sin to look at a nice girl. That\xe2\x80\x99s what she was made for;\nto be loved and to give joy. That\xe2\x80\x99s how I judge it, my good fellow.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHaving crossed the yard and entered a cool dark storeroom filled with\nbarrels, Mary\xc3\xa1nka went up to one of them and repeating the usual prayer\nplunged a dipper into it. Vany\xc3\xbasha standing in the doorway smiled as he\nlooked at her. He thought it very funny that she had only a smock on,\nclose-fitting behind and tucked up in front, and still funnier that she\nwore a necklace of silver coins. He thought this quite un-Russian and\nthat they would all laugh in the serfs\xe2\x80\x99 quarters at home if they saw a\ngirl like that. \xe2\x80\x9c_La fille comme c\xe2\x80\x99est tres bien_, for a change,\xe2\x80\x9d he\nthought. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell that to my master.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you standing in the light for, you devil!\xe2\x80\x9d the girl suddenly\nshouted. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy don\xe2\x80\x99t you pass me the decanter!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHaving filled the decanter with cool red wine, Mary\xc3\xa1nka handed it to\nVany\xc3\xbasha.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive the money to Mother,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, pushing away the hand in which he\nheld the money.\n\nVany\xc3\xbasha laughed.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy are you so cross, little dear?\xe2\x80\x9d he said good-naturedly,\nirresolutely shuffling with his feet while the girl was covering the\nbarrel.\n\nShe began to laugh.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you! Are you kind?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe, my master and I, are very kind,\xe2\x80\x9d Vany\xc3\xbasha answered decidedly. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\nare so kind that wherever we have stayed our hosts were always very\ngrateful. It\xe2\x80\x99s because he\xe2\x80\x99s generous.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe girl stood listening.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd is your master married?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. The master is young and unmarried, because noble gentlemen can\nnever marry young,\xe2\x80\x9d said Vany\xc3\xbasha didactically.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cA likely thing! See what a fed-up buffalo he is\xe2\x80\x94and too young to\nmarry! Is he the chief of you all?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy master is a cadet; that means he\xe2\x80\x99s not yet an officer, but he\xe2\x80\x99s\nmore important than a general\xe2\x80\x94he\xe2\x80\x99s an important man! Because not only\nour colonel, but the Tsar himself, knows him,\xe2\x80\x9d proudly explained\nVany\xc3\xbasha. \xe2\x80\x9cWe are not like those other beggars in the line regiment,\nand our papa himself was a Senator. He had more than a thousand serfs,\nall his own, and they send us a thousand rubles at a time. That\xe2\x80\x99s why\neveryone likes us. Another may be a captain but have no money. What\xe2\x80\x99s\nthe use of that?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo away. I\xe2\x80\x99ll lock up,\xe2\x80\x9d said the girl, interrupting him.\n\nVany\xc3\xbasha brought Ol\xc3\xa9nin the wine and announced that \xe2\x80\x9c_La fille c\xe2\x80\x99est\ntres joulie_,\xe2\x80\x9d and, laughing stupidly, at once went out.\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XIII\n\n\nMeanwhile the tattoo had sounded in the village square. The people had\nreturned from their work. The herd lowed as in clouds of golden dust it\ncrowded at the village gate. The girls and the women hurried through\nthe streets and yards, turning in their cattle. The sun had quite\nhidden itself behind the distant snowy peaks. One pale bluish shadow\nspread over land and sky. Above the darkened gardens stars just\ndiscernible were kindling, and the sounds were gradually hushed in the\nvillage. The cattle having been attended to and left for the night, the\nwomen came out and gathered at the corners of the streets and, cracking\nsunflower seeds with their teeth, settled down on the earthen\nembankments of the houses. Later on Mary\xc3\xa1nka, having finished milking\nthe buffalo and the other two cows, also joined one of these groups.\n\nThe group consisted of several women and girls and one old Cossack man.\n\nThey were talking about the _abrek_ who had been killed.\n\nThe Cossack was narrating and the women questioning him.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI expect he\xe2\x80\x99ll get a handsome reward,\xe2\x80\x9d said one of the women.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course. It\xe2\x80\x99s said that they\xe2\x80\x99ll send him a cross.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cM\xc3\xb3sev did try to wrong him. Took the gun away from him, but the\nauthorities at Kizly\xc3\xa1r heard of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cA mean creature that M\xc3\xb3sev is!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey say Luk\xc3\xa1shka has come home,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked one of the girls.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe and Naz\xc3\xa1rka are merry-making at Y\xc3\xa1mka\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d (Y\xc3\xa1mka was an unmarried,\ndisreputable Cossack woman who kept an illicit pot-house.) \xe2\x80\x9cI heard say\nthey had drunk half a pailful.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat luck that Snatcher has,\xe2\x80\x9d somebody remarked. \xe2\x80\x9cA real snatcher. But\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s no denying he\xe2\x80\x99s a fine lad, smart enough for anything, a\nright-minded lad! His father was just such another. Daddy Kiry\xc3\xa1k was:\nhe takes after his father. When he was killed the whole village howled.\nLook, there they are,\xe2\x80\x9d added the speaker, pointing to the Cossacks who\nwere coming down the street towards them.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd Ergush\xc3\xb3v has managed to come along with them too! The drunkard!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka, Naz\xc3\xa1rka, and Ergush\xc3\xb3v, having emptied half a pail of vodka,\nwere coming towards the girls. The faces of all three, but especially\nthat of the old Cossack, were redder than usual. Ergush\xc3\xb3v was reeling\nand kept laughing and nudging Naz\xc3\xa1rka in the ribs.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy are you not singing?\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted to the girls. \xe2\x80\x9cSing to our\nmerry-making, I tell you!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThey were welcomed with the words, \xe2\x80\x9cHad a good day? Had a good day?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy sing? It\xe2\x80\x99s not a holiday,\xe2\x80\x9d said one of the women. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re tight,\nso you go and sing.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nErgush\xc3\xb3v roared with laughter and nudged Naz\xc3\xa1rka. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99d better sing.\nAnd I\xe2\x80\x99ll begin too. I\xe2\x80\x99m clever, I tell you.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you asleep, fair ones?\xe2\x80\x9d said Naz\xc3\xa1rka. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve come from the cordon\nto drink your health. We\xe2\x80\x99ve already drunk Luk\xc3\xa1shka\xe2\x80\x99s health.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka, when he reached the group, slowly raised his cap and stopped\nin front of the girls. His broad cheekbones and neck were red. He stood\nand spoke softly and sedately, but in his tranquillity and sedateness\nthere was more of animation and strength than in all Naz\xc3\xa1rka\xe2\x80\x99s\nloquacity and bustle. He reminded one of a playful colt that with a\nsnort and a flourish of its tail suddenly stops short and stands as\nthough nailed to the ground with all four feet. Luk\xc3\xa1shka stood quietly\nin front of the girls, his eyes laughed, and he spoke but little as he\nglanced now at his drunken companions and now at the girls. When\nMary\xc3\xa1nka joined the group he raised his cap with a firm deliberate\nmovement, moved out of her way and then stepped in front of her with\none foot a little forward and with his thumbs in his belt, fingering\nhis dagger. Mary\xc3\xa1nka answered his greeting with a leisurely bow of her\nhead, settled down on the earth-bank, and took some seeds out of the\nbosom of her smock. Luk\xc3\xa1shka, keeping his eyes fixed on Mary\xc3\xa1nka,\nslowly cracked seeds and spat out the shells. All were quiet when\nMary\xc3\xa1nka joined the group.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you come for long?\xe2\x80\x9d asked a woman, breaking the silence.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cTill tomorrow morning,\xe2\x80\x9d quietly replied Luk\xc3\xa1shka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, God grant you get something good,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Cossack; \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m glad\nof it, as I\xe2\x80\x99ve just been saying.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I say so too,\xe2\x80\x9d put in the tipsy Ergush\xc3\xb3v, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat a lot of\nvisitors have come,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, pointing to a soldier who was passing\nby. \xe2\x80\x9cThe soldiers\xe2\x80\x99 vodka is good\xe2\x80\x94I like it.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99ve sent three of the devils to us,\xe2\x80\x9d said one of the women.\n\xe2\x80\x9cGrandad went to the village Elders, but they say nothing can be done.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, ha! Have you met with trouble?\xe2\x80\x9d said Ergush\xc3\xb3v.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI expect they have smoked you out with their tobacco?\xe2\x80\x9d asked another\nwoman. \xe2\x80\x9cSmoke as much as you like in the yard, I say, but we won\xe2\x80\x99t\nallow it inside the hut. Not if the Elder himself comes, I won\xe2\x80\x99t allow\nit. Besides, they may rob you. He\xe2\x80\x99s not quartered any of them on\nhimself, no fear, that devil\xe2\x80\x99s son of an Elder.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t like it?\xe2\x80\x9d Ergush\xc3\xb3v began again.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I\xe2\x80\x99ve also heard say that the girls will have to make the soldiers\xe2\x80\x99\nbeds and offer them _chikhir_ and honey,\xe2\x80\x9d said Naz\xc3\xa1rka, putting one\nfoot forward and tilting his cap like Luk\xc3\xa1shka.\n\nErgush\xc3\xb3v burst into a roar of laughter, and seizing the girl nearest to\nhim, he embraced her. \xe2\x80\x9cI tell you true.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow then, you black pitch!\xe2\x80\x9d squealed the girl, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell your old\nwoman.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell her,\xe2\x80\x9d shouted he. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s quite right what Naz\xc3\xa1rka says; a\ncircular has been sent round. He can read, you know. Quite true!\xe2\x80\x9d And\nhe began embracing the next girl.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you up to, you beast?\xe2\x80\x9d squealed the rosy, round-faced\n\xc3\x9astenka, laughing and lifting her arm to hit him.\n\nThe Cossack stepped aside and nearly fell.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere, they say girls have no strength, and you nearly killed me.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGet away, you black pitch, what devil has brought you from the\ncordon?\xe2\x80\x9d said \xc3\x9astenka, and turning away from him she again burst out\nlaughing. \xe2\x80\x9cYou were asleep and missed the _abrek_, didn\xe2\x80\x99t you? Suppose\nhe had done for you it would have been all the better.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99d have howled, I expect,\xe2\x80\x9d said Naz\xc3\xa1rka, laughing.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHowled! A likely thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust look, she doesn\xe2\x80\x99t care. She\xe2\x80\x99d howl, Naz\xc3\xa1rka, eh? Would she?\xe2\x80\x9d said\nErgush\xc3\xb3v.\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka all this time had stood silently looking at Mary\xc3\xa1nka. His gaze\nevidently confused the girl.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Mary\xc3\xa1nka! I hear they\xe2\x80\x99ve quartered one of the chiefs on you?\xe2\x80\x9d he\nsaid, drawing nearer.\n\nMary\xc3\xa1nka, as was her wont, waited before she replied, and slowly\nraising her eyes looked at the Cossack. Luk\xc3\xa1shka\xe2\x80\x99s eyes were laughing\nas if something special, apart from what was said, was taking place\nbetween himself and the girl.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it\xe2\x80\x99s all right for them as they have two huts,\xe2\x80\x9d replied an old\nwoman on Mary\xc3\xa1nka\xe2\x80\x99s behalf, \xe2\x80\x9cbut at F\xc3\xb3mushkin\xe2\x80\x99s now they also have one\nof the chiefs quartered on them and they say one whole corner is packed\nfull with his things, and the family have no room left. Was such a\nthing ever heard of as that they should turn a whole horde loose in the\nvillage?\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd what the plague are they going to do here?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve heard say they\xe2\x80\x99ll build a bridge across the T\xc3\xa9rek,\xe2\x80\x9d said one of\nthe girls.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I\xe2\x80\x99ve been told that they will dig a pit to put the girls in\nbecause they don\xe2\x80\x99t love the lads,\xe2\x80\x9d said Naz\xc3\xa1rka, approaching \xc3\x9astenka;\nand he again made a whimsical gesture which set everybody laughing, and\nErgush\xc3\xb3v, passing by Mary\xc3\xa1nka, who was next in turn, began to embrace\nan old woman.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy don\xe2\x80\x99t you hug Mary\xc3\xa1nka? You should do it to each in turn,\xe2\x80\x9d said\nNaz\xc3\xa1rka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, my old one is sweeter,\xe2\x80\x9d shouted the Cossack, kissing the\nstruggling old woman.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll throttle me,\xe2\x80\x9d she screamed, laughing.\n\nThe tramp of regular footsteps at the other end of the street\ninterrupted their laughter. Three soldiers in their cloaks, with their\nmuskets on their shoulders, were marching in step to relieve guard by\nthe ammunition wagon.\n\nThe corporal, an old cavalry man, looked angrily at the Cossacks and\nled his men straight along the road where Luk\xc3\xa1shka and Naz\xc3\xa1rka were\nstanding, so that they should have to get out of the way. Naz\xc3\xa1rka\nmoved, but Luk\xc3\xa1shka only screwed up his eyes and turned his broad back\nwithout moving from his place.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cPeople are standing here, so you go round,\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered, half turning\nhis head and tossing it contemptuously in the direction of the\nsoldiers.\n\nThe soldiers passed by in silence, keeping step regularly along the\ndusty road.\n\nMary\xc3\xa1nka began laughing and all the other girls chimed in.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat swells!\xe2\x80\x9d said Naz\xc3\xa1rka, \xe2\x80\x9cJust like long-skirted choristers,\xe2\x80\x9d and\nhe walked a few steps down the road imitating the soldiers.\n\nAgain everyone broke into peals of laughter.\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka came slowly up to Mary\xc3\xa1nka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd where have you put up the chief?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\n\nMary\xc3\xa1nka thought for a moment.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve let him have the new hut,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd is he old or young,\xe2\x80\x9d asked Luk\xc3\xa1shka, sitting down beside her.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think I\xe2\x80\x99ve asked?\xe2\x80\x9d answered the girl. \xe2\x80\x9cI went to get him some\n_chikhir_ and saw him sitting at the window with Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka.\nRed-headed he seemed. They\xe2\x80\x99ve brought a whole cartload of things.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAnd she dropped her eyes.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, how glad I am that I got leave from the cordon!\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka,\nmoving closer to the girl and looking straight in her eyes all the\ntime.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd have you come for long?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mary\xc3\xa1nka, smiling slightly.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cTill the morning. Give me some sunflower seeds,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, holding out\nhis hand.\n\nMary\xc3\xa1nka now smiled outright and unfastened the neckband of her smock.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t take them all,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally I felt so dull all the time without you, I swear I did,\xe2\x80\x9d he\nsaid in a calm, restrained whisper, helping himself to some seeds out\nof the bosom of the girl\xe2\x80\x99s smock, and stooping still closer over her he\ncontinued with laughing eyes to talk to her in low tones.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t come, I tell you,\xe2\x80\x9d Mary\xc3\xa1nka suddenly said aloud, leaning away\nfrom him.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo really ... what I wanted to say to you, ...\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Luk\xc3\xa1shka.\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy the Heavens! Do come!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nMary\xc3\xa1nka shook her head, but did so with a smile.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNursey Mary\xc3\xa1nka! Hallo Nursey! Mammy is calling! Supper time!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted\nMary\xc3\xa1nka\xe2\x80\x99s little brother, running towards the group.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m coming,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the girl. \xe2\x80\x9cGo, my dear, go alone\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll come in a\nminute.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka rose and raised his cap.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI expect I had better go home too, that will be best,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, trying\nto appear unconcerned but hardly able to repress a smile, and he\ndisappeared behind the corner of the house.\n\nMeanwhile night had entirely enveloped the village. Bright stars were\nscattered over the dark sky. The streets became dark and empty. Naz\xc3\xa1rka\nremained with the women on the earth-bank and their laughter was still\nheard, but Luk\xc3\xa1shka, having slowly moved away from the girls, crouched\ndown like a cat and then suddenly started running lightly, holding his\ndagger to steady it: not homeward, however, but towards the cornet\xe2\x80\x99s\nhouse. Having passed two streets he turned into a lane and lifting the\nskirt of his coat sat down on the ground in the shadow of a fence. \xe2\x80\x9cA\nregular cornet\xe2\x80\x99s daughter!\xe2\x80\x9d he thought about Mary\xc3\xa1nka. \xe2\x80\x9cWon\xe2\x80\x99t even have\na lark\xe2\x80\x94the devil! But just wait a bit.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe approaching footsteps of a woman attracted his attention. He began\nlistening, and laughed all by himself. Mary\xc3\xa1nka with bowed head,\nstriking the pales of the fences with a switch, was walking with rapid\nregular strides straight towards him. Luk\xc3\xa1shka rose. Mary\xc3\xa1nka started\nand stopped.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat an accursed devil! You frightened me! So you have not gone home?\xe2\x80\x9d\nshe said, and laughed aloud.\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka put one arm round her and with the other hand raised her face.\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat I wanted to tell you, by Heaven!\xe2\x80\x9d his voice trembled and broke.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you talking of, at night time!\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mary\xc3\xa1nka. \xe2\x80\x9cMother is\nwaiting for me, and you\xe2\x80\x99d better go to your sweetheart.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAnd freeing herself from his arms she ran away a few steps. When she\nhad reached the wattle fence of her home she stopped and turned to the\nCossack who was running beside her and still trying to persuade her to\nstay a while with him.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what do you want to say, midnight-gadabout?\xe2\x80\x9d and she again began\nlaughing.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t laugh at me, Mary\xc3\xa1nka! By the Heaven! Well, what if I have a\nsweetheart? May the devil take her! Only say the word and now I\xe2\x80\x99ll love\nyou\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll do anything you wish. Here they are!\xe2\x80\x9d and he jingled the money\nin his pocket. \xe2\x80\x9cNow we can live splendidly. Others have pleasures, and\nI? I get no pleasure from you, Mary\xc3\xa1nka dear!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe girl did not answer. She stood before him breaking her switch into\nlittle bits with a rapid movement of her fingers.\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka suddenly clenched his teeth and fists.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd why keep waiting and waiting? Don\xe2\x80\x99t I love you, darling? You can\ndo what you like with me,\xe2\x80\x9d said he suddenly, frowning angrily and\nseizing both her hands.\n\nThe calm expression of Mary\xc3\xa1nka\xe2\x80\x99s face and voice did not change.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t bluster, Luk\xc3\xa1shka, but listen to me,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered, not pulling\naway her hands but holding the Cossack at arm\xe2\x80\x99s length. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s true I am\na girl, but you listen to me! It does not depend on me, but if you love\nme I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you this. Let go my hands, I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you without.\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll\nmarry you, but you\xe2\x80\x99ll never get any nonsense from me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary\xc3\xa1nka\nwithout turning her face.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat, you\xe2\x80\x99ll marry me? Marriage does not depend on us. Love me\nyourself, Mary\xc3\xa1nka dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka, from sullen and furious\nbecoming again gentle, submissive, and tender, and smiling as he looked\nclosely into her eyes.\n\nMary\xc3\xa1nka clung to him and kissed him firmly on the lips.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBrother dear!\xe2\x80\x9d she whispered, pressing him convulsively to her. Then,\nsuddenly tearing herself away, she ran into the gate of her house\nwithout looking round.\n\nIn spite of the Cossack\xe2\x80\x99s entreaties to wait another minute to hear\nwhat he had to say, Mary\xc3\xa1nka did not stop.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo,\xe2\x80\x9d she cried, \xe2\x80\x9cyou\xe2\x80\x99ll be seen! I do believe that devil, our lodger,\nis walking about the yard.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cCornet\xe2\x80\x99s daughter,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Luk\xc3\xa1shka. \xe2\x80\x9cShe will marry me.
