Marriage is\nall very well, but you just love me!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe found Naz\xc3\xa1rka at Y\xc3\xa1mka\xe2\x80\x99s house, and after having a spree with him\nwent to Dun\xc3\xa1yka\xe2\x80\x99s house, where, in spite of her not being faithful to\nhim, he spent the night.\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XIV\n\n\nIt was quite true that Ol\xc3\xa9nin had been walking about the yard when\nMary\xc3\xa1nka entered the gate, and had heard her say, \xe2\x80\x9cThat devil, our\nlodger, is walking about.\xe2\x80\x9d He had spent that evening with Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka\nin the porch of his new lodging. He had had a table, a samovar, wine,\nand a candle brought out, and over a cup of tea and a cigar he listened\nto the tales the old man told seated on the threshold at his feet.\nThough the air was still, the candle dripped and flickered: now\nlighting up the post of the porch, now the table and crockery, now the\ncropped white head of the old man. Moths circled round the flame and,\nshedding the dust of their wings, fluttered on the table and in the\nglasses, flew into the candle flame, and disappeared in the black space\nbeyond. Ol\xc3\xa9nin and Er\xc3\xb3shka had emptied five bottles of _chikhir_.\nEr\xc3\xb3shka filled the glasses every time, offering one to Ol\xc3\xa9nin, drinking\nhis health, and talking untiringly. He told of Cossack life in the old\ndays: of his father, \xe2\x80\x9cThe Broad\xe2\x80\x9d, who alone had carried on his back a\nboar\xe2\x80\x99s carcass weighing three hundredweight, and drank two pails of\n_chikhir_ at one sitting. He told of his own days and his chum G\xc3\xadrchik,\nwith whom during the plague he used to smuggle felt cloaks across the\nT\xc3\xa9rek. He told how one morning he had killed two deer, and about his\n\xe2\x80\x9clittle soul\xe2\x80\x9d who used to run to him at the cordon at night. He told\nall this so eloquently and picturesquely that Ol\xc3\xa9nin did not notice how\ntime passed. \xe2\x80\x9cAh yes, my dear fellow, you did not know me in my golden\ndays; then I\xe2\x80\x99d have shown you things. Today it\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x98Er\xc3\xb3shka licks the\njug\xe2\x80\x99, but then Er\xc3\xb3shka was famous in the whole regiment. Whose was the\nfinest horse? Who had a Gurda sword? To whom should one go to get a\ndrink? With whom go on the spree? Who should be sent to the mountains\nto kill Ahmet Khan? Why, always Er\xc3\xb3shka! Whom did the girls love?\nAlways Er\xc3\xb3shka had to answer for it. Because I was a real brave: a\ndrinker, a thief (I used to seize herds of horses in the mountains), a\nsinger; I was a master of every art! There are no Cossacks like that\nnowadays. It\xe2\x80\x99s disgusting to look at them. When they\xe2\x80\x99re that high\n(Er\xc3\xb3shka held his hand three feet from the ground) they put on idiotic\nboots and keep looking at them\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s all the pleasure they know. Or\nthey\xe2\x80\x99ll drink themselves foolish, not like men but all wrong. And who\nwas I? I was Er\xc3\xb3shka, the thief; they knew me not only in this village\nbut up in the mountains. Tartar princes, my _kunaks_, used to come to\nsee me! I used to be everybody\xe2\x80\x99s _kunak_. If he was a Tartar\xe2\x80\x94with a\nTartar; an Armenian\xe2\x80\x94with an Armenian; a soldier\xe2\x80\x94with a soldier; an\nofficer\xe2\x80\x94with an officer! I didn\xe2\x80\x99t care as long as he was a drinker. He\nsays you should cleanse yourself from intercourse with the world, not\ndrink with soldiers, not eat with a Tartar.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho says all that?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, our teacher! But listen to a Mullah or a Tartar Cadi. He says,\n\xe2\x80\x98You unbelieving Giaours, why do you eat pig?\xe2\x80\x99 That shows that everyone\nhas his own law. But I think it\xe2\x80\x99s all one. God has made everything for\nthe joy of man. There is no sin in any of it. Take example from an\nanimal. It lives in the Tartar\xe2\x80\x99s reeds or in ours. Wherever it happens\nto go, there is its home! Whatever God gives it, that it eats! But our\npeople say we have to lick red-hot plates in hell for that. And I think\nit\xe2\x80\x99s all a fraud,\xe2\x80\x9d he added after a pause.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is a fraud?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, what the preachers say. We had an army captain in Chervl\xc3\xabna who\nwas my _kunak:_ a fine fellow just like me. He was killed in Ch\xc3\xa9chnya.\nWell, he used to say that the preachers invent all that out of their\nown heads. \xe2\x80\x98When you die the grass will grow on your grave and that\xe2\x80\x99s\nall!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d The old man laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cHe was a desperate fellow.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how old are you?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Lord only knows! I must be about seventy. When a Tsaritsa reigned\nin Russia I was no longer very small. So you can reckon it out. I must\nbe seventy.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes you must, but you are still a fine fellow.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, thank Heaven I am healthy, quite healthy, except that a woman, a\nwitch, has harmed me....\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, just harmed me.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd so when you die the grass will grow?\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\nEr\xc3\xb3shka evidently did not wish to express his thought clearly. He was\nsilent for a while.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what did you think? Drink!\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted suddenly, smiling and\nhanding Ol\xc3\xa9nin some wine.\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XV\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what was I saying?\xe2\x80\x9d he continued, trying to remember. \xe2\x80\x9cYes,\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s the sort of man I am. I am a hunter. There is no hunter to equal\nme in the whole army. I will find and show you any animal and any bird,\nand what and where. I know it all! I have dogs, and two guns, and nets,\nand a screen and a hawk. I have everything, thank the Lord! If you are\nnot bragging but are a real sportsman, I\xe2\x80\x99ll show you everything. Do you\nknow what a man I am? When I have found a track\xe2\x80\x94I know the animal. I\nknow where he will lie down and where he\xe2\x80\x99ll drink or wallow. I make\nmyself a perch and sit there all night watching. What\xe2\x80\x99s the good of\nstaying at home? One only gets into mischief, gets drunk. And here\nwomen come and chatter, and boys shout at me\xe2\x80\x94enough to drive one mad\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a different matter when you go out at nightfall, choose yourself\na place, press down the reeds and sit there and stay waiting, like a\njolly fellow. One knows everything that goes on in the woods. One looks\nup at the sky: the stars move, you look at them and find out from them\nhow the time goes. One looks round\xe2\x80\x94the wood is rustling; one goes on\nwaiting, now there comes a crackling\xe2\x80\x94a boar comes to rub himself; one\nlistens to hear the young eaglets screech and then the cocks give voice\nin the village, or the geese. When you hear the geese you know it is\nnot yet midnight. And I know all about it! Or when a gun is fired\nsomewhere far away, thoughts come to me. One thinks, who is that\nfiring? Is it another Cossack like myself who has been watching for\nsome animal? And has he killed it? Or only wounded it so that now the\npoor thing goes through the reeds smearing them with its blood all for\nnothing? I don\xe2\x80\x99t like that! Oh, how I dislike it! Why injure a beast?\nYou fool, you fool! Or one thinks, \xe2\x80\x98Maybe an _abrek_ has killed some\nsilly little Cossack.\xe2\x80\x99 All this passes through one\xe2\x80\x99s mind. And once as\nI sat watching by the river I saw a cradle floating down. It was sound\nexcept for one corner which was broken off. Thoughts did come that\ntime! I thought some of your soldiers, the devils, must have got into a\nTartar village and seized the Ch\xc3\xa9chen women, and one of the devils has\nkilled the little one: taken it by its legs, and hit its head against a\nwall. Don\xe2\x80\x99t they do such things? Sh! Men have no souls! And thoughts\ncame to me that filled me with pity. I thought: they\xe2\x80\x99ve thrown away the\ncradle and driven the wife out, and her brave has taken his gun and\ncome across to our side to rob us. One watches and thinks. And when one\nhears a litter breaking through the thicket, something begins to knock\ninside one. Dear one, come this way! \xe2\x80\x98They\xe2\x80\x99ll scent me,\xe2\x80\x99 one thinks;\nand one sits and does not stir while one\xe2\x80\x99s heart goes dun! dun! dun!\nand simply lifts you. Once this spring a fine litter came near me, I\nsaw something black. \xe2\x80\x98In the name of the Father and of the Son,\xe2\x80\x99 and I\nwas just about to fire when she grunts to her pigs: \xe2\x80\x98Danger, children,\xe2\x80\x99\nshe says, \xe2\x80\x98there\xe2\x80\x99s a man here,\xe2\x80\x99 and off they all ran, breaking through\nthe bushes. And she had been so close I could almost have bitten her.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow could a sow tell her brood that a man was there?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you think? You think the beast\xe2\x80\x99s a fool? No, he is wiser than\na man though you do call him a pig! He knows everything. Take this for\ninstance. A man will pass along your track and not notice it; but a pig\nas soon as it gets onto your track turns and runs at once: that shows\nthere is wisdom in him, since he scents your smell and you don\xe2\x80\x99t. And\nthere is this to be said too: you wish to kill it and it wishes to go\nabout the woods alive. You have one law and it has another. It is a\npig, but it is no worse than you\xe2\x80\x94it too is God\xe2\x80\x99s creature. Ah, dear!\nMan is foolish, foolish, foolish!\xe2\x80\x9d The old man repeated this several\ntimes and then, letting his head drop, he sat thinking.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin also became thoughtful, and descending from the porch with his\nhands behind his back began pacing up and down the yard.\n\nEr\xc3\xb3shka, rousing himself, raised his head and began gazing intently at\nthe moths circling round the flickering flame of the candle and burning\nthemselves in it.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cFool, fool!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere are you flying to? Fool, fool!\xe2\x80\x9d He rose\nand with his thick fingers began to drive away the moths.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll burn, little fool! Fly this way, there\xe2\x80\x99s plenty of room.\xe2\x80\x9d He\nspoke tenderly, trying to catch them delicately by their wings with his\nthick fingers and then letting them fly again. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are killing\nyourself and I am sorry for you!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe sat a long time chattering and sipping out of the bottle. Ol\xc3\xa9nin\npaced up and down the yard. Suddenly he was struck by the sound of\nwhispering outside the gate. Involuntarily holding his breath, he heard\na woman\xe2\x80\x99s laughter, a man\xe2\x80\x99s voice, and the sound of a kiss.\nIntentionally rustling the grass under his feet he crossed to the\nopposite side of the yard, but after a while the wattle fence creaked.\nA Cossack in a dark Circassian coat and a white sheepskin cap passed\nalong the other side of the fence (it was Luke), and a tall woman with\na white kerchief on her head went past Ol\xc3\xa9nin. \xe2\x80\x9cYou and I have nothing\nto do with one another\xe2\x80\x9d was what Mary\xc3\xa1nka\xe2\x80\x99s firm step gave him to\nunderstand. He followed her with his eyes to the porch of the hut, and\nhe even saw her through the window take off her kerchief and sit down.\nAnd suddenly a feeling of lonely depression and some vague longings and\nhopes, and envy of someone or other, overcame the young man\xe2\x80\x99s soul.\n\nThe last lights had been put out in the huts. The last sounds had died\naway in the village. The wattle fences and the cattle gleaming white in\nthe yards, the roofs of the houses and the stately poplars, all seemed\nto be sleeping the labourers\xe2\x80\x99 healthy peaceful sleep. Only the\nincessant ringing voices of frogs from the damp distance reached the\nyoung man. In the east the stars were growing fewer and fewer and\nseemed to be melting in the increasing light, but overhead they were\ndenser and deeper than before. The old man was dozing with his head on\nhis hand. A cock crowed in the yard opposite, but Ol\xc3\xa9nin still paced up\nand down thinking of something. The sound of a song sung by several\nvoices reached him and he stepped up to the fence and listened. The\nvoices of several young Cossacks carolled a merry song, and one voice\nwas distinguishable among them all by its firm strength.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know who is singing there?\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man, rousing himself.\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is the Brave, Luk\xc3\xa1shka. He has killed a Ch\xc3\xa9chen and now he\nrejoices. And what is there to rejoice at? ... The fool, the fool!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd have you ever killed people?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou devil!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted the old man. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you asking? One must not\ntalk so. It is a serious thing to destroy a human being ... Ah, a very\nserious thing! Good-bye, my dear fellow. I\xe2\x80\x99ve eaten my fill and am\ndrunk,\xe2\x80\x9d he said rising. \xe2\x80\x9cShall I come tomorrow to go shooting?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, come!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMind, get up early; if you oversleep you will be fined!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever fear, I\xe2\x80\x99ll be up before you,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\nThe old man left. The song ceased, but one could hear footsteps and\nmerry talk. A little later the singing broke out again but farther\naway, and Er\xc3\xb3shka\xe2\x80\x99s loud voice chimed in with the other. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat people,\nwhat a life!\xe2\x80\x9d thought Ol\xc3\xa9nin with a sigh as he returned alone to his\nhut.\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XVI\n\n\nDaddy Er\xc3\xb3shka was a superannuated and solitary Cossack: twenty years\nago his wife had gone over to the Orthodox Church and run away from him\nand married a Russian sergeant-major, and he had no children. He was\nnot bragging when he spoke of himself as having been the boldest\ndare-devil in the village when he was young. Everybody in the regiment\nknew of his old-time prowess. The death of more than one Russian, as\nwell as Ch\xc3\xa9chen, lay on his conscience. He used to go plundering in the\nmountains, and robbed the Russians too; and he had twice been in\nprison. The greater part of his life was spent in the forests, hunting.\nThere he lived for days on a crust of bread and drank nothing but\nwater. But on the other hand, when he was in the village he made merry\nfrom morning to night. After leaving Ol\xc3\xa9nin he slept for a couple of\nhours and awoke before it was light. He lay on his bed thinking of the\nman he had become acquainted with the evening before. Ol\xc3\xa9nin\xe2\x80\x99s\n\xe2\x80\x9csimplicity\xe2\x80\x9d (simplicity in the sense of not grudging him a drink)\npleased him very much, and so did Ol\xc3\xa9nin himself. He wondered why the\nRussians were all \xe2\x80\x9csimple\xe2\x80\x9d and so rich, and why they were educated, and\nyet knew nothing. He pondered on these questions and also considered\nwhat he might get out of Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\nDaddy Er\xc3\xb3shka\xe2\x80\x99s hut was of a good size and not old, but the absence of\na woman was very noticeable in it. Contrary to the usual cleanliness of\nthe Cossacks, the whole of this hut was filthy and exceedingly untidy.\nA blood-stained coat had been thrown on the table, half a dough-cake\nlay beside a plucked and mangled crow with which to feed the hawk.\nSandals of raw hide, a gun, a dagger, a little bag, wet clothes, and\nsundry rags lay scattered on the benches. In a corner stood a tub with\nstinking water, in which another pair of sandals were being steeped,\nand near by was a gun and a hunting-screen. On the floor a net had been\nthrown down and several dead pheasants lay there, while a hen tied by\nits leg was walking about near the table pecking among the dirt. In the\nunheated oven stood a broken pot with some kind of milky liquid. On the\ntop of the oven a falcon was screeching and trying to break the cord by\nwhich it was tied, and a moulting hawk sat quietly on the edge of the\noven, looking askance at the hen and occasionally bowing its head to\nright and left. Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka himself, in his shirt, lay on his back on\na short bed rigged up between the wall and the oven, with his strong\nlegs raised and his feet on the oven. He was picking with his thick\nfingers at the scratches left on his hands by the hawk, which he was\naccustomed to carry without wearing gloves. The whole room, especially\nnear the old man, was filled with that strong but not unpleasant\nmixture of smells that he always carried about with him.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Uyde-ma_, Daddy?\xe2\x80\x9d (Is Daddy in?) came through the window in a sharp\nvoice, which he at once recognized as Luk\xc3\xa1shka\xe2\x80\x99s.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Uyde, Uyde, Uyde_. I am in!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted the old man. \xe2\x80\x9cCome in, neighbour\nMark, Luke Mark. Come to see Daddy? On your way to the cordon?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAt the sound of his master\xe2\x80\x99s shout the hawk flapped his wings and\npulled at his cord.\n\nThe old man was fond of Luk\xc3\xa1shka, who was the only man he excepted from\nhis general contempt for the younger generation of Cossacks. Besides\nthat, Luk\xc3\xa1shka and his mother, as near neighbours, often gave the old\nman wine, clotted cream, and other home produce which Er\xc3\xb3shka did not\npossess. Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka, who all his life had allowed himself to get\ncarried away, always explained his infatuations from a practical point\nof view. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, why not?\xe2\x80\x9d he used to say to himself. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll give them\nsome fresh meat, or a bird, and they won\xe2\x80\x99t forget Daddy: they\xe2\x80\x99ll\nsometimes bring a cake or a piece of pie.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood morning, Mark! I am glad to see you,\xe2\x80\x9d shouted the old man\ncheerfully, and quickly putting down his bare feet he jumped off his\nbed and walked a step or two along the creaking floor, looked down at\nhis out-turned toes, and suddenly, amused by the appearance of his\nfeet, smiled, stamped with his bare heel on the ground, stamped again,\nand then performed a funny dance-step. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s clever, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked,\nhis small eyes glistening. Luk\xc3\xa1shka smiled faintly. \xe2\x80\x9cGoing back to the\ncordon?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the old man.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have brought the _chikhir_ I promised you when we were at the\ncordon.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay Christ save you!\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man, and he took up the extremely\nwide trousers that were lying on the floor, and his _beshmet_, put them\non, fastened a strap round his waist, poured some water from an\nearthenware pot over his hands, wiped them on the old trousers,\nsmoothed his beard with a bit of comb, and stopped in front of\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka. \xe2\x80\x9cReady,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka fetched a cup, wiped it and filled it with wine, and then\nhanded it to the old man.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour health! To the Father and the Son!\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man, accepting\nthe wine with solemnity. \xe2\x80\x9cMay you have what you desire, may you always\nbe a hero, and obtain a cross.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka also drank a little after repeating a prayer, and then put the\nwine on the table. The old man rose and brought out some dried fish\nwhich he laid on the threshold, where he beat it with a stick to make\nit tender; then, having put it with his horny hands on a blue plate\n(his only one), he placed it on the table.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have all I want. I have victuals, thank God!\xe2\x80\x9d he said proudly.\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, and what of M\xc3\xb3sev?\xe2\x80\x9d he added.\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka, evidently wishing to know the old man\xe2\x80\x99s opinion, told him how\nthe officer had taken the gun from him.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind the gun,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you don\xe2\x80\x99t give the gun you\nwill get no reward.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut they say, Daddy, it\xe2\x80\x99s little reward a fellow gets when he is not\nyet a mounted Cossack; and the gun is a fine one, a Crimean, worth\neighty rubles.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh, let it go! I had a dispute like that with an officer, he wanted my\nhorse. \xe2\x80\x98Give it me and you\xe2\x80\x99ll be made a cornet,\xe2\x80\x99 says he. I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t,\nand I got nothing!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Daddy, but you see I have to buy a horse; and they say you can\xe2\x80\x99t\nget one the other side of the river under fifty rubles, and mother has\nnot yet sold our wine.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh, we didn\xe2\x80\x99t bother,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man; \xe2\x80\x9cwhen Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka was your\nage he already stole herds of horses from the Nog\xc3\xa1y folk and drove them\nacross the T\xc3\xa9rek. Sometimes we\xe2\x80\x99d give a fine horse for a quart of vodka\nor a cloak.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy so cheap?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Luk\xc3\xa1shka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re a fool, a fool, Mark,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man contemptuously. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy,\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s what one steals for, so as not to be stingy! As for you, I\nsuppose you haven\xe2\x80\x99t so much as seen how one drives off a herd of\nhorses? Why don\xe2\x80\x99t you speak?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s one to say, Daddy?\xe2\x80\x9d replied Luk\xc3\xa1shka. \xe2\x80\x9cIt seems we are not the\nsame sort of men as you were.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re a fool, Mark, a fool! \xe2\x80\x98Not the same sort of men!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d retorted the\nold man, mimicking the Cossack lad. \xe2\x80\x9cI was not that sort of Cossack at\nyour age.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Luk\xc3\xa1shka.\n\nThe old man shook his head contemptuously.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDaddy Er\xc3\xb3shka was _simple;_ he did not grudge anything! That\xe2\x80\x99s why I\nwas _kunak_ with all Ch\xc3\xa9chnya. A _kunak_ would come to visit me and I\xe2\x80\x99d\nmake him drunk with vodka and make him happy and put him to sleep with\nme, and when I went to see him I\xe2\x80\x99d take him a present\xe2\x80\x94a dagger! That\xe2\x80\x99s\nthe way it is done, and not as you do nowadays: the only amusement lads\nhave now is to crack seeds and spit out the shells!\xe2\x80\x9d the old man\nfinished contemptuously, imitating the present-day Cossacks cracking\nseeds and spitting out the shells.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka; \xe2\x80\x9cthat\xe2\x80\x99s so!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you wish to be a fellow of the right sort, be a brave and not a\npeasant! Because even a peasant can buy a horse\xe2\x80\x94pay the money and take\nthe horse.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThey were silent for a while.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, of course it\xe2\x80\x99s dull both in the village and the cordon, Daddy:\nbut there\xe2\x80\x99s nowhere one can go for a bit of sport. All our fellows are\nso timid. Take Naz\xc3\xa1rka. The other day when we went to the Tartar\nvillage, Gir\xc3\xa9y Khan asked us to come to Nog\xc3\xa1y to take some horses, but\nno one went, and how was I to go alone?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what of Daddy? Do you think I am quite dried up? ... No, I\xe2\x80\x99m not\ndried up. Let me have a horse and I\xe2\x80\x99ll be off to Nog\xc3\xa1y at once.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the good of talking nonsense!\xe2\x80\x9d said Luke. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99d better tell me\nwhat to do about Gir\xc3\xa9y Khan. He says, \xe2\x80\x98Only bring horses to the T\xc3\xa9rek,\nand then even if you bring a whole stud I\xe2\x80\x99ll find a place for them.\xe2\x80\x99\nYou see he\xe2\x80\x99s also a shaven-headed Tartar\xe2\x80\x94how\xe2\x80\x99s one to believe him?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou may trust Gir\xc3\xa9y Khan, all his kin were good people. His father too\nwas a faithful _kunak_. But listen to Daddy and I won\xe2\x80\x99t teach you\nwrong: make him take an oath, then it will be all right. And if you go\nwith him, have your pistol ready all the same, especially when it comes\nto dividing up the horses. I was nearly killed that way once by a\nCh\xc3\xa9chen. I wanted ten rubles from him for a horse. Trusting is all\nright, but don\xe2\x80\x99t go to sleep without a gun.\xe2\x80\x9d Luk\xc3\xa1shka listened\nattentively to the old man.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, Daddy, have you any stone-break grass?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked after a pause.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I haven\xe2\x80\x99t any, but I\xe2\x80\x99ll teach you how to get it. You\xe2\x80\x99re a good lad\nand won\xe2\x80\x99t forget the old man.... Shall I tell you?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell me, Daddy.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know a tortoise? She\xe2\x80\x99s a devil, the tortoise is!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course I know!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cFind her nest and fence it round so that she can\xe2\x80\x99t get in. Well,\nshe\xe2\x80\x99ll come, go round it, and then will go off to find the stone-break\ngrass and will bring some along and destroy the fence. Anyhow next\nmorning come in good time, and where the fence is broken there you\xe2\x80\x99ll\nfind the stone-break grass lying. Take it wherever you like. No lock\nand no bar will be able to stop you.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you tried it yourself, Daddy?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs for trying, I have not tried it, but I was told of it by good\npeople. I used only one charm: that was to repeat the Pilgrim rhyme\nwhen mounting my horse; and no one ever killed me!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the Pilgrim rhyme, Daddy?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat, don\xe2\x80\x99t you know it? Oh, what people! You\xe2\x80\x99re right to ask Daddy.\nWell, listen, and repeat after me:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHail! Ye, living in Sion,\nThis is your King,\nOur steeds we shall sit on,\nSophonius is weeping.\nZacharias is speaking,\nFather Pilgrim,\nMankind ever loving.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cKind ever loving,\xe2\x80\x9d the old man repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you know it now? Try it.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka laughed.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, Daddy, was it that that hindered their killing you? Maybe it\njust happened so!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve grown too clever! You learn it all, and say it. It will do you\nno harm. Well, suppose you have sung \xe2\x80\x98Pilgrim\xe2\x80\x99, it\xe2\x80\x99s all right,\xe2\x80\x9d and\nthe old man himself began laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cBut just one thing, Luke, don\xe2\x80\x99t\nyou go to Nog\xc3\xa1y!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cTimes have changed. You are not the same men. You\xe2\x80\x99ve become rubbishy\nCossacks! And see how many Russians have come down on us! You\xe2\x80\x99d get to\nprison. Really, give it up! Just as if you could! Now G\xc3\xadrchik and I, we\nused...\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAnd the old man was about to begin one of his endless tales, but\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka glanced at the window and interrupted him.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is quite light. Daddy. It\xe2\x80\x99s time to be off. Look us up some day.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay Christ save you! I\xe2\x80\x99ll go to the officer; I promised to take him\nout shooting. He seems a good fellow.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XVII\n\n\nFrom Er\xc3\xb3shka\xe2\x80\x99s hut Luk\xc3\xa1shka went home. As he returned, the dewy mists\nwere rising from the ground and enveloped the village. In various\nplaces the cattle, though out of sight, could be heard beginning to\nstir. The cocks called to one another with increasing frequency and\ninsistence. The air was becoming more transparent, and the villagers\nwere getting up. Not till he was close to it could Luk\xc3\xa1shka discern the\nfence of his yard, all wet with dew, the porch of the hut, and the open\nshed. From the misty yard he heard the sound of an axe chopping wood.\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka entered the hut. His mother was up, and stood at the oven\nthrowing wood into it. His little sister was still lying in bed asleep.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Luk\xc3\xa1shka, had enough holiday-making?\xe2\x80\x9d asked his mother softly.\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere did you spend the night?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was in the village,\xe2\x80\x9d replied her son reluctantly, reaching for his\nmusket, which he drew from its cover and examined carefully.\n\nHis mother swayed her head.\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka poured a little gunpowder onto the pan, took out a little bag\nfrom which he drew some empty cartridge cases which he began filling,\ncarefully plugging each one with a ball wrapped in a rag. Then, having\ntested the loaded cartridges with his teeth and examined them, he put\ndown the bag.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, Mother, I told you the bags wanted mending; have they been\ndone?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes, our dumb girl was mending something last night. Why, is it\ntime for you to be going back to the cordon? I haven\xe2\x80\x99t seen anything of\nyou!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, as soon as I have got ready I shall have to go,\xe2\x80\x9d answered\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka, tying up the gunpowder. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd where is our dumb one? Outside?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cChopping wood, I expect. She kept fretting for you. \xe2\x80\x98I shall not see\nhim at all!\xe2\x80\x99 she said. She puts her hand to her face like this, and\nclicks her tongue and presses her hands to her heart as much as to\nsay\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98sorry.\xe2\x80\x99 Shall I call her in? She understood all about the\n_abrek_.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cCall her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I had some tallow there; bring it: I\nmust grease my sword.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe old woman went out, and a few minutes later Luk\xc3\xa1shka\xe2\x80\x99s dumb sister\ncame up the creaking steps and entered the hut. She was six years older\nthan her brother and would have been extremely like him had it not been\nfor the dull and coarsely changeable expression (common to all deaf and\ndumb people) of her face. She wore a coarse smock all patched; her feet\nwere bare and muddy, and on her head she had an old blue kerchief. Her\nneck, arms, and face were sinewy like a peasant\xe2\x80\x99s. Her clothing and her\nwhole appearance indicated that she always did the hard work of a man.\nShe brought in a heap of logs which she threw down by the oven. Then\nshe went up to her brother, and with a joyful smile which made her\nwhole face pucker up, touched him on the shoulder and began making\nrapid signs to him with her hands, her face, and whole body.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s right, that\xe2\x80\x99s right, St\xc3\xabpka is a trump!\xe2\x80\x9d answered the brother,\nnodding. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s fetched everything and mended everything, she\xe2\x80\x99s a\ntrump! Here, take this for it!\xe2\x80\x9d He brought out two pieces of\ngingerbread from his pocket and gave them to her.\n\nThe dumb woman\xe2\x80\x99s face flushed with pleasure, and she began making a\nweird noise for joy. Having seized the gingerbread she began to\ngesticulate still more rapidly, frequently pointing in one direction\nand passing her thick finger over her eyebrows and her face. Luk\xc3\xa1shka\nunderstood her and kept nodding, while he smiled slightly. She was\ntelling him to give the girls dainties, and that the girls liked him,\nand that one girl, Mary\xc3\xa1nka\xe2\x80\x94the best of them all\xe2\x80\x94loved him. She\nindicated Mary\xc3\xa1nka by rapidly pointing in the direction of Mary\xc3\xa1nka\xe2\x80\x99s\nhome and to her own eyebrows and face, and by smacking her lips and\nswaying her head. \xe2\x80\x9cLoves\xe2\x80\x9d she expressed by pressing her hands to her\nbreast, kissing her hand, and pretending to embrace someone. Their\nmother returned to the hut, and seeing what her dumb daughter was\nsaying, smiled and shook her head. Her daughter showed her the\ngingerbread and again made the noise which expressed joy.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI told Ul\xc3\xadtka the other day that I\xe2\x80\x99d send a matchmaker to them,\xe2\x80\x9d said\nthe mother. \xe2\x80\x9cShe took my words well.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka looked silently at his mother.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how about selling the wine, mother? I need a horse.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll cart it when I have time. I must get the barrels ready,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\nmother, evidently not wishing her son to meddle in domestic matters.\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen you go out you\xe2\x80\x99ll find a bag in the passage. I borrowed from the\nneighbours and got something for you to take back to the cordon; or\nshall I put it in your saddle-bag?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Luk\xc3\xa1shka. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd if Gir\xc3\xa9y Khan should come across\nthe river send him to me at the cordon, for I shan\xe2\x80\x99t get leave again\nfor a long time now; I have some business with him.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe began to get ready to start.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will send him on,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old woman. \xe2\x80\x9cIt seems you have been\nspreeing at Y\xc3\xa1mka\xe2\x80\x99s all the time. I went out in the night to see the\ncattle, and I think it was your voice I heard singing songs.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka did not reply, but went out into the passage, threw the bags\nover his shoulder, tucked up the skirts of his coat, took his musket,\nand then stopped for a moment on the threshold.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-bye, mother!\xe2\x80\x9d he said as he closed the gate behind him. \xe2\x80\x9cSend me\na small barrel with Naz\xc3\xa1rka. I promised it to the lads, and he\xe2\x80\x99ll call\nfor it.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay Christ keep you, Luk\xc3\xa1shka. God be with you! I\xe2\x80\x99ll send you some,\nsome from the new barrel,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old woman, going to the fence: \xe2\x80\x9cBut\nlisten,\xe2\x80\x9d she added, leaning over the fence.\n\nThe Cossack stopped.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve been making merry here; well, that\xe2\x80\x99s all right. Why should not\na young man amuse himself? God has sent you luck and that\xe2\x80\x99s good. But\nnow look out and mind, my son. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you go and get into mischief.\nAbove all, satisfy your superiors: one has to! And I will sell the wine\nand find money for a horse and will arrange a match with the girl for\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, all right!\xe2\x80\x9d answered her son, frowning.\n\nHis deaf sister shouted to attract his attention. She pointed to her\nhead and the palm of her hand, to indicate the shaved head of a\nCh\xc3\xa9chen. Then she frowned, and pretending to aim with a gun, she\nshrieked and began rapidly humming and shaking her head. This meant\nthat Luk\xc3\xa1shka should kill another Ch\xc3\xa9chen.\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka understood. He smiled, and shifting the gun at his back under\nhis cloak stepped lightly and rapidly, and soon disappeared in the\nthick mist.\n\nThe old woman, having stood a little while at the gate, returned\nsilently to the hut and immediately began working.\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XVIII\n\n\nLukasha returned to the cordon and at the same time Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka\nwhistled to his dogs and, climbing over his wattle fence, went to\nOl\xc3\xa9nin\xe2\x80\x99s lodging, passing by the back of the houses (he disliked\nmeeting women before going out hunting or shooting). He found Ol\xc3\xa9nin\nstill asleep, and even Vany\xc3\xbasha, though awake, was still in bed and\nlooking round the room considering whether it was not time to get up,\nwhen Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka, gun on shoulder and in full hunter\xe2\x80\x99s trappings,\nopened the door.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cA cudgel!\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted in his deep voice. \xe2\x80\x9cAn alarm! The Ch\xc3\xa9chens are\nupon us! Iv\xc3\xa1n! Get the samovar ready for your master, and get up\nyourself\xe2\x80\x94quick,\xe2\x80\x9d cried the old man. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s our way, my good man! Why\neven the girls are already up! Look out of the window. See, she\xe2\x80\x99s going\nfor water and you\xe2\x80\x99re still sleeping!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin awoke and jumped up, feeling fresh and lighthearted at the sight\nof the old man and at the sound of his voice.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuick, Vany\xc3\xbasha, quick!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that the way you go hunting?\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man. \xe2\x80\x9cOthers are having\ntheir breakfast and you are asleep! Lyam! Here!\xe2\x80\x9d he called to his dog.\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs your gun ready?\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted, as loud as if a whole crowd were in\nthe hut.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it\xe2\x80\x99s true I\xe2\x80\x99m guilty, but it can\xe2\x80\x99t be helped! The powder,\nVany\xc3\xbasha, and the wads!\xe2\x80\x9d said Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cA fine!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted the old man.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Du tay voulay vou?_\xe2\x80\x9d asked Vany\xc3\xbasha, grinning.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re not one of us\xe2\x80\x94your gabble is not like our speech, you devil!\xe2\x80\x9d\nthe old man shouted at Vany\xc3\xbasha, showing the stumps of his teeth.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cA first offence must be forgiven,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ol\xc3\xa9nin playfully, drawing on\nhis high boots.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe first offence shall be forgiven,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Er\xc3\xb3shka, \xe2\x80\x9cbut if you\noversleep another time you\xe2\x80\x99ll be fined a pail of _chikhir_. When it\ngets warmer you won\xe2\x80\x99t find the deer.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd even if we do find him he is wiser than we are,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ol\xc3\xa9nin,\nrepeating the words spoken by the old man the evening before, \xe2\x80\x9cand you\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t deceive him!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, laugh away! You kill one first, and then you may talk. Now then,\nhurry up! Look, there\xe2\x80\x99s the master himself coming to see you,\xe2\x80\x9d added\nEr\xc3\xb3shka, looking out of the window. \xe2\x80\x9cJust see how he\xe2\x80\x99s got himself up.\nHe\xe2\x80\x99s put on a new coat so that you should see that he\xe2\x80\x99s an officer. Ah,\nthese people, these people!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nSure enough Vany\xc3\xbasha came in and announced that the master of the house\nwished to see Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9c_L\xe2\x80\x99arjan!_\xe2\x80\x9d he remarked profoundly, to forewarn his master of the\nmeaning of this visitation. Following him, the master of the house in a\nnew Circassian coat with an officer\xe2\x80\x99s stripes on the shoulders and with\npolished boots (quite exceptional among Cossacks) entered the room,\nswaying from side to side, and congratulated his lodger on his safe\narrival.\n\nThe cornet, Elias Vas\xc3\xadlich, was an _educated_ Cossack. He had been to\nRussia proper, was a regimental schoolteacher, and above all he was\nnoble. He wished to appear noble, but one could not help feeling\nbeneath his grotesque pretence of polish, his affectation, his\nself-confidence, and his absurd way of speaking, he was just the same\nas Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka. This could also be clearly seen by his sunburnt face\nand his hands and his red nose. Ol\xc3\xa9nin asked him to sit down.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood morning, Father Elias Vas\xc3\xadlich,\xe2\x80\x9d said Er\xc3\xb3shka, rising with (or so\nit seemed to Ol\xc3\xa9nin) an ironically low bow.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood morning. Daddy. So you\xe2\x80\x99re here already,\xe2\x80\x9d said the cornet, with a\ncareless nod.\n\nThe cornet was a man of about forty, with a grey pointed beard, skinny\nand lean, but handsome and very fresh-looking for his age. Having come\nto see Ol\xc3\xa9nin he was evidently afraid of being taken for an ordinary\nCossack, and wanted to let Ol\xc3\xa9nin feel his importance from the first.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s our Egyptian Nimrod,\xe2\x80\x9d he remarked, addressing Ol\xc3\xa9nin and\npointing to the old man with a self-satisfied smile. \xe2\x80\x9cA mighty hunter\nbefore the Lord! He\xe2\x80\x99s our foremost man on every hand. You\xe2\x80\x99ve already\nbeen pleased to get acquainted with him.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nDaddy Er\xc3\xb3shka gazed at his feet in their shoes of wet raw hide and\nshook his head thoughtfully at the cornet\xe2\x80\x99s ability and learning, and\nmuttered to himself: \xe2\x80\x9cGyptian Nimvrod! What things he invents!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you see we mean to go hunting,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir, exactly,\xe2\x80\x9d said the cornet, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I have a small business with\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you want?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSeeing that you are a gentleman,\xe2\x80\x9d began the cornet, \xe2\x80\x9cand as I may\nunderstand myself to be in the rank of an officer too, and therefore we\nmay always progressively negotiate, as gentlemen do.\xe2\x80\x9d (He stopped and\nlooked with a smile at Ol\xc3\xa9nin and at the old man.) \xe2\x80\x9cBut if you have the\ndesire with my consent, then, as my wife is a foolish woman of our\nclass, she could not quite comprehend your words of yesterday\xe2\x80\x99s date.\nTherefore my quarters might be let for six rubles to the Regimental\nAdjutant, without the stables; but I can always avert that from myself\nfree of charge. But, as you desire, therefore I, being myself of an\nofficer\xe2\x80\x99s rank, can come to an agreement with you in everything\npersonally, as an inhabitant of this district, not according to our\ncustoms, but can maintain the conditions in every way....\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpeaks clearly!\xe2\x80\x9d muttered the old man.\n\nThe cornet continued in the same strain for a long time. At last, not\nwithout difficulty, Ol\xc3\xa9nin gathered that the cornet wished to let his\nrooms to him, Ol\xc3\xa9nin, for six rubles a month. The latter gladly agreed\nto this, and offered his visitor a glass of tea. The cornet declined\nit.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAccording to our silly custom we consider it a sort of sin to drink\nout of a \xe2\x80\x98worldly\xe2\x80\x99 tumbler,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThough, of course, with my\neducation I may understand, but my wife from her human weakness...\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell then, will you have some tea?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you will permit me, I will bring my own particular glass,\xe2\x80\x9d answered\nthe cornet, and stepped out into the porch.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBring me my glass!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried.\n\nIn a few minutes the door opened and a young sunburnt arm in a print\nsleeve thrust itself in, holding a tumbler in the hand. The cornet went\nup, took it, and whispered something to his daughter. Ol\xc3\xa9nin poured tea\nfor the cornet into the latter\xe2\x80\x99s own \xe2\x80\x9cparticular\xe2\x80\x9d glass, and for\nEr\xc3\xb3shka into a \xe2\x80\x9cworldly\xe2\x80\x9d glass.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHowever, I do not desire to detain you,\xe2\x80\x9d said the cornet, scalding his\nlips and emptying his tumbler. \xe2\x80\x9cI too have a great liking for fishing,\nand I am here, so to say, only on leave of absence for recreation from\nmy duties. I too have the desire to tempt fortune and see whether some\n_Gifts of the T\xc3\xa9rek_ may not fall to my share. I hope you too will come\nand see us and have a drink of our wine, according to the custom of our\nvillage,\xe2\x80\x9d he added.\n\nThe cornet bowed, shook hands with Ol\xc3\xa9nin, and went out. While Ol\xc3\xa9nin\nwas getting ready, he heard the cornet giving orders to his family in\nan authoritative and sensible tone, and a few minutes later he saw him\npass by the window in a tattered coat with his trousers rolled up to\nhis knees and a fishing net over his shoulder.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cA rascal!\xe2\x80\x9d said Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka, emptying his \xe2\x80\x9cworldly\xe2\x80\x9d tumbler. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\nwill you really pay him six rubles? Was such a thing ever heard of?\nThey would let you the best hut in the village for two rubles. What a\nbeast! Why, I\xe2\x80\x99d let you have mine for three!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I\xe2\x80\x99ll remain here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSix rubles!... Clearly it\xe2\x80\x99s a fool\xe2\x80\x99s money. Eh, eh, eh!\xe2\x80\x9d answered the\nold man. \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s have some _chikhir_, Iv\xc3\xa1n!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHaving had a snack and a drink of vodka to prepare themselves for the\nroad, Ol\xc3\xa9nin and the old man went out together before eight o\xe2\x80\x99clock.\n\nAt the gate they came up against a wagon to which a pair of oxen were\nharnessed. With a white kerchief tied round her head down to her eyes,\na coat over her smock, and wearing high boots, Mary\xc3\xa1nka with a long\nswitch in her hand was dragging the oxen by a cord tied to their horns.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMammy,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man, pretending that he was going to seize her.\n\nMary\xc3\xa1nka flourished her switch at him and glanced merrily at them both\nwith her beautiful eyes.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin felt still more light-hearted.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow then, come on, come on,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, throwing his gun on his shoulder\nand conscious of the girl\xe2\x80\x99s eyes upon him.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGee up!\xe2\x80\x9d sounded Mary\xc3\xa1nka\xe2\x80\x99s voice behind them, followed by the creak\nof the moving wagon.\n\nAs long as their road lay through the pastures at the back of the\nvillage Er\xc3\xb3shka went on talking. He could not forget the cornet and\nkept on abusing him.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy are you so angry with him?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s stingy. I don\xe2\x80\x99t like it,\xe2\x80\x9d answered the old man. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99ll leave it\nall behind when he dies! Then who\xe2\x80\x99s he saving up for? He\xe2\x80\x99s built two\nhouses, and he\xe2\x80\x99s got a second garden from his brother by a law-suit.\nAnd in the matter of papers what a dog he is! They come to him from\nother villages to fill up documents. As he writes it out, exactly so it\nhappens. He gets it quite exact. But who is he saving for? He\xe2\x80\x99s only\ngot one boy and the girl; when she\xe2\x80\x99s married who\xe2\x80\x99ll be left?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell then, he\xe2\x80\x99s saving up for her dowry,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat dowry? The girl is sought after, she\xe2\x80\x99s a fine girl. But he\xe2\x80\x99s such\na devil that he must yet marry her to a rich fellow. He wants to get a\nbig price for her. There\xe2\x80\x99s Luke, a Cossack, a neighbour and a nephew of\nmine, a fine lad. It\xe2\x80\x99s he who killed the Ch\xc3\xa9chen\xe2\x80\x94he has been wooing her\nfor a long time, but he hasn\xe2\x80\x99t let him have her. He\xe2\x80\x99s given one excuse,\nand another, and a third. \xe2\x80\x98The girl\xe2\x80\x99s too young,\xe2\x80\x99 he says. But I know\nwhat he is thinking. He wants to keep them bowing to him. He\xe2\x80\x99s been\nacting shamefully about that girl. Still, they will get her for\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka, because he is the best Cossack in the village, a brave, who\nhas killed an _abrek_ and will be rewarded with a cross.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how about this? When I was walking up and down the yard last\nnight, I saw my landlord\xe2\x80\x99s daughter and some Cossack kissing,\xe2\x80\x9d said\nOl\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re pretending!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the old man, stopping.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn my word,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWomen are the devil,\xe2\x80\x9d said Er\xc3\xb3shka pondering. \xe2\x80\x9cBut what Cossack was\nit?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what sort of a cap had he, a white one?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd a red coat? About your height?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, a bit taller.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s he!\xe2\x80\x9d and Er\xc3\xb3shka burst out laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s himself, it\xe2\x80\x99s Mark. He\nis Luke, but I call him Mark for a joke. His very self! I love him. I\nwas just such a one myself. What\xe2\x80\x99s the good of minding them? My\nsweetheart used to sleep with her mother and her sister-in-law, but I\nmanaged to get in. She used to sleep upstairs; that witch her mother\nwas a regular demon; it\xe2\x80\x99s awful how she hated me. Well, I used to come\nwith a chum, G\xc3\xadrchik his name was. We\xe2\x80\x99d come under her window and I\xe2\x80\x99d\nclimb on his shoulders, push up the window and begin groping about. She\nused to sleep just there on a bench. Once I woke her up and she nearly\ncalled out. She hadn\xe2\x80\x99t recognized me. \xe2\x80\x98Who is there?\xe2\x80\x99 she said, and I\ncould not answer. Her mother was even beginning to stir, but I took off\nmy cap and shoved it over her mouth; and she at once knew it by a seam\nin it, and ran out to me. I used not to want anything then. She\xe2\x80\x99d bring\nalong clotted cream and grapes and everything,\xe2\x80\x9d added Er\xc3\xb3shka (who\nalways explained things practically), \xe2\x80\x9cand she wasn\xe2\x80\x99t the only one. It\nwas a life!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what now?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow we\xe2\x80\x99ll follow the dog, get a pheasant to settle on a tree, and then\nyou may fire.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWould you have made up to Mary\xc3\xa1nka?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAttend to the dogs. I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you tonight,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man, pointing\nto his favourite dog, Lyam.\n\nAfter a pause they continued talking, while they went about a hundred\npaces. Then the old man stopped again and pointed to a twig that lay\nacross the path.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you think of that?\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou think it\xe2\x80\x99s nothing? It\xe2\x80\x99s bad\nthat this stick is lying so.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy is it bad?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe smiled.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you don\xe2\x80\x99t know anything. Just listen to me. When a stick lies like\nthat don\xe2\x80\x99t you step across it, but go round it or throw it off the path\nthis way, and say \xe2\x80\x98Father and Son and Holy Ghost,\xe2\x80\x99 and then go on with\nGod\xe2\x80\x99s blessing. Nothing will happen to you. That\xe2\x80\x99s what the old men\nused to teach me.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, what rubbish!\xe2\x80\x9d said Ol\xc3\xa9nin. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99d better tell me more about\nMary\xc3\xa1nka. Does she carry on with Luk\xc3\xa1shka?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush ... be quiet now!\xe2\x80\x9d the old man again interrupted in a whisper:\n\xe2\x80\x9cjust listen, we\xe2\x80\x99ll go round through the forest.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAnd the old man, stepping quietly in his soft shoes, led the way by a\nnarrow path leading into the dense, wild, overgrown forest. Now and\nagain with a frown he turned to look at Ol\xc3\xa9nin, who rustled and\nclattered with his heavy boots and, carrying his gun carelessly,\nseveral times caught the twigs of trees that grew across the path.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t make a noise. Step softly, soldier!\xe2\x80\x9d the old man whispered\nangrily.\n\nThere was a feeling in the air that the sun had risen. The mist was\ndissolving but it still enveloped the tops of the trees. The forest\nlooked terribly high. At every step the aspect changed: what had\nappeared like a tree proved to be a bush, and a reed looked like a\ntree.\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XIX\n\n\nThe mist had partly lifted, showing the wet reed thatches, and was now\nturning into dew that moistened the road and the grass beside the\nfence. Smoke rose everywhere in clouds from the chimneys. The people\nwere going out of the village, some to their work, some to the river,\nand some to the cordon. The hunters walked together along the damp,\ngrass-grown path. The dogs, wagging their tails and looking at their\nmasters, ran on both sides of them. Myriads of gnats hovered in the air\nand pursued the hunters, covering their backs, eyes, and hands. The air\nwas fragrant with the grass and with the dampness of the forest. Ol\xc3\xa9nin\ncontinually looked round at the ox-cart in which Mary\xc3\xa1nka sat urging on\nthe oxen with a long switch.\n\nIt was calm. The sounds from the village, audible at first, now no\nlonger reached the sportsmen. Only the brambles cracked as the dogs ran\nunder them, and now and then birds called to one another. Ol\xc3\xa9nin knew\nthat danger lurked in the forest, that _abreks_ always hid in such\nplaces. But he knew too that in the forest, for a man on foot, a gun is\na great protection. Not that he was afraid, but he felt that another in\nhis place might be; and looking into the damp misty forest and\nlistening to the rare and faint sounds with strained attention, he\nchanged his hold on his gun and experienced a pleasant feeling that was\nnew to him. Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka went in front, stopping and carefully\nscanning every puddle where an animal had left a double track, and\npointing it out to Ol\xc3\xa9nin. He hardly spoke at all and only occasionally\nmade remarks in a whisper. The track they were following had once been\nmade by wagons, but the grass had long overgrown it. The elm and\nplane-tree forest on both sides of them was so dense and overgrown with\ncreepers that it was impossible to see anything through it. Nearly\nevery tree was enveloped from top to bottom with wild grape vines, and\ndark bramble bushes covered the ground thickly. Every little glade was\novergrown with blackberry bushes and grey feathery reeds. In places,\nlarge hoof-prints and small funnel-shaped pheasant-trails led from the\npath into the thicket. The vigour of the growth of this forest,\nuntrampled by cattle, struck Ol\xc3\xa9nin at every turn, for he had never\nseen anything like it. This forest, the danger, the old man and his\nmysterious whispering, Mary\xc3\xa1nka with her virile upright bearing, and\nthe mountains\xe2\x80\x94all this seemed to him like a dream.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cA pheasant has settled,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered the old man, looking round and\npulling his cap over his face\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cCover your mug! A pheasant!\xe2\x80\x9d he waved\nhis arm angrily at Ol\xc3\xa9nin and pushed forward almost on all fours. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t like a man\xe2\x80\x99s mug.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin was still behind him when the old man stopped and began\nexamining a tree. A cock-pheasant on the tree clucked at the dog that\nwas barking at it, and Ol\xc3\xa9nin saw the pheasant; but at that moment a\nreport, as of a cannon, came from Er\xc3\xb3shka\xe2\x80\x99s enormous gun, the bird\nfluttered up and, losing some feathers, fell to the ground. Coming up\nto the old man Ol\xc3\xa9nin disturbed another, and raising his gun he aimed\nand fired. The pheasant flew swiftly up and then, catching at the\nbranches as he fell, dropped like a stone to the ground.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood man!\xe2\x80\x9d the old man (who could not hit a flying bird) shouted,\nlaughing.\n\nHaving picked up the pheasants they went on. Ol\xc3\xa9nin, excited by the\nexercise and the praise, kept addressing remarks to the old man.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cStop! Come this way,\xe2\x80\x9d the old man interrupted. \xe2\x80\x9cI noticed the track of\ndeer here yesterday.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAfter they had turned into the thicket and gone some three hundred\npaces they scrambled through into a glade overgrown with reeds and\npartly under water. Ol\xc3\xa9nin failed to keep up with the old huntsman and\npresently Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka, some twenty paces in front, stooped down,\nnodding and beckoning with his arm. On coming up with him Ol\xc3\xa9nin saw a\nman\xe2\x80\x99s footprint to which the old man was pointing.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cD\xe2\x80\x99you see?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, well?\xe2\x80\x9d said Ol\xc3\xa9nin, trying to speak as calmly as he could. \xe2\x80\x9cA\nman\xe2\x80\x99s footstep!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nInvoluntarily a thought of Cooper\xe2\x80\x99s _Pathfinder_ and of _abreks_\nflashed through Ol\xc3\xa9nin\xe2\x80\x99s mind, but noticing the mysterious manner with\nwhich the old man moved on, he hesitated to question him and remained\nin doubt whether this mysteriousness was caused by fear of danger or by\nthe sport.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, it\xe2\x80\x99s my own footprint,\xe2\x80\x9d the old man said quietly, and pointed to\nsome grass under which the track of an animal was just perceptible.\n\nThe old man went on, and Ol\xc3\xa9nin kept up with him. Descending to lower\nground some twenty paces farther on they came upon a spreading\npear-tree, under which, on the black earth, lay the fresh dung of some\nanimal.\n\nThe spot, all covered over with wild vines, was like a cosy arbour,\ndark and cool.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s been here this morning,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man with a sigh; \xe2\x80\x9cthe lair\nis still damp, quite fresh.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nSuddenly they heard a terrible crash in the forest some ten paces from\nwhere they stood. They both started and seized their guns, but they\ncould see nothing and only heard the branches breaking. The rhythmical\nrapid thud of galloping was heard for a moment and then changed into a\nhollow rumble which resounded farther and farther off, re-echoing in\nwider and wider circles through the forest. Ol\xc3\xa9nin felt as though\nsomething had snapped in his heart. He peered carefully but vainly into\nthe green thicket and then turned to the old man. Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka with\nhis gun pressed to his breast stood motionless; his cap was thrust\nbackwards, his eyes gleamed with an unwonted glow, and his open mouth,\nwith its worn yellow teeth, seemed to have stiffened in that position.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cA horned stag!\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered, and throwing down his gun in despair he\nbegan pulling at his grey beard, \xe2\x80\x9cHere it stood. We should have come\nround by the path.... Fool! fool!\xe2\x80\x9d and he gave his beard an angry tug.\n\xe2\x80\x9cFool! Pig!\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated, pulling painfully at his own beard. Through\nthe forest something seemed to fly away in the mist, and ever farther\nand farther off was heard the sound of the flight of the stag.\n\nIt was already dusk when, hungry, tired, but full of vigour, Ol\xc3\xa9nin\nreturned with the old man. Dinner was ready. He ate and drank with the\nold man till he felt warm and merry. Ol\xc3\xa9nin then went out into the\nporch. Again, to the west, the mountains rose before his eyes. Again\nthe old man told his endless stories of hunting, of _abreks_, of\nsweethearts, and of all that free and reckless life. Again the fair\nMary\xc3\xa1nka went in and out and across the yard, her beautiful powerful\nform outlined by her smock.\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XX\n\n\nThe next day Ol\xc3\xa9nin went alone to the spot where he and the old man\nstartled the stag. Instead of passing round through the gate he climbed\nover the prickly hedge, as everybody else did, and before he had had\ntime to pull out the thorns that had caught in his coat, his dog, which\nhad run on in front, started two pheasants. He had hardly stepped among\nthe briers when the pheasants began to rise at every step (the old man\nhad not shown him that place the day before as he meant to keep it for\nshooting from behind the screen). Ol\xc3\xa9nin fired twelve times and killed\nfive pheasants, but clambering after them through the briers he got so\nfatigued that he was drenched with perspiration. He called off his dog,\nuncocked his gun, put in a bullet above the small shot, and brushing\naway the mosquitoes with the wide sleeve of his Circassian coat he went\nslowly to the spot where they had been the day before. It was however\nimpossible to keep back the dog, who found trails on the very path, and\nOl\xc3\xa9nin killed two more pheasants, so that after being detained by this\nit was getting towards noon before he began to find the place he was\nlooking for.\n\nThe day was perfectly clear, calm, and hot. The morning moisture had\ndried up even in the forest, and myriads of mosquitoes literally\ncovered his face, his back, and his arms. His dog had turned from black\nto grey, its back being covered with mosquitoes, and so had Ol\xc3\xa9nin\xe2\x80\x99s\ncoat through which the insects thrust their stings. Ol\xc3\xa9nin was ready to\nrun away from them and it seemed to him that it was impossible to live\nin this country in the summer. He was about to go home, but remembering\nthat other people managed to endure such pain he resolved to bear it\nand gave himself up to be devoured. And strange to say, by noontime the\nfeeling became actually pleasant. He even felt that without this\nmosquito-filled atmosphere around him, and that mosquito-paste mingled\nwith perspiration which his hand smeared over his face, and that\nunceasing irritation all over his body, the forest would lose for him\nsome of its character and charm. These myriads of insects were so well\nsuited to that monstrously lavish wild vegetation, these multitudes of\nbirds and beasts which filled the forest, this dark foliage, this hot\nscented air, these runlets filled with turbid water which everywhere\nsoaked through from the T\xc3\xa9rek and gurgled here and there under the\noverhanging leaves, that the very thing which had at first seemed to\nhim dreadful and intolerable now seemed pleasant. After going round the\nplace where yesterday they had found the animal and not finding\nanything, he felt inclined to rest. The sun stood right above the\nforest and poured its perpendicular rays down on his back and head\nwhenever he came out into a glade or onto the road. The seven heavy\npheasants dragged painfully at his waist. Having found the traces of\nyesterday\xe2\x80\x99s stag he crept under a bush into the thicket just where the\nstag had lain, and lay down in its lair. He examined the dark foliage\naround him, the place marked by the stag\xe2\x80\x99s perspiration and yesterday\xe2\x80\x99s\ndung, the imprint of the stag\xe2\x80\x99s knees, the bit of black earth it had\nkicked up, and his own footprints of the day before. He felt cool and\ncomfortable and did not think of or wish for anything. And suddenly he\nwas overcome by such a strange feeling of causeless joy and of love for\neverything, that from an old habit of his childhood he began crossing\nhimself and thanking someone. Suddenly, with extraordinary clearness,\nhe thought: \xe2\x80\x9cHere am I, Dm\xc3\xadtri Ol\xc3\xa9nin, a being quite distinct from\nevery other being, now lying all alone Heaven only knows where\xe2\x80\x94where a\nstag used to live\xe2\x80\x94an old stag, a beautiful stag who perhaps had never\nseen a man, and in a place where no human being has ever sat or thought\nthese thoughts. Here I sit, and around me stand old and young trees,\none of them festooned with wild grape vines, and pheasants are\nfluttering, driving one another about and perhaps scenting their\nmurdered brothers.\xe2\x80\x9d He felt his pheasants, examined them, and wiped the\nwarm blood off his hand onto his coat. \xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps the jackals scent them\nand with dissatisfied faces go off in another direction: above me,\nflying in among the leaves which to them seem enormous islands,\nmosquitoes hang in the air and buzz: one, two, three, four, a hundred,\na thousand, a million mosquitoes, and all of them buzz something or\nother and each one of them is separate from all else and is just such a\nseparate Dm\xc3\xadtri Ol\xc3\xa9nin as I am myself.\xe2\x80\x9d He vividly imagined what the\nmosquitoes buzzed: \xe2\x80\x9cThis way, this way, lads! Here\xe2\x80\x99s some one we can\neat!\xe2\x80\x9d They buzzed and stuck to him. And it was clear to him that he was\nnot a Russian nobleman, a member of Moscow society, the friend and\nrelation of so-and-so and so-and-so, but just such a mosquito, or\npheasant, or deer, as those that were now living all around him. \xe2\x80\x9cJust\nas they, just as Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka, I shall live awhile and die, and as he\nsays truly: \xe2\x80\x98grass will grow and nothing more\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what though the grass does grow?\xe2\x80\x9d he continued thinking. \xe2\x80\x9cStill I\nmust live and be happy, because happiness is all I desire. Never mind\nwhat I am\xe2\x80\x94an animal like all the rest, above whom the grass will grow\nand nothing more; or a frame in which a bit of the one God has been\nset,\xe2\x80\x94still I must live in the very best way. How then must I live to be\nhappy, and why was I not happy before?\xe2\x80\x9d And he began to recall his\nformer life and he felt disgusted with himself. He appeared to himself\nto have been terribly exacting and selfish, though he now saw that all\nthe while he really needed nothing for himself. And he looked round at\nthe foliage with the light shining through it, at the setting sun and\nthe clear sky, and he felt just as happy as before. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy am I happy,\nand what used I to live for?\xe2\x80\x9d thought he. \xe2\x80\x9cHow much I exacted for\nmyself; how I schemed and did not manage to gain anything but shame and\nsorrow! and, there now, I require nothing to be happy;\xe2\x80\x9d and suddenly a\nnew light seemed to reveal itself to him. \xe2\x80\x9cHappiness is this!\xe2\x80\x9d he said\nto himself. \xe2\x80\x9cHappiness lies in living for others. That is evident. The\ndesire for happiness is innate in every man; therefore it is\nlegitimate. When trying to satisfy it selfishly\xe2\x80\x94that is, by seeking for\noneself riches, fame, comforts, or love\xe2\x80\x94it may happen that\ncircumstances arise which make it impossible to satisfy these desires.\nIt follows that it is these desires that are illegitimate, but not the\nneed for happiness. But what desires can always be satisfied despite\nexternal circumstances? What are they? Love, self-sacrifice.\xe2\x80\x9d He was so\nglad and excited when he had discovered this, as it seemed to him, new\ntruth, that he jumped up and began impatiently seeking some one to\nsacrifice himself for, to do good to and to love. \xe2\x80\x9cSince one wants\nnothing for oneself,\xe2\x80\x9d he kept thinking, \xe2\x80\x9cwhy not live for others?\xe2\x80\x9d He\ntook up his gun with the intention of returning home quickly to think\nthis out and to find an opportunity of doing good. He made his way out\nof the thicket. When he had come out into the glade he looked around\nhim; the sun was no longer visible above the tree-tops. It had grown\ncooler and the place seemed to him quite strange and not like the\ncountry round the village. Everything seemed changed\xe2\x80\x94the weather and\nthe character of the forest; the sky was wrapped in clouds, the wind\nwas rustling in the tree-tops, and all around nothing was visible but\nreeds and dying broken-down trees. He called to his dog who had run\naway to follow some animal, and his voice came back as in a desert. And\nsuddenly he was seized with a terrible sense of weirdness. He grew\nfrightened. He remembered the _abreks_ and the murders he had been told\nabout, and he expected every moment that an _abrek_ would spring from\nbehind every bush and he would have to defend his life and die, or be a\ncoward. He thought of God and of the future life as for long he had not\nthought about them. And all around was that same gloomy stern wild\nnature. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd is it worth while living for oneself,\xe2\x80\x9d thought he, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen\nat any moment you may die, and die without having done any good, and so\nthat no one will know of it?\xe2\x80\x9d He went in the direction where he fancied\nthe village lay. Of his shooting he had no further thought; but he felt\ntired to death and peered round at every bush and tree with particular\nattention and almost with terror, expecting every moment to be called\nto account for his life. After having wandered about for a considerable\ntime he came upon a ditch down which was flowing cold sandy water from\nthe T\xc3\xa9rek, and, not to go astray any longer, he decided to follow it.\nHe went on without knowing where the ditch would lead him. Suddenly the\nreeds behind him crackled. He shuddered and seized his gun, and then\nfelt ashamed of himself: the over-excited dog, panting hard, had thrown\nitself into the cold water of the ditch and was lapping it!\n\nHe too had a drink, and then followed the dog in the direction it\nwished to go, thinking it would lead him to the village. But despite\nthe dog\xe2\x80\x99s company everything around him seemed still more dreary. The\nforest grew darker and the wind grew stronger and stronger in the tops\nof the broken old trees. Some large birds circled screeching round\ntheir nests in those trees. The vegetation grew poorer and he came\noftener and oftener upon rustling reeds and bare sandy spaces covered\nwith animal footprints. To the howling of the wind was added another\nkind of cheerless monotonous roar. Altogether his spirits became\ngloomy. Putting his hand behind him he felt his pheasants, and found\none missing. It had broken off and was lost, and only the bleeding head\nand beak remained sticking in his belt. He felt more frightened than he\nhad ever done before. He began to pray to God, and feared above all\nthat he might die without having done anything good or kind; and he so\nwanted to live, and to live so as to perform a feat of self-sacrifice.\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XXI\n\n\nSuddenly it was as though the sun had shone into his soul. He heard\nRussian being spoken, and also heard the rapid smooth flow of the\nT\xc3\xa9rek, and a few steps farther in front of him saw the brown moving\nsurface of the river, with the dim-coloured wet sand of its banks and\nshallows, the distant steppe, the cordon watch-tower outlined above the\nwater, a saddled and hobbled horse among the brambles, and then the\nmountains opening out before him. The red sun appeared for an instant\nfrom under a cloud and its last rays glittered brightly along the river\nover the reeds, on the watch-tower, and on a group of Cossacks, among\nwhom Luk\xc3\xa1shka\xe2\x80\x99s vigorous figure attracted Ol\xc3\xa9nin\xe2\x80\x99s involuntary\nattention.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin felt that he was again, without any apparent cause, perfectly\nhappy. He had come upon the N\xc3\xadzhni-Prot\xc3\xb3tsk post on the T\xc3\xa9rek, opposite\na pro-Russian Tartar village on the other side of the river. He\naccosted the Cossacks, but not finding as yet any excuse for doing\nanyone a kindness, he entered the hut; nor in the hut did he find any\nsuch opportunity. The Cossacks received him coldly. On entering the mud\nhut he lit a cigarette. The Cossacks paid little attention to him,\nfirst because he was smoking a cigarette, and secondly because they had\nsomething else to divert them that evening. Some hostile Ch\xc3\xa9chens,\nrelatives of the _abrek_ who had been killed, had come from the hills\nwith a scout to ransom the body; and the Cossacks were waiting for\ntheir Commanding Officer\xe2\x80\x99s arrival from the village. The dead man\xe2\x80\x99s\nbrother, tall and well shaped with a short cropped beard which was dyed\nred, despite his very tattered coat and cap was calm and majestic as a\nking. His face was very like that of the dead _abrek_. He did not deign\nto look at anyone, and never once glanced at the dead body, but sitting\non his heels in the shade he spat as he smoked his short pipe, and\noccasionally uttered some few guttural sounds of command, which were\nrespectfully listened to by his companion. He was evidently a brave who\nhad met Russians more than once before in quite other circumstances,\nand nothing about them could astonish or even interest him. Ol\xc3\xa9nin was\nabout to approach the dead body and had begun to look at it when the\nbrother, looking up at him from under his brows with calm contempt,\nsaid something sharply and angrily. The scout hastened to cover the\ndead man\xe2\x80\x99s face with his coat. Ol\xc3\xa9nin was struck by the dignified and\nstern expression of the brave\xe2\x80\x99s face. He began to speak to him, asking\nfrom what village he came, but the Ch\xc3\xa9chen, scarcely giving him a\nglance, spat contemptuously and turned away. Ol\xc3\xa9nin was so surprised at\nthe Ch\xc3\xa9chen not being interested in him that he could only put it down\nto the man\xe2\x80\x99s stupidity or ignorance of Russian; so he turned to the\nscout, who also acted as interpreter. The scout was as ragged as the\nother, but instead of being red-haired he was black-haired, restless,\nwith extremely white gleaming teeth and sparkling black eyes. The scout\nwillingly entered into conversation and asked for a cigarette.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere were five brothers,\xe2\x80\x9d began the scout in his broken Russian.\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is the third brother the Russians have killed, only two are left.\nHe is a brave, a great brave!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, pointing to the Ch\xc3\xa9chen. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen\nthey killed Ahmet Khan (the dead brave) this one was sitting on the\nopposite bank among the reeds. He saw it all. Saw him laid in the skiff\nand brought to the bank. He sat there till the night and wished to kill\nthe old man, but the others would not let him.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka went up to the speaker, and sat down. \xe2\x80\x9cOf what village?\xe2\x80\x9d asked\nhe.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom there in the hills,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the scout, pointing to the misty\nbluish gorge beyond the T\xc3\xa9rek. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you know Suuk-su? It is about eight\nmiles beyond that.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know Gir\xc3\xa9y Khan in Suuk-su?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Luk\xc3\xa1shka, evidently proud of\nthe acquaintance. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is my _kunak_.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is my neighbour,\xe2\x80\x9d answered the scout.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s a trump!\xe2\x80\x9d and Luk\xc3\xa1shka, evidently much interested, began talking\nto the scout in Tartar.\n\nPresently a Cossack captain, with the head of the village, arrived on\nhorseback with a suite of two Cossacks. The captain\xe2\x80\x94one of the new type\nof Cossack officers\xe2\x80\x94wished the Cossacks \xe2\x80\x9cGood health,\xe2\x80\x9d but no one\nshouted in reply, \xe2\x80\x9cHail! Good health to your honour,\xe2\x80\x9d as is customary\nin the Russian Army, and only a few replied with a bow. Some, and among\nthem Luk\xc3\xa1shka, rose and stood erect. The corporal replied that all was\nwell at the outposts. All this seemed ridiculous: it was as if these\nCossacks were playing at being soldiers. But these formalities soon\ngave place to ordinary ways of behaviour, and the captain, who was a\nsmart Cossack just like the others, began speaking fluently in Tartar\nto the interpreter. They filled in some document, gave it to the scout,\nand received from him some money. Then they approached the body.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich of you is Luke Gavr\xc3\xadlov?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the captain.\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka took off his cap and came forward.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have reported your exploit to the Commander. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what will\ncome of it. I have recommended you for a cross; you\xe2\x80\x99re too young to be\nmade a sergeant. Can you read?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what a fine fellow to look at!\xe2\x80\x9d said the captain, again playing\nthe commander. \xe2\x80\x9cPut on your cap. Which of the Gavr\xc3\xadlovs does he come\nof? ... the Broad, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis nephew,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the corporal.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know, I know. Well, lend a hand, help them,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, turning to the\nCossacks.\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka\xe2\x80\x99s face shone with joy and seemed handsomer than usual. He\nmoved away from the corporal, and having put on his cap sat down beside\nOl\xc3\xa9nin.\n\nWhen the body had been carried to the skiff the brother Ch\xc3\xa9chen\ndescended to the bank. The Cossacks involuntarily stepped aside to let\nhim pass. He jumped into the boat and pushed off from the bank with his\npowerful leg, and now, as Ol\xc3\xa9nin noticed, for the first time threw a\nrapid glance at all the Cossacks and then abruptly asked his companion\na question. The latter answered something and pointed to Luk\xc3\xa1shka. The\nCh\xc3\xa9chen looked at him and, turning slowly away, gazed at the opposite\nbank. That look expressed not hatred but cold contempt. He again made\nsome remark.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is he saying?\xe2\x80\x9d Ol\xc3\xa9nin asked of the fidgety scout.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYours kill ours, ours slay yours. It\xe2\x80\x99s always the same,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the\nscout, evidently inventing, and he smiled, showing his white teeth, as\nhe jumped into the skiff.\n\nThe dead man\xe2\x80\x99s brother sat motionless, gazing at the opposite bank. He\nwas so full of hatred and contempt that there was nothing on this side\nof the river that moved his curiosity. The scout, standing up at one\nend of the skiff and dipping his paddle now on one side now on the\nother, steered skilfully while talking incessantly. The skiff became\nsmaller and smaller as it moved obliquely across the stream, the voices\nbecame scarcely audible, and at last, still within sight, they landed\non the opposite bank where their horses stood waiting. There they\nlifted out the corpse and (though the horse shied) laid it across one\nof the saddles, mounted, and rode at a foot-pace along the road past a\nTartar village from which a crowd came out to look at them. The\nCossacks on the Russian side of the river were highly satisfied and\njovial. Laughter and jokes were heard on all sides. The captain and the\nhead of the village entered the mud hut to regale themselves. Luk\xc3\xa1shka,\nvainly striving to impart a sedate expression to his merry face, sat\ndown with his elbows on his knees beside Ol\xc3\xa9nin and whittled away at a\nstick.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you smoke?\xe2\x80\x9d he said with assumed curiosity. \xe2\x80\x9cIs it good?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe evidently spoke because he noticed Ol\xc3\xa9nin felt ill at ease and\nisolated among the Cossacks.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s just a habit,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Ol\xc3\xa9nin. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cH\xe2\x80\x99m, if one of us were to smoke there would be a row! Look there now,\nthe mountains are not far off,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Luk\xc3\xa1shka, \xe2\x80\x9cyet you can\xe2\x80\x99t get\nthere! How will you get back alone? It\xe2\x80\x99s getting dark. I\xe2\x80\x99ll take you,\nif you like. You ask the corporal to give me leave.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a fine fellow!\xe2\x80\x9d thought Ol\xc3\xa9nin, looking at the Cossack\xe2\x80\x99s bright\nface. He remembered Mary\xc3\xa1nka and the kiss he had heard by the gate, and\nhe was sorry for Luk\xc3\xa1shka and his want of culture. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat confusion it\nis,\xe2\x80\x9d he thought. \xe2\x80\x9cA man kills another and is happy and satisfied with\nhimself as if he had done something excellent.
