Can it be that nothing\ntells him that it is not a reason for any rejoicing, and that happiness\nlies not in killing, but in sacrificing oneself?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you had better not meet him again now, mate!\xe2\x80\x9d said one of the\nCossacks who had seen the skiff off, addressing Luk\xc3\xa1shka. \xe2\x80\x9cDid you hear\nhim asking about you?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka raised his head.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy godson?\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka, meaning by that word the dead Ch\xc3\xa9chen.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour godson won\xe2\x80\x99t rise, but the red one is the godson\xe2\x80\x99s brother!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet him thank God that he got off whole himself,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Luk\xc3\xa1shka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you glad about?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ol\xc3\xa9nin. \xe2\x80\x9cSupposing your brother had\nbeen killed; would you be glad?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe Cossack looked at Ol\xc3\xa9nin with laughing eyes. He seemed to have\nunderstood all that Ol\xc3\xa9nin wished to say to him, but to be above such\nconsiderations.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that happens too! Don\xe2\x80\x99t our fellows get killed sometimes?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XXII\n\n\nThe Captain and the head of the village rode away, and Ol\xc3\xa9nin, to\nplease Luk\xc3\xa1shka as well as to avoid going back alone through the dark\nforest, asked the corporal to give Luk\xc3\xa1shka leave, and the corporal did\nso. Ol\xc3\xa9nin thought that Luk\xc3\xa1shka wanted to see Mary\xc3\xa1nka and he was also\nglad of the companionship of such a pleasant-looking and sociable\nCossack. Luk\xc3\xa1shka and Mary\xc3\xa1nka he involuntarily united in his mind, and\nhe found pleasure in thinking about them. \xe2\x80\x9cHe loves Mary\xc3\xa1nka,\xe2\x80\x9d thought\nOl\xc3\xa9nin, \xe2\x80\x9cand I could love her,\xe2\x80\x9d and a new and powerful emotion of\ntenderness overcame him as they walked homewards together through the\ndark forest. Luk\xc3\xa1shka too felt happy; something akin to love made\nitself felt between these two very different young men. Every time they\nglanced at one another they wanted to laugh.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy which gate do you enter?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy the middle one. But I\xe2\x80\x99ll see you as far as the marsh. After that\nyou have nothing to fear.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin laughed.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think I am afraid? Go back, and thank you. I can get on alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s all right! What have I to do? And how can you help being afraid?\nEven we are afraid,\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka to set Ol\xc3\xa9nin\xe2\x80\x99s self-esteem at rest,\nand he laughed too.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen come in with me. We\xe2\x80\x99ll have a talk and a drink and in the morning\nyou can go back.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cCouldn\xe2\x80\x99t I find a place to spend the night?\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Luk\xc3\xa1shka. \xe2\x80\x9cBut\nthe corporal asked me to go back.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI heard you singing last night, and also saw you.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cEvery one...\xe2\x80\x9d and Luke swayed his head.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it true you are getting married?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMother wants me to marry. But I have not got a horse yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAren\xe2\x80\x99t you in the regular service?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh dear no! I\xe2\x80\x99ve only just joined, and have not got a horse yet, and\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know how to get one. That\xe2\x80\x99s why the marriage does not come off.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what would a horse cost?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe were bargaining for one beyond the river the other day and they\nwould not take sixty rubles for it, though it is a Nog\xc3\xa1y horse.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you come and be my drab\xc3\xa1nt?\xe2\x80\x9d (A drab\xc3\xa1nt was a kind of orderly\nattached to an officer when campaigning.) \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll get it arranged and\nwill give you a horse,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ol\xc3\xa9nin suddenly. \xe2\x80\x9cReally now, I have two\nand I don\xe2\x80\x99t want both.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t want it?\xe2\x80\x9d Luk\xc3\xa1shka said, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy should you make me a\npresent? We\xe2\x80\x99ll get on by ourselves by God\xe2\x80\x99s help.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, really! Or don\xe2\x80\x99t you want to be a drab\xc3\xa1nt?\xe2\x80\x9d said Ol\xc3\xa9nin, glad that\nit had entered his head to give a horse to Luk\xc3\xa1shka, though, without\nknowing why, he felt uncomfortable and confused and did not know what\nto say when he tried to speak.\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka was the first to break the silence.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you a house of your own in Russia?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin could not refrain from replying that he had not only one, but\nseveral houses.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cA good house? Bigger than ours?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Luk\xc3\xa1shka good-naturedly.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMuch bigger; ten times as big and three storeys high,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd have you horses such as ours?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have a hundred horses, worth three or four hundred rubles each, but\nthey are not like yours. They are trotters, you know.... But still, I\nlike the horses here best.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, and did you come here of your own free will, or were you sent?\xe2\x80\x9d\nsaid Luk\xc3\xa1shka, laughing at him. \xe2\x80\x9cLook! that\xe2\x80\x99s where you lost your way,\xe2\x80\x9d\nhe added, \xe2\x80\x9cyou should have turned to the right.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI came by my own wish,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Ol\xc3\xa9nin. \xe2\x80\x9cI wanted to see your parts\nand to join some expeditions.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would go on an expedition any day,\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka. \xe2\x80\x9cD\xe2\x80\x99you hear the\njackals howling?\xe2\x80\x9d he added, listening.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, don\xe2\x80\x99t you feel any horror at having killed a man?\xe2\x80\x9d asked\nOl\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s there to be frightened about? But I should like to join an\nexpedition,\xe2\x80\x9d Luk\xc3\xa1shka repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cHow I want to! How I want to!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps we may be going together. Our company is going before the\nholidays, and your \xe2\x80\x98hundred\xe2\x80\x99 too.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what did you want to come here for? You\xe2\x80\x99ve a house and horses and\nserfs. In your place I\xe2\x80\x99d do nothing but make merry! And what is your\nrank?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am a cadet, but have been recommended for a commission.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, if you\xe2\x80\x99re not bragging about your home, if I were you I\xe2\x80\x99d never\nhave left it! Yes, I\xe2\x80\x99d never have gone away anywhere. Do you find it\npleasant living among us?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, very pleasant,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\nIt had grown quite dark before, talking in this way, they approached\nthe village. They were still surrounded by the deep gloom of the\nforest. The wind howled through the tree-tops. The jackals suddenly\nseemed to be crying close beside them, howling, chuckling, and sobbing;\nbut ahead of them in the village the sounds of women\xe2\x80\x99s voices and the\nbarking of dogs could already be heard; the outlines of the huts were\nclearly to be seen; lights gleamed and the air was filled with the\npeculiar smell of _kisyak_ smoke. Ol\xc3\xa9nin felt keenly, that night\nespecially, that here in this village was his home, his family, all his\nhappiness, and that he never had and never would live so happily\nanywhere as he did in this Cossack village. He was so fond of everybody\nand especially of Luk\xc3\xa1shka that night. On reaching home, to Luk\xc3\xa1shka\xe2\x80\x99s\ngreat surprise, Ol\xc3\xa9nin with his own hands led out of the shed a horse\nhe had bought in Gr\xc3\xb3znoe\xe2\x80\x94it was not the one he usually rode but\nanother\xe2\x80\x94not a bad horse though no longer young, and gave it to\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy should you give me a present?\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka, \xe2\x80\x9cI have not yet done\nanything for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally it is nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Ol\xc3\xa9nin. \xe2\x80\x9cTake it, and you will give me\na present, and we\xe2\x80\x99ll go on an expedition against the enemy together.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka became confused.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what d\xe2\x80\x99you mean by it? As if a horse were of little value,\xe2\x80\x9d he\nsaid without looking at the horse.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake it, take it! If you don\xe2\x80\x99t you will offend me. Vany\xc3\xbasha! Take the\ngrey horse to his house.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka took hold of the halter.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell then, thank you! This is something unexpected, undreamt of.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin was as happy as a boy of twelve.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cTie it up here. It\xe2\x80\x99s a good horse. I bought it in Gr\xc3\xb3znoe; it gallops\nsplendidly! Vany\xc3\xbasha, bring us some _chikhir_. Come into the hut.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe wine was brought. Luk\xc3\xa1shka sat down and took the wine-bowl.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod willing I\xe2\x80\x99ll find a way to repay you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, finishing his\nwine. \xe2\x80\x9cHow are you called?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDm\xc3\xadtri Andr\xc3\xa9ich.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, \xe2\x80\x99Mitry Andr\xc3\xa9ich, God bless you. We will be _kunaks_. Now you\nmust come to see us. Though we are not rich people still we can treat a\n_kunak_, and I will tell mother in case you need anything\xe2\x80\x94clotted cream\nor grapes\xe2\x80\x94and if you come to the cordon I\xe2\x80\x99m your servant to go hunting\nor to go across the river, anywhere you like! There now, only the other\nday, what a boar I killed, and I divided it among the Cossacks, but if\nI had only known, I\xe2\x80\x99d have given it to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s all right, thank you! But don\xe2\x80\x99t harness the horse, it has never\nbeen in harness.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy harness the horse? And there is something else I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you if\nyou like,\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka, bending his head. \xe2\x80\x9cI have a _kunak_, Gir\xc3\xa9y\nKhan. He asked me to lie in ambush by the road where they come down\nfrom the mountains. Shall we go together? I\xe2\x80\x99ll not betray you. I\xe2\x80\x99ll be\nyour _murid_.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, we\xe2\x80\x99ll go; we\xe2\x80\x99ll go some day.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka seemed quite to have quieted down and to have understood\nOl\xc3\xa9nin\xe2\x80\x99s attitude towards him. His calmness and the ease of his\nbehaviour surprised Ol\xc3\xa9nin, and he did not even quite like it. They\ntalked long, and it was late when Luk\xc3\xa1shka, not tipsy (he never was\ntipsy) but having drunk a good deal, left Ol\xc3\xa9nin after shaking hands.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin looked out of the window to see what he would do. Luk\xc3\xa1shka went\nout, hanging his head. Then, having led the horse out of the gate, he\nsuddenly shook his head, threw the reins of the halter over its head,\nsprang onto its back like a cat, gave a wild shout, and galloped down\nthe street. Ol\xc3\xa9nin expected that Luk\xc3\xa1shka would go to share his joy\nwith Mary\xc3\xa1nka, but though he did not do so Ol\xc3\xa9nin still felt his soul\nmore at ease than ever before in his life. He was as delighted as a\nboy, and could not refrain from telling Vany\xc3\xbasha not only that he had\ngiven Luk\xc3\xa1shka the horse, but also why he had done it, as well as his\nnew theory of happiness.\n\nVany\xc3\xbasha did not approve of his theory, and announced that \xe2\x80\x9c_l\xe2\x80\x99argent\nil n\xe2\x80\x99y a pas!_\xe2\x80\x9d and that therefore it was all nonsense.\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka rode home, jumped off the horse, and handed it over to his\nmother, telling her to let it out with the communal Cossack herd. He\nhimself had to return to the cordon that same night. His deaf sister\nundertook to take the horse, and explained by signs that when she saw\nthe man who had given the horse, she would bow down at his feet. The\nold woman only shook her head at her son\xe2\x80\x99s story, and decided in her\nown mind that he had stolen it. She therefore told the deaf girl to\ntake it to the herd before daybreak.\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka went back alone to the cordon pondering over Ol\xc3\xa9nin\xe2\x80\x99s action.\nThough he did not consider the horse a good one, yet it was worth at\nleast forty rubles and Luk\xc3\xa1shka was very glad to have the present. But\nwhy it had been given him he could not at all understand, and therefore\nhe did not experience the least feeling of gratitude. On the contrary,\nvague suspicions that the cadet had some evil intentions filled his\nmind. What those intentions were he could not decide, but neither could\nhe admit the idea that a stranger would give him a horse worth forty\nrubles for nothing, just out of kindness; it seemed impossible. Had he\nbeen drunk one might understand it! He might have wished to show off.\nBut the cadet had been sober, and therefore must have wished to bribe\nhim to do something wrong. \xe2\x80\x9cEh, humbug!\xe2\x80\x9d thought Luk\xc3\xa1shka. \xe2\x80\x9cHaven\xe2\x80\x99t I\ngot the horse and we\xe2\x80\x99ll see later on. I\xe2\x80\x99m not a fool myself and we\nshall see who\xe2\x80\x99ll get the better of the other,\xe2\x80\x9d he thought, feeling the\nnecessity of being on his guard, and therefore arousing in himself\nunfriendly feelings towards Ol\xc3\xa9nin. He told no one how he had got the\nhorse. To some he said he had bought it, to others he replied\nevasively. However, the truth soon got about in the village, and\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka\xe2\x80\x99s mother and Mary\xc3\xa1nka, as well as Elias Vas\xc3\xadlich and other\nCossacks, when they heard of Ol\xc3\xa9nin\xe2\x80\x99s unnecessary gift, were perplexed,\nand began to be on their guard against the cadet. But despite their\nfears his action aroused in them a great respect for his simplicity and\nwealth.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you heard,\xe2\x80\x9d said one, \xe2\x80\x9cthat the cadet quartered on Elias Vas\xc3\xadlich\nhas thrown a fifty-ruble horse at Luk\xc3\xa1shka? He\xe2\x80\x99s rich! ...\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I heard of it,\xe2\x80\x9d replied another profoundly, \xe2\x80\x9che must have done\nhim some great service. We shall see what will come of this cadet. Eh!\nwhat luck that Snatcher has!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThose cadets are crafty, awfully crafty,\xe2\x80\x9d said a third. \xe2\x80\x9cSee if he\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t go setting fire to a building, or doing something!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XXIII\n\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin\xe2\x80\x99s life went on with monotonous regularity. He had little\nintercourse with the commanding officers or with his equals. The\nposition of a rich cadet in the Caucasus was peculiarly advantageous in\nthis respect. He was not sent out to work, or for training. As a reward\nfor going on an expedition he was recommended for a commission, and\nmeanwhile he was left in peace. The officers regarded him as an\naristocrat and behaved towards him with dignity. Cardplaying and the\nofficers\xe2\x80\x99 carousals accompanied by the soldier-singers, of which he had\nhad experience when he was with the detachment, did not seem to him\nattractive, and he also avoided the society and life of the officers in\nthe village. The life of officers stationed in a Cossack village has\nlong had its own definite form. Just as every cadet or officer when in\na fort regularly drinks porter, plays cards, and discusses the rewards\ngiven for taking part in the expeditions, so in the Cossack villages he\nregularly drinks _chikhir_ with his hosts, treats the girls to\nsweet-meats and honey, dangles after the Cossack women, and falls in\nlove, and occasionally marries there. Ol\xc3\xa9nin always took his own path\nand had an unconscious objection to the beaten tracks. And here, too,\nhe did not follow the ruts of a Caucasian officer\xe2\x80\x99s life.\n\nIt came quite naturally to him to wake up at daybreak. After drinking\ntea and admiring from his porch the mountains, the morning, and\nMary\xc3\xa1nka, he would put on a tattered ox-hide coat, sandals of soaked\nraw hide, buckle on a dagger, take a gun, put cigarettes and some lunch\nin a little bag, call his dog, and soon after five o\xe2\x80\x99clock would start\nfor the forest beyond the village. Towards seven in the evening he\nwould return tired and hungry with five or six pheasants hanging from\nhis belt (sometimes with some other animal) and with his bag of food\nand cigarettes untouched. If the thoughts in his head had lain like the\nlunch and cigarettes in the bag, one might have seen that during all\nthose fourteen hours not a single thought had moved in it. He returned\nmorally fresh, strong, and perfectly happy, and he could not tell what\nhe had been thinking about all the time. Were they ideas, memories, or\ndreams that had been flitting through his mind? They were frequently\nall three. He would rouse himself and ask what he had been thinking\nabout; and would see himself as a Cossack working in a vineyard with\nhis Cossack wife, or an _abrek_ in the mountains, or a boar running\naway from himself. And all the time he kept peering and watching for a\npheasant, a boar, or a deer.\n\nIn the evening Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka would be sure to be sitting with him.\nVany\xc3\xbasha would bring a jug of _chikhir_, and they would converse\nquietly, drink, and separate to go quite contentedly to bed. The next\nday he would again go shooting, again be healthily weary, again they\nwould sit conversing and drink their fill, and again be happy.\nSometimes on a holiday or day of rest Ol\xc3\xa9nin spent the whole day at\nhome. Then his chief occupation was watching Mary\xc3\xa1nka, whose every\nmovement, without realizing it himself, he followed greedily from his\nwindow or his porch. He regarded Mary\xc3\xa1nka and loved her (so he thought)\njust as he loved the beauty of the mountains and the sky, and he had no\nthought of entering into any relations with her. It seemed to him that\nbetween him and her such relations as there were between her and the\nCossack Luk\xc3\xa1shka could not exist, and still less such as often existed\nbetween rich officers and other Cossack girls. It seemed to him that if\nhe tried to do as his fellow officers did, he would exchange his\ncomplete enjoyment of contemplation for an abyss of suffering,\ndisillusionment, and remorse. Besides, he had already achieved a\ntriumph of self-sacrifice in connexion with her which had given him\ngreat pleasure, and above all he was in a way afraid of Mary\xc3\xa1nka and\nwould not for anything have ventured to utter a word of love to her\nlightly.\n\nOnce during the summer, when Ol\xc3\xa9nin had not gone out shooting but was\nsitting at home, quite unexpectedly a Moscow acquaintance, a very young\nman whom he had met in society, came in.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, _mon cher_, my dear fellow, how glad I was when I heard that you\nwere here!\xe2\x80\x9d he began in his Moscow French, and he went on intermingling\nFrench words in his remarks. \xe2\x80\x9cThey said, \xe2\x80\x98Ol\xc3\xa9nin\xe2\x80\x99. What Ol\xc3\xa9nin? and I\nwas so pleased.... Fancy fate bringing us together here! Well, and how\nare you? How? Why?\xe2\x80\x9d and Prince Bel\xc3\xa9tski told his whole story: how he\nhad temporarily entered the regiment, how the Commander-in-Chief had\noffered to take him as an adjutant, and how he would take up the post\nafter this campaign although personally he felt quite indifferent about\nit.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cLiving here in this hole one must at least make a career\xe2\x80\x94get a\ncross\xe2\x80\x94or a rank\xe2\x80\x94be transferred to the Guards. That is quite\nindispensable, not for myself but for the sake of my relations and\nfriends. The prince received me very well; he is a very decent fellow,\xe2\x80\x9d\nsaid Bel\xc3\xa9tski, and went on unceasingly. \xe2\x80\x9cI have been recommended for\nthe St. Anna Cross for the expedition. Now I shall stay here a bit\nuntil we start on the campaign. It\xe2\x80\x99s capital here. What women! Well,\nand how are you getting on? I was told by our captain, St\xc3\xa1rtsev you\nknow, a kind-hearted stupid creature.... Well, he said you were living\nlike an awful savage, seeing no one! I quite understand you don\xe2\x80\x99t want\nto be mixed up with the set of officers we have here. I am so glad now\nyou and I will be able to see something of one another. I have put up\nat the Cossack corporal\xe2\x80\x99s house. There is such a girl there. \xc3\x9astenka! I\ntell you she\xe2\x80\x99s just charming.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAnd more and more French and Russian words came pouring forth from that\nworld which Ol\xc3\xa9nin thought he had left for ever. The general opinion\nabout Bel\xc3\xa9tski was that he was a nice, good-natured fellow. Perhaps he\nreally was; but in spite of his pretty, good-natured face, Ol\xc3\xa9nin\nthought him extremely unpleasant. He seemed just to exhale that\nfilthiness which Ol\xc3\xa9nin had forsworn. What vexed him most was that he\ncould not\xe2\x80\x94had not the strength\xe2\x80\x94abruptly to repulse this man who came\nfrom that world: as if that old world he used to belong to had an\nirresistible claim on him. Ol\xc3\xa9nin felt angry with Bel\xc3\xa9tski and with\nhimself, yet against his wish he introduced French phrases into his own\nconversation, was interested in the Commander-in-Chief and in their\nMoscow acquaintances, and because in this Cossack village he and\nBel\xc3\xa9tski both spoke French, he spoke contemptuously of their fellow\nofficers and of the Cossacks, and was friendly with Bel\xc3\xa9tski, promising\nto visit him and inviting him to drop in to see him. Ol\xc3\xa9nin however did\nnot himself go to see Bel\xc3\xa9tski. Vany\xc3\xbasha for his part approved of\nBel\xc3\xa9tski, remarking that he was a real gentleman.\n\nBel\xc3\xa9tski at once adopted the customary life of a rich officer in a\nCossack village. Before Ol\xc3\xa9nin\xe2\x80\x99s eyes, in one month he came to be like\nan old resident of the village; he made the old men drunk, arranged\nevening parties, and himself went to parties arranged by the\ngirls\xe2\x80\x94bragged of his conquests, and even got so far that, for some\nunknown reason, the women and girls began calling him grandad, and the\nCossacks, to whom a man who loved wine and women was clearly\nunderstandable, got used to him and even liked him better than they did\nOl\xc3\xa9nin, who was a puzzle to them.\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XXIV\n\n\nIt was five in the morning. Vany\xc3\xbasha was in the porch heating the\nsamovar, and using the leg of a long boot instead of bellows. Ol\xc3\xa9nin\nhad already ridden off to bathe in the T\xc3\xa9rek. (He had recently invented\na new amusement: to swim his horse in the river.) His landlady was in\nher outhouse, and the dense smoke of the kindling fire rose from the\nchimney. The girl was milking the buffalo cow in the shed. \xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t keep\nquiet, the damned thing!\xe2\x80\x9d came her impatient voice, followed by the\nrhythmical sound of milking.\n\nFrom the street in front of the house horses\xe2\x80\x99 hoofs were heard\nclattering briskly, and Ol\xc3\xa9nin, riding bareback on a handsome dark-grey\nhorse which was still wet and shining, rode up to the gate. Mary\xc3\xa1nka\xe2\x80\x99s\nhandsome head, tied round with a red kerchief, appeared from the shed\nand again disappeared. Ol\xc3\xa9nin was wearing a red silk shirt, a white\nCircassian coat girdled with a strap which carried a dagger, and a tall\ncap. He sat his well-fed wet horse with a slightly conscious elegance\nand, holding his gun at his back, stooped to open the gate. His hair\nwas still wet, and his face shone with youth and health. He thought\nhimself handsome, agile, and like a brave; but he was mistaken. To any\nexperienced Caucasian he was still only a soldier. When he noticed that\nthe girl had put out her head he stooped with particular smartness,\nthrew open the gate and, tightening the reins, swished his whip and\nentered the yard. \xe2\x80\x9cIs tea ready, Vany\xc3\xbasha?\xe2\x80\x9d he cried gaily, not looking\nat the door of the shed. He felt with pleasure how his fine horse,\npressing down its flanks, pulling at the bridle and with every muscle\nquivering and with each foot ready to leap over the fence, pranced on\nthe hard clay of the yard. _\xe2\x80\x9cC\xe2\x80\x99est pr\xc3\xaat_,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Vany\xc3\xbasha. Ol\xc3\xa9nin\nfelt as if Mary\xc3\xa1nka\xe2\x80\x99s beautiful head was still looking out of the shed\nbut he did not turn to look at her. As he jumped down from his horse he\nmade an awkward movement and caught his gun against the porch, and\nturned a frightened look towards the shed, where there was no one to be\nseen and whence the sound of milking could still be heard.\n\nSoon after he had entered the hut he came out again and sat down with\nhis pipe and a book on the side of the porch which was not yet exposed\nto the rays of the sun. He meant not to go anywhere before dinner that\nday, and to write some long-postponed letters; but somehow he felt\ndisinclined to leave his place in the porch, and he was as reluctant to\ngo back into the hut as if it had been a prison. The housewife had\nheated her oven, and the girl, having driven the cattle, had come back\nand was collecting _kisyak_ and heaping it up along the fence. Ol\xc3\xa9nin\nwent on reading, but did not understand a word of what was written in\nthe book that lay open before him. He kept lifting his eyes from it and\nlooking at the powerful young woman who was moving about. Whether she\nstepped into the moist morning shadow thrown by the house, or went out\ninto the middle of the yard lit up by the joyous young light, so that\nthe whole of her stately figure in its bright coloured garment gleamed\nin the sunshine and cast a black shadow\xe2\x80\x94he always feared to lose any\none of her movements. It delighted him to see how freely and gracefully\nher figure bent: into what folds her only garment, a pink smock, draped\nitself on her bosom and along her shapely legs; how she drew herself up\nand her tight-drawn smock showed the outline of her heaving bosom, how\nthe soles of her narrow feet in her worn red slippers rested on the\nground without altering their shape; how her strong arms with the\nsleeves rolled up, exerting the muscles, used the spade almost as if in\nanger, and how her deep dark eyes sometimes glanced at him. Though the\ndelicate brows frowned, yet her eyes expressed pleasure and a knowledge\nof her own beauty.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, Ol\xc3\xa9nin, have you been up long?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bel\xc3\xa9tski as he entered the\nyard dressed in the coat of a Caucasian officer.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, Bel\xc3\xa9tski,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Ol\xc3\xa9nin, holding out his hand. \xe2\x80\x9cHow is it you\nare out so early?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had to. I was driven out; we are having a ball tonight. Mary\xc3\xa1nka, of\ncourse you\xe2\x80\x99ll come to \xc3\x9astenka\xe2\x80\x99s?\xe2\x80\x9d he added, turning to the girl.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin felt surprised that Bel\xc3\xa9tski could address this woman so easily.\nBut Mary\xc3\xa1nka, as though she had not heard him, bent her head, and\nthrowing the spade across her shoulder went with her firm masculine\ntread towards the outhouse.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s shy, the wench is shy,\xe2\x80\x9d Bel\xc3\xa9tski called after her. \xe2\x80\x9cShy of you,\xe2\x80\x9d\nhe added as, smiling gaily, he ran up the steps of the porch.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow is it you are having a ball and have been driven out?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s at \xc3\x9astenka\xe2\x80\x99s, at my landlady\xe2\x80\x99s, that the ball is, and you two are\ninvited. A ball consists of a pie and a gathering of girls.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat should we do there?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBel\xc3\xa9tski smiled knowingly and winked, jerking his head in the direction\nof the outhouse into which Mary\xc3\xa1nka had disappeared.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin shrugged his shoulders and blushed.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, really you are a strange fellow!\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome now, don\xe2\x80\x99t pretend\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin frowned, and Bel\xc3\xa9tski noticing this smiled insinuatingly. \xe2\x80\x9cOh,\ncome, what do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cliving in the same house\xe2\x80\x94and such a\nfine girl, a splendid girl, a perfect beauty\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWonderfully beautiful! I never saw such a woman before,\xe2\x80\x9d replied\nOl\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell then?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bel\xc3\xa9tski, quite unable to understand the situation.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt may be strange,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Ol\xc3\xa9nin, \xe2\x80\x9cbut why should I not say what is\ntrue? Since I have lived here women don\xe2\x80\x99t seem to exist for me. And it\nis so good, really! Now what can there be in common between us and\nwomen like these? Er\xc3\xb3shka\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s a different matter! He and I have a\npassion in common\xe2\x80\x94sport.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere now! In common! And what have I in common with Am\xc3\xa1lia Iv\xc3\xa1novna?\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s the same thing! You may say they\xe2\x80\x99re not very clean\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s another\nmatter... _\xc3\x80 la guerre, comme \xc3\xa0 la guerre!_...\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I have never known any Am\xc3\xa1lia Iv\xc3\xa1novas, and have never known how\nto behave with women of that sort,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Ol\xc3\xa9nin. \xe2\x80\x9cOne cannot respect\nthem, but these I do respect.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell go on respecting them! Who wants to prevent you?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin did not reply. He evidently wanted to complete what he had begun\nto say. It was very near his heart.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know I am an exception...\xe2\x80\x9d He was visibly confused. \xe2\x80\x9cBut my life has\nso shaped itself that I not only see no necessity to renounce my rules,\nbut I could not live here, let alone live as happily as I am doing,\nwere I to live as you do. Therefore I look for something quite\ndifferent from what you look for.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBel\xc3\xa9tski raised his eyebrows incredulously. \xe2\x80\x9cAnyhow, come to me this\nevening; Mary\xc3\xa1nka will be there and I will make you acquainted. Do\ncome, please! If you feel dull you can go away. Will you come?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would come, but to speak frankly I am afraid of being seriously\ncarried away.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, oh, oh!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Bel\xc3\xa9tski. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly come, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll see that you\naren\xe2\x80\x99t. Will you? On your word?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would come, but really I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand what we shall do; what\npart we shall play!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease, I beg of you. You will come?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, perhaps I\xe2\x80\x99ll come,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally now! Charming women such as one sees nowhere else, and to live\nlike a monk! What an idea! Why spoil your life and not make use of what\nis at hand? Have you heard that our company is ordered to\nVozdv\xc3\xadzhensk?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHardly. I was told the 8th Company would be sent there,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. I have had a letter from the adjutant there. He writes that the\nPrince himself will take part in the campaign. I am very glad I shall\nsee something of him. I\xe2\x80\x99m beginning to get tired of this place.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hear we shall start on a raid soon.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have not heard of it; but I have heard that Krinov\xc3\xadtsin has received\nthe Order of St. Anna for a raid. He expected a lieutenancy,\xe2\x80\x9d said\nBel\xc3\xa9tski laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cHe was let in! He has set off for headquarters.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nIt was growing dusk and Ol\xc3\xa9nin began thinking about the party. The\ninvitation he had received worried him. He felt inclined to go, but\nwhat might take place there seemed strange, absurd, and even rather\nalarming. He knew that neither Cossack men nor older women, nor anyone\nbesides the girls, were to be there. What was going to happen? How was\nhe to behave? What would they talk about? What connexion was there\nbetween him and those wild Cossack girls? Bel\xc3\xa9tski had told him of such\ncurious, cynical, and yet rigid relations. It seemed strange to think\nthat he would be there in the same hut with Mary\xc3\xa1nka and perhaps might\nhave to talk to her. It seemed to him impossible when he remembered her\nmajestic bearing. But Bel\xc3\xa9tski spoke of it as if it were all perfectly\nsimple. \xe2\x80\x9cIs it possible that Bel\xc3\xa9tski will treat Mary\xc3\xa1nka in the same\nway? That is interesting,\xe2\x80\x9d thought he. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, better not go. It\xe2\x80\x99s all so\nhorrid, so vulgar, and above all\xe2\x80\x94it leads to nothing!\xe2\x80\x9d But again he was\nworried by the question of what would take place; and besides he felt\nas if bound by a promise. He went out without having made up his mind\none way or the other, but he walked as far as Bel\xc3\xa9tski\xe2\x80\x99s, and went in\nthere.\n\nThe hut in which Bel\xc3\xa9tski lived was like Ol\xc3\xa9nin\xe2\x80\x99s. It was raised nearly\nfive feet from the ground on wooden piles, and had two rooms. In the\nfirst (which Ol\xc3\xa9nin entered by the steep flight of steps) feather beds,\nrugs, blankets, and cushions were tastefully and handsomely arranged,\nCossack fashion, along the main wall. On the side wall hung brass\nbasins and weapons, while on the floor, under a bench, lay watermelons\nand pumpkins. In the second room there was a big brick oven, a table,\nand sectarian icons. It was here that Bel\xc3\xa9tski was quartered, with his\ncamp-bed and his pack and trunks. His weapons hung on the wall with a\nlittle rug behind them, and on the table were his toilet appliances and\nsome portraits. A silk dressing-gown had been thrown on the bench.\nBel\xc3\xa9tski himself, clean and good-looking, lay on the bed in his\nunderclothing, reading _Les Trois Mousquetaires_.\n\nHe jumped up.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere, you see how I have arranged things. Fine! Well, it\xe2\x80\x99s good that\nyou have come. They are working furiously. Do you know what the pie is\nmade of? Dough with a stuffing of pork and grapes. But that\xe2\x80\x99s not the\npoint. You just look at the commotion out there!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAnd really, on looking out of the window they saw an unusual bustle\ngoing on in the hut. Girls ran in and out, now for one thing and now\nfor another.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill it soon be ready?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bel\xc3\xa9tski.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery soon! Why? Is Grandad hungry?\xe2\x80\x9d and from the hut came the sound of\nringing laughter.\n\n\xc3\x9astenka, plump, small, rosy, and pretty, with her sleeves turned up,\nran into Bel\xc3\xa9tski\xe2\x80\x99s hut to fetch some plates.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGet away or I shall smash the plates!\xe2\x80\x9d she squeaked, escaping from\nBel\xc3\xa9tski. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99d better come and help,\xe2\x80\x9d she shouted to Ol\xc3\xa9nin,\nlaughing. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd don\xe2\x80\x99t forget to get some refreshments for the girls.\xe2\x80\x9d\n(\xe2\x80\x9cRefreshments\xe2\x80\x9d meaning spicebread and sweets.)\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd has Mary\xc3\xa1nka come?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course! She brought some dough.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bel\xc3\xa9tski, \xe2\x80\x9cif one were to dress \xc3\x9astenka up and\nclean and polish her up a bit, she\xe2\x80\x99d be better than all our beauties.\nHave you ever seen that Cossack woman who married a colonel; she was\ncharming! B\xc3\xb3rsheva? What dignity! Where do they get it...\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have not seen B\xc3\xb3rsheva, but I think nothing could be better than the\ncostume they wear here.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, I\xe2\x80\x99m first-rate at fitting into any kind of life,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bel\xc3\xa9tski\nwith a sigh of pleasure. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll go and see what they are up to.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe threw his dressing-gown over his shoulders and ran out, shouting,\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you look after the \xe2\x80\x98refreshments\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin sent Bel\xc3\xa9tski\xe2\x80\x99s orderly to buy spice-bread and honey; but it\nsuddenly seemed to him so disgusting to give money (as if he were\nbribing someone) that he gave no definite reply to the orderly\xe2\x80\x99s\nquestion: \xe2\x80\x9cHow much spice-bread with peppermint, and how much with\nhoney?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust as you please.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall I spend all the money,\xe2\x80\x9d asked the old soldier impressively. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\npeppermint is dearer. It\xe2\x80\x99s sixteen kopeks.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, yes, spend it all,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Ol\xc3\xa9nin and sat down by the window,\nsurprised that his heart was thumping as if he were preparing himself\nfor something serious and wicked.\n\nHe heard screaming and shrieking in the girls\xe2\x80\x99 hut when Bel\xc3\xa9tski went\nthere, and a few moments later saw how he jumped out and ran down the\nsteps, accompanied by shrieks, bustle, and laughter.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cTurned out,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\n\nA little later \xc3\x9astenka entered and solemnly invited her visitors to\ncome in: announcing that all was ready.\n\nWhen they came into the room they saw that everything was really ready.\n\xc3\x9astenka was rearranging the cushions along the wall. On the table,\nwhich was covered by a disproportionately small cloth, was a decanter\nof _chikhir_ and some dried fish. The room smelt of dough and grapes.\nSome half dozen girls in smart tunics, with their heads not covered as\nusual with kerchiefs, were huddled together in a corner behind the\noven, whispering, giggling, and spluttering with laughter.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI humbly beg you to do honour to my patron saint,\xe2\x80\x9d said \xc3\x9astenka,\ninviting her guests to the table.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin noticed Mary\xc3\xa1nka among the group of girls, who without exception\nwere all handsome, and he felt vexed and hurt that he met her in such\nvulgar and awkward circumstances. He felt stupid and awkward, and made\nup his mind to do what Bel\xc3\xa9tski did. Bel\xc3\xa9tski stepped to the table\nsomewhat solemnly yet with confidence and ease, drank a glass of wine\nto \xc3\x9astenka\xe2\x80\x99s health, and invited the others to do the same. \xc3\x9astenka\nannounced that girls don\xe2\x80\x99t drink.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe might with a little honey,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed a voice from among the group\nof girls.\n\nThe orderly, who had just returned with the honey and spice-cakes, was\ncalled in. He looked askance (whether with envy or with contempt) at\nthe gentlemen, who in his opinion were on the spree; and carefully and\nconscientiously handed over to them a piece of honeycomb and the cakes\nwrapped up in a piece of greyish paper, and began explaining\ncircumstantially all about the price and the change, but Bel\xc3\xa9tski sent\nhim away.\n\nHaving mixed honey with wine in the glasses, and having lavishly\nscattered the three pounds of spice-cakes on the table, Bel\xc3\xa9tski\ndragged the girls from their corners by force, made them sit down at\nthe table, and began distributing the cakes among them. Ol\xc3\xa9nin\ninvoluntarily noticed how Mary\xc3\xa1nka\xe2\x80\x99s sunburnt but small hand closed on\ntwo round peppermint nuts and one brown one, and that she did not know\nwhat to do with them. The conversation was halting and constrained, in\nspite of \xc3\x9astenka\xe2\x80\x99s and Bel\xc3\xa9tski\xe2\x80\x99s free and easy manner and their wish\nto enliven the company. Ol\xc3\xa9nin faltered, and tried to think of\nsomething to say, feeling that he was exciting curiosity and perhaps\nprovoking ridicule and infecting the others with his shyness. He\nblushed, and it seemed to him that Mary\xc3\xa1nka in particular was feeling\nuncomfortable. \xe2\x80\x9cMost likely they are expecting us to give them some\nmoney,\xe2\x80\x9d thought he. \xe2\x80\x9cHow are we to do it? And how can we manage\nquickest to give it and get away?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XXV\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow is it you don\xe2\x80\x99t know your own lodger?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bel\xc3\xa9tski, addressing\nMary\xc3\xa1nka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow is one to know him if he never comes to see us?\xe2\x80\x9d answered\nMary\xc3\xa1nka, with a look at Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin felt frightened, he did not know of what. He flushed and, hardly\nknowing what he was saying, remarked: \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m afraid of your mother. She\ngave me such a scolding the first time I went in.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nMary\xc3\xa1nka burst out laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd so you were frightened?\xe2\x80\x9d she said,\nand glanced at him and turned away.\n\nIt was the first time Ol\xc3\xa9nin had seen the whole of her beautiful face.\nTill then he had seen her with her kerchief covering her to the eyes.\nIt was not for nothing that she was reckoned the beauty of the village.\n\xc3\x9astenka was a pretty girl, small, plump, rosy, with merry brown eyes,\nand red lips which were perpetually smiling and chattering. Mary\xc3\xa1nka on\nthe contrary was certainly not pretty but beautiful. Her features might\nhave been considered too masculine and almost harsh had it not been for\nher tall stately figure, her powerful chest and shoulders, and\nespecially the severe yet tender expression of her long dark eyes which\nwere darkly shadowed beneath their black brows, and for the gentle\nexpression of her mouth and smile. She rarely smiled, but her smile was\nalways striking. She seemed to radiate virginal strength and health.\nAll the girls were good-looking, but they themselves and Bel\xc3\xa9tski, and\nthe orderly when he brought in the spice-cakes, all involuntarily gazed\nat Mary\xc3\xa1nka, and anyone addressing the girls was sure to address her.\nShe seemed a proud and happy queen among them.\n\nBel\xc3\xa9tski, trying to keep up the spirit of the party, chattered\nincessantly, made the girls hand round _chikhir_, fooled about with\nthem, and kept making improper remarks in French about Mary\xc3\xa1nka\xe2\x80\x99s\nbeauty to Ol\xc3\xa9nin, calling her \xe2\x80\x9cyours\xe2\x80\x9d (_la v\xc3\xb4tre_), and advising him to\nbehave as he did himself. Ol\xc3\xa9nin felt more and more uncomfortable. He\nwas devising an excuse to get out and run away when Bel\xc3\xa9tski announced\nthat \xc3\x9astenka, whose saint\xe2\x80\x99s day it was, must offer _chikhir_ to\neverybody with a kiss. She consented on condition that they should put\nmoney on her plate, as is the custom at weddings.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat fiend brought me to this disgusting feast?\xe2\x80\x9d thought Ol\xc3\xa9nin,\nrising to go away.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere are you off to?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll fetch some tobacco,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, meaning to escape, but Bel\xc3\xa9tski\nseized his hand.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have some money,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to him in French.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne can\xe2\x80\x99t go away, one has to pay here,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Ol\xc3\xa9nin bitterly,\nvexed at his own awkwardness. \xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t I really behave like Bel\xc3\xa9tski? I\nought not to have come, but once I am here I must not spoil their fun.\nI must drink like a Cossack,\xe2\x80\x9d and taking the wooden bowl (holding about\neight tumblers) he almost filled it with _chikhir_ and drank it almost\nall. The girls looked at him, surprised and almost frightened, as he\ndrank. It seemed to them strange and not right. \xc3\x9astenka brought them\nanother glass each, and kissed them both. \xe2\x80\x9cThere girls, now we\xe2\x80\x99ll have\nsome fun,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, clinking on the plate the four rubles the men had\nput there.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin no longer felt awkward, but became talkative.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Mary\xc3\xa1nka, it\xe2\x80\x99s your turn to offer us wine and a kiss,\xe2\x80\x9d said\nBel\xc3\xa9tski, seizing her hand.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I\xe2\x80\x99ll give you such a kiss!\xe2\x80\x9d she said playfully, preparing to\nstrike at him.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne can kiss Grandad without payment,\xe2\x80\x9d said another girl.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s a sensible girl,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bel\xc3\xa9tski, kissing the struggling girl.\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, you must offer it,\xe2\x80\x9d he insisted, addressing Mary\xc3\xa1nka. \xe2\x80\x9cOffer a\nglass to your lodger.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAnd taking her by the hand he led her to the bench and sat her down\nbeside Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a beauty,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, turning her head to see it in profile.\n\nMary\xc3\xa1nka did not resist but proudly smiling turned her long eyes\ntowards Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cA beautiful girl,\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Bel\xc3\xa9tski.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, see what a beauty I am,\xe2\x80\x9d Mary\xc3\xa1nka\xe2\x80\x99s look seemed to endorse.\nWithout considering what he was doing Ol\xc3\xa9nin embraced Mary\xc3\xa1nka and was\ngoing to kiss her, but she suddenly extricated herself, upsetting\nBel\xc3\xa9tski and pushing the top off the table, and sprang away towards the\noven. There was much shouting and laughter. Then Bel\xc3\xa9tski whispered\nsomething to the girls and suddenly they all ran out into the passage\nand locked the door behind them.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy did you kiss Bel\xc3\xa9tski and won\xe2\x80\x99t kiss me?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, just so. I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to, that\xe2\x80\x99s all!\xe2\x80\x9d she answered, pouting and\nfrowning. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s Grandad,\xe2\x80\x9d she added with a smile. She went to the door\nand began to bang at it. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy have you locked the door, you devils?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, let them be there and us here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ol\xc3\xa9nin, drawing closer to\nher.\n\nShe frowned, and sternly pushed him away with her hand. And again she\nappeared so majestically handsome to Ol\xc3\xa9nin that he came to his senses\nand felt ashamed of what he was doing. He went to the door and began\npulling at it himself.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBel\xc3\xa9tski! Open the door! What a stupid joke!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nMary\xc3\xa1nka again gave a bright happy laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cAh, you\xe2\x80\x99re afraid of me?\xe2\x80\x9d\nshe said.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you know you\xe2\x80\x99re as cross as your mother.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpend more of your time with Er\xc3\xb3shka; that will make the girls love\nyou!\xe2\x80\x9d And she smiled, looking straight and close into his eyes.\n\nHe did not know what to reply. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd if I were to come to see you\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d he\nlet fall.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat would be a different matter,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied, tossing her head.\n\nAt that moment Bel\xc3\xa9tski pushed the door open, and Mary\xc3\xa1nka sprang away\nfrom Ol\xc3\xa9nin and in doing so her thigh struck his leg.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s all nonsense what I have been thinking about\xe2\x80\x94love and\nself-sacrifice and Luk\xc3\xa1shka. Happiness is the one thing. He who is\nhappy is right,\xe2\x80\x9d flashed through Ol\xc3\xa9nin\xe2\x80\x99s mind, and with a strength\nunexpected to himself he seized and kissed the beautiful Mary\xc3\xa1nka on\nher temple and her cheek. Mary\xc3\xa1nka was not angry, but only burst into a\nloud laugh and ran out to the other girls.\n\nThat was the end of the party. \xc3\x9astenka\xe2\x80\x99s mother, returned from her\nwork, gave all the girls a scolding, and turned them all out.\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XXVI\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Ol\xc3\xa9nin, as he walked home. \xe2\x80\x9cI need only slacken the\nreins a bit and I might fall desperately in love with this Cossack\ngirl.\xe2\x80\x9d He went to bed with these thoughts, but expected it all to blow\nover and that he would continue to live as before.\n\nBut the old life did not return. His relations to Mary\xc3\xa1nka were\nchanged. The wall that had separated them was broken down. Ol\xc3\xa9nin now\ngreeted her every time they met.\n\nThe master of the house having returned to collect the rent, on hearing\nof Ol\xc3\xa9nin\xe2\x80\x99s wealth and generosity invited him to his hut. The old woman\nreceived him kindly, and from the day of the party onwards Ol\xc3\xa9nin often\nwent in of an evening and sat with them till late at night. He seemed\nto be living in the village just as he used to, but within him\neverything had changed. He spent his days in the forest, and towards\neight o\xe2\x80\x99clock, when it began to grow dusk, he would go to see his\nhosts, alone or with Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka. They grew so used to him that they\nwere surprised when he stayed away. He paid well for his wine and was a\nquiet fellow. Vany\xc3\xbasha would bring him his tea and he would sit down in\na corner near the oven. The old woman did not mind him but went on with\nher work, and over their tea or their _chikhir_ they talked about\nCossack affairs, about the neighbours, or about Russia: Ol\xc3\xa9nin relating\nand the others inquiring. Sometimes he brought a book and read to\nhimself. Mary\xc3\xa1nka crouched like a wild goat with her feet drawn up\nunder her, sometimes on the top of the oven, sometimes in a dark\ncorner. She did not take part in the conversations, but Ol\xc3\xa9nin saw her\neyes and face and heard her moving or cracking sunflower seeds, and he\nfelt that she listened with her whole being when he spoke, and was\naware of his presence while he silently read to himself. Sometimes he\nthought her eyes were fixed on him, and meeting their radiance he\ninvoluntarily became silent and gazed at her. Then she would instantly\nhide her face and he would pretend to be deep in conversation with the\nold woman, while he listened all the time to her breathing and to her\nevery movement and waited for her to look at him again. In the presence\nof others she was generally bright and friendly with him, but when they\nwere alone together she was shy and rough. Sometimes he came in before\nMary\xc3\xa1nka had returned home. Suddenly he would hear her firm footsteps\nand catch a glimmer of her blue cotton smock at the open door. Then she\nwould step into the middle of the hut, catch sight of him, and her eyes\nwould give a scarcely perceptible kindly smile, and he would feel happy\nand frightened.\n\nHe neither sought for nor wished for anything from her, but every day\nher presence became more and more necessary to him.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin had entered into the life of the Cossack village so fully that\nhis past seemed quite foreign to him. As to the future, especially a\nfuture outside the world in which he was now living, it did not\ninterest him at all. When he received letters from home, from relatives\nand friends, he was offended by the evident distress with which they\nregarded him as a lost man, while he in his village considered those as\nlost who did not live as he was living. He felt sure he would never\nrepent of having broken away from his former surroundings and of having\nsettled down in this village to such a solitary and original life. When\nout on expeditions, and when quartered at one of the forts, he felt\nhappy too; but it was here, from under Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka\xe2\x80\x99s wing, from the\nforest and from his hut at the end of the village, and especially when\nhe thought of Mary\xc3\xa1nka and Luk\xc3\xa1shka, that he seemed to see the\nfalseness of his former life. That falseness used to rouse his\nindignation even before, but now it seemed inexpressibly vile and\nridiculous. Here he felt freer and freer every day and more and more of\na man. The Caucasus now appeared entirely different to what his\nimagination had painted it. He had found nothing at all like his\ndreams, nor like the descriptions of the Caucasus he had heard and\nread. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are none of all those chestnut steeds, precipices, Amalet\nBeks, heroes or villains,\xe2\x80\x9d thought he. \xe2\x80\x9cThe people live as nature\nlives: they die, are born, unite, and more are born\xe2\x80\x94they fight, eat and\ndrink, rejoice and die, without any restrictions but those that nature\nimposes on sun and grass, on animal and tree. They have no other laws.\xe2\x80\x9d\nTherefore these people, compared to himself, appeared to him beautiful,\nstrong, and free, and the sight of them made him feel ashamed and sorry\nfor himself. Often it seriously occurred to him to throw up everything,\nto get registered as a Cossack, to buy a hut and cattle and marry a\nCossack woman (only not Mary\xc3\xa1nka, whom he conceded to Luk\xc3\xa1shka), and to\nlive with Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka and go shooting and fishing with him, and go\nwith the Cossacks on their expeditions. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy ever don\xe2\x80\x99t I do it? What\nam I waiting for?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked himself, and he egged himself on and shamed\nhimself. \xe2\x80\x9cAm I afraid of doing what I hold to be reasonable and right?\nIs the wish to be a simple Cossack, to live close to nature, not to\ninjure anyone but even to do good to others, more stupid than my former\ndreams, such as those of becoming a minister of state or a colonel?\xe2\x80\x9d\nbut a voice seemed to say that he should wait, and not take any\ndecision. He was held back by a dim consciousness that he could not\nlive altogether like Er\xc3\xb3shka and Luk\xc3\xa1shka because he had a different\nidea of happiness\xe2\x80\x94he was held back by the thought that happiness lies\nin self-sacrifice. What he had done for Luk\xc3\xa1shka continued to give him\njoy. He kept looking for occasions to sacrifice himself for others, but\ndid not meet with them. Sometimes he forgot this newly discovered\nrecipe for happiness and considered himself capable of identifying his\nlife with Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka\xe2\x80\x99s, but then he quickly bethought himself and\npromptly clutched at the idea of conscious self-sacrifice, and from\nthat basis looked calmly and proudly at all men and at their happiness.\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XXVII\n\n\nJust before the vintage Luk\xc3\xa1shka came on horseback to see Ol\xc3\xa9nin. He\nlooked more dashing than ever.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell? Are you getting married?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ol\xc3\xa9nin, greeting him merrily.\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka gave no direct reply.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere, I\xe2\x80\x99ve exchanged your horse across the river. This _is_ a horse!\nA Kabard\xc3\xa1 horse from the Lov stud. I know horses.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThey examined the new horse and made him caracole about the yard. The\nhorse really was an exceptionally fine one, a broad and long gelding,\nwith glossy coat, thick silky tail, and the soft fine mane and crest of\na thoroughbred. He was so well fed that \xe2\x80\x9cyou might go to sleep on his\nback\xe2\x80\x9d as Luk\xc3\xa1shka expressed it. His hoofs, eyes, teeth, were\nexquisitely shaped and sharply outlined, as one only finds them in very\npure-bred horses. Ol\xc3\xa9nin could not help admiring the horse, he had not\nyet met with such a beauty in the Caucasus.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how it goes!\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka, patting its neck. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat a step! And\nso clever\xe2\x80\x94he simply runs after his master.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you have to add much to make the exchange?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did not count it,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Luk\xc3\xa1shka with a smile. \xe2\x80\x9cI got him from a\n_kunak_.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cA wonderfully beautiful horse! What would you take for it?\xe2\x80\x9d asked\nOl\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been offered a hundred and fifty rubles for it, but I\xe2\x80\x99ll give\nit you for nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka, merrily. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly say the word and\nit\xe2\x80\x99s yours. I\xe2\x80\x99ll unsaddle it and you may take it. Only give me some\nsort of a horse for my duties.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, on no account.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell then, here is a dagger I\xe2\x80\x99ve brought you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka,\nunfastening his girdle and taking out one of the two daggers which hung\nfrom it. \xe2\x80\x9cI got it from across the river.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, thank you!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd mother has promised to bring you some grapes herself.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s quite unnecessary. We\xe2\x80\x99ll balance up some day. You see I don\xe2\x80\x99t\noffer you any money for the dagger!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow could you? We are _kunaks_. It\xe2\x80\x99s just the same as when Gir\xc3\xa9y Khan\nacross the river took me into his home and said, \xe2\x80\x98Choose what you\nlike!\xe2\x80\x99 So I took this sword. It\xe2\x80\x99s our custom.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThey went into the hut and had a drink.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you staying here awhile?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I have come to say good-bye. They are sending me from the cordon\nto a company beyond the T\xc3\xa9rek. I am going tonight with my comrade\nNaz\xc3\xa1rka.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd when is the wedding to be?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall be coming back for the betrothal, and then I shall return to\nthe company again,\xe2\x80\x9d Luk\xc3\xa1shka replied reluctantly.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat, and see nothing of your betrothed?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust so\xe2\x80\x94what is the good of looking at her? When you go on campaign\nask in our company for Luk\xc3\xa1shka the Broad. But what a lot of boars\nthere are in our parts! I\xe2\x80\x99ve killed two. I\xe2\x80\x99ll take you.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, good-bye! Christ save you.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka mounted his horse, and without calling on Mary\xc3\xa1nka, rode\ncaracoling down the street, where Naz\xc3\xa1rka was already awaiting him.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, shan\xe2\x80\x99t we call round?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Naz\xc3\xa1rka, winking in the direction\nof Y\xc3\xa1mka\xe2\x80\x99s house.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a good one!\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka. \xe2\x80\x9cHere, take my horse to her and if\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t come soon give him some hay. I shall reach the company by the\nmorning anyway.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHasn\xe2\x80\x99t the cadet given you anything more?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am thankful to have paid him back with a dagger\xe2\x80\x94he was going to ask\nfor the horse,\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka, dismounting and handing over the horse\nto Naz\xc3\xa1rka.\n\nHe darted into the yard past Ol\xc3\xa9nin\xe2\x80\x99s very window, and came up to the\nwindow of the cornet\xe2\x80\x99s hut. It was already quite dark. Mary\xc3\xa1nka,\nwearing only her smock, was combing her hair preparing for bed.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s I\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d whispered the Cossack.\n\nMary\xc3\xa1nka\xe2\x80\x99s look was severely indifferent, but her face suddenly\nbrightened up when she heard her name. She opened the window and leant\nout, frightened and joyous.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x94what do you want?\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOpen!\xe2\x80\x9d uttered Luk\xc3\xa1shka. \xe2\x80\x9cLet me in for a minute. I am so sick of\nwaiting! It\xe2\x80\x99s awful!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe took hold of her head through the window and kissed her.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally, do open!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you talk nonsense? I\xe2\x80\x99ve told you I won\xe2\x80\x99t! Have you come for\nlong?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe did not answer but went on kissing her, and she did not ask again.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere, through the window one can\xe2\x80\x99t even hug you properly,\xe2\x80\x9d said\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMary\xc3\xa1nka dear!\xe2\x80\x9d came the voice of her mother, \xe2\x80\x9cwho is that with you?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka took off his cap, which might have been seen, and crouched\ndown by the window.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo, be quick!\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Mary\xc3\xa1nka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cLuk\xc3\xa1shka called round,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered; \xe2\x80\x9che was asking for Daddy.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell then send him here!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s gone; said he was in a hurry.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nIn fact, Luk\xc3\xa1shka, stooping, as with big strides he passed under the\nwindows, ran out through the yard and towards Y\xc3\xa1mka\xe2\x80\x99s house unseen by\nanyone but Ol\xc3\xa9nin. After drinking two bowls of _chikhir_ he and Naz\xc3\xa1rka\nrode away to the outpost. The night was warm, dark, and calm. They rode\nin silence, only the footfall of their horses was heard. Luk\xc3\xa1shka\nstarted a song about the Cossack, Ming\xc3\xa1l, but stopped before he had\nfinished the first verse, and after a pause, turning to Naz\xc3\xa1rka, said:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, she wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t let me in!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh?\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Naz\xc3\xa1rka. \xe2\x80\x9cI knew she wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t. D\xe2\x80\x99you know what Y\xc3\xa1mka\ntold me? The cadet has begun going to their house. Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka brags\nthat he got a gun from the cadet for getting him Mary\xc3\xa1nka.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe lies, the old devil!\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka, angrily. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s not such a\ngirl. If he does not look out I\xe2\x80\x99ll wallop that old devil\xe2\x80\x99s sides,\xe2\x80\x9d and\nhe began his favourite song:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom the village of Izm\xc3\xa1ylov,\nFrom the master\xe2\x80\x99s favourite garden,\nOnce escaped a keen-eyed falcon.\nSoon after him a huntsman came a-riding,\nAnd he beckoned to the falcon that had strayed,\nBut the bright-eyed bird thus answered:\n\xe2\x80\x98In gold cage you could not keep me,\nOn your hand you could not hold me,\nSo now I fly to blue seas far away.\nThere a white swan I will kill,\nOf sweet swan-flesh have my fill.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XXVIII\n\n\nThe betrothal was taking place in the cornet\xe2\x80\x99s hut. Luk\xc3\xa1shka had\nreturned to the village, but had not been to see Ol\xc3\xa9nin, and Ol\xc3\xa9nin had\nnot gone to the betrothal though he had been invited. He was sad as he\nhad never been since he settled in this Cossack village. He had seen\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka earlier in the evening and was worried by the question why\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka was so cold towards him. Ol\xc3\xa9nin shut himself up in his hut and\nbegan writing in his diary as follows:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMany things have I pondered over lately and much have I changed,\xe2\x80\x9d\nwrote he, \xe2\x80\x9cand I have come back to the copybook maxim: The one way to\nbe happy is to love, to love self-denyingly, to love everybody and\neverything; to spread a web of love on all sides and to take all who\ncome into it. In this way I caught Vany\xc3\xbasha, Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka, Luk\xc3\xa1shka,\nand Mary\xc3\xa1nka.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAs Ol\xc3\xa9nin was finishing this sentence Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka entered the room.\n\nEr\xc3\xb3shka was in the happiest frame of mind. A few evenings before this,\nOl\xc3\xa9nin had gone to see him and had found him with a proud and happy\nface deftly skinning the carcass of a boar with a small knife in the\nyard. The dogs (Lyam his pet among them) were lying close by watching\nwhat he was doing and gently wagging their tails. The little boys were\nrespectfully looking at him through the fence and not even teasing him\nas was their wont. His women neighbours, who were as a rule not too\ngracious towards him, greeted him and brought him, one a jug of\n_chikhir_, another some clotted cream, and a third a little flour. The\nnext day Er\xc3\xb3shka sat in his store-room all covered with blood, and\ndistributed pounds of boar-flesh, taking in payment money from some and\nwine from others. His face clearly expressed, \xe2\x80\x9cGod has sent me luck. I\nhave killed a boar, so now I am wanted.\xe2\x80\x9d Consequently, he naturally\nbegan to drink, and had gone on for four days never leaving the\nvillage. Besides which he had had something to drink at the betrothal.\n\nHe came to Ol\xc3\xa9nin quite drunk: his face red, his beard tangled, but\nwearing a new _beshmet_ trimmed with gold braid; and he brought with\nhim a _balal\xc3\xa1yka_ which he had obtained beyond the river. He had long\npromised Ol\xc3\xa9nin this treat, and felt in the mood for it, so that he was\nsorry to find Ol\xc3\xa9nin writing.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWrite on, write on, my lad,\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered, as if he thought that a\nspirit sat between him and the paper and must not be frightened away,\nand he softly and silently sat down on the floor. When Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka\nwas drunk his favourite position was on the floor. Ol\xc3\xa9nin looked round,\nordered some wine to be brought, and continued to write. Er\xc3\xb3shka found\nit dull to drink by himself and he wished to talk.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve been to the betrothal at the cornet\xe2\x80\x99s. But there! They\xe2\x80\x99re\nshwine!\xe2\x80\x94Don\xe2\x80\x99t want them!\xe2\x80\x94Have come to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd where did you get your _balal\xc3\xa1yka?_\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ol\xc3\xa9nin, still writing.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve been beyond the river and got it there, brother mine,\xe2\x80\x9d he\nanswered, also very quietly. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m a master at it. Tartar or Cossack,\nsquire or soldiers\xe2\x80\x99 songs, any kind you please.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin looked at him again, smiled, and went on writing.\n\nThat smile emboldened the old man.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, leave off, my lad, leave off!\xe2\x80\x9d he said with sudden firmness.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, perhaps I will.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, people have injured you but leave them alone, spit at them!\nCome, what\xe2\x80\x99s the use of writing and writing, what\xe2\x80\x99s the good?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAnd he tried to mimic Ol\xc3\xa9nin by tapping the floor with his thick\nfingers, and then twisted his big face to express contempt.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the good of writing quibbles. Better have a spree and show\nyou\xe2\x80\x99re a man!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nNo other conception of writing found place in his head except that of\nlegal chicanery.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin burst out laughing and so did Er\xc3\xb3shka. Then, jumping up from the\nfloor, the latter began to show off his skill on the _balal\xc3\xa1yka_ and to\nsing Tartar songs.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy write, my good fellow! You\xe2\x80\x99d better listen to what I\xe2\x80\x99ll sing to\nyou. When you\xe2\x80\x99re dead you won\xe2\x80\x99t hear any more songs. Make merry now!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nFirst he sang a song of his own composing accompanied by a dance:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, dee, dee, dee, dee, dee, dim,\nSay where did they last see him?\nIn a booth, at the fair,\nHe was selling pins, there.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\nThen he sang a song he had learnt from his former sergeant-major:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDeep I fell in love on Monday,\nTuesday nothing did but sigh,\nWednesday I popped the question,\nThursday waited her reply.\nFriday, late, it came at last,\nThen all hope for me was past!\nSaturday my life to take\nI determined like a man,\nBut for my salvation\xe2\x80\x99s sake\nSunday morning changed my plan!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\nThen he sang again:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh dee, dee, dee, dee, dee, dim,\nSay where did they last see him?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\nAnd after that, winking, twitching his shoulders, and footing it to the\ntune, he sang:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will kiss you and embrace,\nRibbons red twine round you;\nAnd I\xe2\x80\x99ll call you little Grace.\nOh, you little Grace now do\nTell me, do you love me true?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\nAnd he became so excited that with a sudden dashing movement he started\ndancing around the room accompanying himself the while.\n\nSongs like \xe2\x80\x9cDee, dee, dee\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cgentlemen\xe2\x80\x99s songs\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he sang for Ol\xc3\xa9nin\xe2\x80\x99s\nbenefit, but after drinking three more tumblers of _chikhir_ he\nremembered old times and began singing real Cossack and Tartar songs.\nIn the midst of one of his favourite songs his voice suddenly trembled\nand he ceased singing, and only continued strumming on the _balal\xc3\xa1yka_.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, my dear friend!\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\n\nThe peculiar sound of his voice made Ol\xc3\xa9nin look round.\n\nThe old man was weeping. Tears stood in his eyes and one tear was\nrunning down his cheek.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are gone, my young days, and will never come back!\xe2\x80\x9d he said,\nblubbering and halting. \xe2\x80\x9cDrink, why don\xe2\x80\x99t you drink!\xe2\x80\x9d he suddenly\nshouted with a deafening roar, without wiping away his tears.\n\nThere was one Tartar song that specially moved him. It had few words,\nbut its charm lay in the sad refrain. \xe2\x80\x9cAy day, dalalay!\xe2\x80\x9d Er\xc3\xb3shka\ntranslated the words of the song: \xe2\x80\x9cA youth drove his sheep from the\n_aoul_ to the mountains: the Russians came and burnt the _aoul_, they\nkilled all the men and took all the women into bondage. The youth\nreturned from the mountains. Where the _aoul_ had stood was an empty\nspace; his mother not there, nor his brothers, nor his house; one tree\nalone was left standing. The youth sat beneath the tree and wept.\n\xe2\x80\x98Alone like thee, alone am I left,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d and Er\xc3\xb3shka began singing: \xe2\x80\x9cAy\nday, dalalay!\xe2\x80\x9d and the old man repeated several times this wailing,\nheart-rending refrain.\n\nWhen he had finished the refrain Er\xc3\xb3shka suddenly seized a gun that\nhung on the wall, rushed hurriedly out into the yard and fired off both\nbarrels into the air. Then again he began, more dolefully, his \xe2\x80\x9cAy day,\ndalalay\xe2\x80\x94ah, ah,\xe2\x80\x9d and ceased.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin followed him into the porch and looked up into the starry sky in\nthe direction where the shots had flashed. In the cornet\xe2\x80\x99s house there\nwere lights and the sound of voices. In the yard girls were crowding\nround the porch and the windows, and running backwards and forwards\nbetween the hut and the outhouse. Some Cossacks rushed out of the hut\nand could not refrain from shouting, re-echoing the refrain of Daddy\nEr\xc3\xb3shka\xe2\x80\x99s song and his shots.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy are you not at the betrothal?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind them! Never mind them!\xe2\x80\x9d muttered the old man, who had\nevidently been offended by something there. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t like them, I don\xe2\x80\x99t.\nOh, those people! Come back into the hut! Let them make merry by\nthemselves and we\xe2\x80\x99ll make merry by ourselves.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin went in.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd Luk\xc3\xa1shka, is he happy? Won\xe2\x80\x99t he come to see me?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat, Luk\xc3\xa1shka? They\xe2\x80\x99ve lied to him and said I am getting his girl for\nyou,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered the old man. \xe2\x80\x9cBut what\xe2\x80\x99s the girl? She will be ours if\nwe want her. Give enough money\xe2\x80\x94and she\xe2\x80\x99s ours. I\xe2\x80\x99ll fix it up for you.\nReally!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Daddy, money can do nothing if she does not love me. You\xe2\x80\x99d better\nnot talk like that!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are not loved, you and I. We are forlorn,\xe2\x80\x9d said Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka\nsuddenly, and again he began to cry.\n\nListening to the old man\xe2\x80\x99s talk Ol\xc3\xa9nin had drunk more than usual. \xe2\x80\x9cSo\nnow my Luk\xc3\xa1shka is happy,\xe2\x80\x9d thought he; yet he felt sad. The old man had\ndrunk so much that evening that he fell down on the floor and Vany\xc3\xbasha\nhad to call soldiers in to help, and spat as they dragged the old man\nout. He was so angry with the old man for his bad behaviour that he did\nnot even say a single French word.\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XXIX\n\n\nIt was August. For days the sky had been cloudless, the sun scorched\nunbearably and from early morning the warm wind raised a whirl of hot\nsand from the sand-drifts and from the road, and bore it in the air\nthrough the reeds, the trees, and the village. The grass and the leaves\non the trees were covered with dust, the roads and dried-up salt\nmarshes were baked so hard that they rang when trodden on. The water\nhad long since subsided in the T\xc3\xa9rek and rapidly vanished and dried up\nin the ditches. The slimy banks of the pond near the village were\ntrodden bare by the cattle and all day long you could hear the\nsplashing of water and the shouting of girls and boys bathing. The\nsand-drifts and the reeds were already drying up in the steppes, and\nthe cattle, lowing, ran into the fields in the day-time. The boars\nmigrated into the distant reed-beds and to the hills beyond the T\xc3\xa9rek.\nMosquitoes and gnats swarmed in thick clouds over the low lands and\nvillages. The snow-peaks were hidden in grey mist. The air was rarefied\nand smoky. It was said that _abreks_ had crossed the now shallow river\nand were prowling on this side of it. Every night the sun set in a\nglowing red blaze. It was the busiest time of the year. The villagers\nall swarmed in the melon-fields and the vineyards. The vineyards\nthickly overgrown with twining verdure lay in cool, deep shade.\nEverywhere between the broad translucent leaves, ripe, heavy, black\nclusters peeped out. Along the dusty road from the vineyards the\ncreaking carts moved slowly, heaped up with black grapes. Clusters of\nthem, crushed by the wheels, lay in the dirt. Boys and girls in smocks\nstained with grape-juice, with grapes in their hands and mouths, ran\nafter their mothers. On the road you continually came across tattered\nlabourers with baskets of grapes on their powerful shoulders; Cossack\nmaidens, veiled with kerchiefs to their eyes, drove bullocks harnessed\nto carts laden high with grapes. Soldiers who happened to meet these\ncarts asked for grapes, and the maidens, clambering up without stopping\ntheir carts, would take an armful of grapes and drop them into the\nskirts of the soldiers\xe2\x80\x99 coats. In some homesteads they had already\nbegun pressing the grapes; and the smell of the emptied skins filled\nthe air. One saw the blood-red troughs in the pent-houses in the yards\nand Nog\xc3\xa1y labourers with their trousers rolled up and their legs\nstained with the juice. Grunting pigs gorged themselves with the empty\nskins and rolled about in them. The flat roofs of the outhouses were\nall spread over with the dark amber clusters drying in the sun. Daws\nand magpies crowded round the roofs, picking the seeds and fluttering\nfrom one place to another.\n\nThe fruits of the year\xe2\x80\x99s labour were being merrily gathered in, and\nthis year the fruit was unusually fine and plentiful.\n\nIn the shady green vineyards amid a sea of vines, laughter, songs,\nmerriment, and the voices of women were to be heard on all sides, and\nglimpses of their bright-coloured garments could be seen.\n\nJust at noon Mary\xc3\xa1nka was sitting in their vineyard in the shade of a\npeach-tree, getting out the family dinner from under an unharnessed\ncart. Opposite her, on a spread-out horse-cloth, sat the cornet (who\nhad returned from the school) washing his hands by pouring water on\nthem from a little jug. Her little brother, who had just come straight\nout of the pond, stood wiping his face with his wide sleeves, and gazed\nanxiously at his sister and his mother and breathed deeply, awaiting\nhis dinner. The old mother, with her sleeves rolled up over her strong\nsunburnt arms, was arranging grapes, dried fish, and clotted cream on a\nlittle low, circular Tartar table. The cornet wiped his hands, took off\nhis cap, crossed himself, and moved nearer to the table. The boy seized\nthe jug and eagerly began to drink. The mother and daughter crossed\ntheir legs under them and sat down by the table. Even in the shade it\nwas intolerably hot. The air above the vineyard smelt unpleasant: the\nstrong warm wind passing amid the branches brought no coolness, but\nonly monotonously bent the tops of the pear, peach, and mulberry trees\nwith which the vineyard was sprinkled. The cornet, having crossed\nhimself once more, took a little jug of _chikhir_ that stood behind him\ncovered with a vine-leaf, and having had a drink from the mouth of the\njug passed it to the old woman. He had nothing on over his shirt, which\nwas unfastened at the neck and showed his shaggy muscular chest. His\nfine-featured cunning face looked cheerful; neither in his attitude nor\nin his words was his usual wiliness to be seen; he was cheerful and\nnatural.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall we finish the bit beyond the shed tonight?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, wiping his\nwet beard.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll manage it,\xe2\x80\x9d replied his wife, \xe2\x80\x9cif only the weather does not\nhinder us. The D\xc3\xabmkins have not half finished yet,\xe2\x80\x9d she added. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly\n\xc3\x9astenka is at work there, wearing herself out.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat can you expect of them?\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man proudly.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere, have a drink, Mary\xc3\xa1nka dear!\xe2\x80\x9d said the old woman, passing the\njug to the girl. \xe2\x80\x9cGod willing we\xe2\x80\x99ll have enough to pay for the wedding\nfeast,\xe2\x80\x9d she added.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s not yet awhile,\xe2\x80\x9d said the cornet with a slight frown.\n\nThe girl hung her head.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t we mention it?\xe2\x80\x9d said the old woman. \xe2\x80\x9cThe affair is\nsettled, and the time is drawing near too.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t make plans beforehand,\xe2\x80\x9d said the cornet. \xe2\x80\x9cNow we have the\nharvest to get in.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you seen Luk\xc3\xa1shka\xe2\x80\x99s new horse?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the old woman. \xe2\x80\x9cThat which\nDm\xc3\xadtri Andr\xc3\xa9ich Ol\xc3\xa9nin gave him is gone \xe2\x80\x94 he\xe2\x80\x99s exchanged it.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I have not; but I spoke with the servant today,\xe2\x80\x9d said the cornet,\n\xe2\x80\x9cand he said his master has again received a thousand rubles.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cRolling in riches, in short,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old woman.\n\nThe whole family felt cheerful and contented.\n\nThe work was progressing successfully. The grapes were more abundant\nand finer than they had expected. After dinner Mary\xc3\xa1nka threw some\ngrass to the oxen, folded her _beshmet_ for a pillow, and lay down\nunder the wagon on the juicy down-trodden grass. She had on only a red\nkerchief over her head and a faded blue print smock, yet she felt\nunbearably hot. Her face was burning, and she did not know where to put\nher feet, her eyes were moist with sleepiness and weariness, her lips\nparted involuntarily, and her chest heaved heavily and deeply.\n\nThe busy time of year had begun a fortnight ago and the continuous\nheavy labour had filled the girl\xe2\x80\x99s life. At dawn she jumped up, washed\nher face with cold water, wrapped herself in a shawl, and ran out\nbarefoot to see to the cattle. Then she hurriedly put on her shoes and\nher _beshmet_ and, taking a small bundle of bread, she harnessed the\nbullocks and drove away to the vineyards for the whole day.
