There she\ncut the grapes and carried the baskets with only an hour\xe2\x80\x99s interval for\nrest, and in the evening she returned to the village, bright and not\ntired, dragging the bullocks by a rope or driving them with a long\nstick. After attending to the cattle, she took some sunflower seeds in\nthe wide sleeve of her smock and went to the corner of the street to\ncrack them and have some fun with the other girls. But as soon as it\nwas dusk she returned home, and after having supper with her parents\nand her brother in the dark outhouse, she went into the hut, healthy\nand free from care, and climbed onto the oven, where half drowsing she\nlistened to their lodger\xe2\x80\x99s conversation. As soon as he went away she\nwould throw herself down on her bed and sleep soundly and quietly till\nmorning. And so it went on day after day. She had not seen Luk\xc3\xa1shka\nsince the day of their betrothal, but calmly awaited the wedding. She\nhad got used to their lodger and felt his intent looks with pleasure.\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XXX\n\n\nAlthough there was no escape from the heat and the mosquitoes swarmed\nin the cool shadow of the wagons, and her little brother tossing about\nbeside her kept pushing her, Mary\xc3\xa1nka having drawn her kerchief over\nher head was just falling asleep, when suddenly their neighbour \xc3\x9astenka\ncame running towards her and, diving under the wagon, lay down beside\nher.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSleep, girls, sleep!\xe2\x80\x9d said \xc3\x9astenka, making herself comfortable under\nthe wagon. \xe2\x80\x9cWait a bit,\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cthis won\xe2\x80\x99t do!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nShe jumped up, plucked some green branches, and stuck them through the\nwheels on both sides of the wagon and hung her _beshmet_ over them.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me in,\xe2\x80\x9d she shouted to the little boy as she again crept under the\nwagon. \xe2\x80\x9cIs this the place for a Cossack\xe2\x80\x94with the girls? Go away!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nWhen alone under the wagon with her friend, \xc3\x9astenka suddenly put both\nher arms round her, and clinging close to her began kissing her cheeks\nand neck.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDarling, sweetheart,\xe2\x80\x9d she kept repeating, between bursts of shrill,\nclear laughter.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you\xe2\x80\x99ve learnt it from Grandad,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary\xc3\xa1nka, struggling. \xe2\x80\x9cStop\nit!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAnd they both broke into such peals of laughter that Mary\xc3\xa1nka\xe2\x80\x99s mother\nshouted to them to be quiet.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you jealous?\xe2\x80\x9d asked \xc3\x9astenka in a whisper.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat humbug! Let me sleep. What have you come for?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBut \xc3\x9astenka kept on, \xe2\x80\x9cI say! But I wanted to tell you such a thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nMary\xc3\xa1nka raised herself on her elbow and arranged the kerchief which\nhad slipped off.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know something about your lodger!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s nothing to know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary\xc3\xa1nka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you rogue of a girl!\xe2\x80\x9d said \xc3\x9astenka, nudging her with her elbow and\nlaughing. \xe2\x80\x9cWon\xe2\x80\x99t tell anything. Does he come to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe does. What of that?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary\xc3\xa1nka with a sudden blush.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I\xe2\x80\x99m a simple lass. I tell everybody. Why should I pretend?\xe2\x80\x9d said\n\xc3\x9astenka, and her bright rosy face suddenly became pensive. \xe2\x80\x9cWhom do I\nhurt? I love him, that\xe2\x80\x99s all about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGrandad, do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, yes!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the sin?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, Mary\xc3\xa1nka! When is one to have a good time if not while one\xe2\x80\x99s still\nfree? When I marry a Cossack I shall bear children and shall have\ncares. There now, when you get married to Luk\xc3\xa1shka not even a thought\nof joy will enter your head: children will come, and work!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell? Some who are married live happily. It makes no difference!\xe2\x80\x9d\nMary\xc3\xa1nka replied quietly.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo tell me just this once what has passed between you and Luk\xc3\xa1shka?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat has passed? A match was proposed. Father put it off for a year,\nbut now it\xe2\x80\x99s been settled and they\xe2\x80\x99ll marry us in autumn.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what did he say to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nMary\xc3\xa1nka smiled.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat should he say? He said he loved me. He kept asking me to come to\nthe vineyards with him.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust see what pitch! But you didn\xe2\x80\x99t go, did you? And what a dare-devil\nhe has become: the first among the braves. He makes merry out there in\nthe army too! The other day our K\xc3\xadrka came home; he says: \xe2\x80\x98What a horse\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka\xe2\x80\x99s got in exchange!\xe2\x80\x99 But all the same I expect he frets after\nyou. And what else did he say?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMust you know everything?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary\xc3\xa1nka laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cOne night he came\nto my window tipsy, and asked me to let him in.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you didn\xe2\x80\x99t let him?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet him, indeed! Once I have said a thing I keep to it firm as a\nrock,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mary\xc3\xa1nka seriously.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cA fine fellow! If he wanted her, no girl would refuse him.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, let him go to the others,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Mary\xc3\xa1nka proudly.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t pity him?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do pity him, but I\xe2\x80\x99ll have no nonsense. It is wrong.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xc3\x9astenka suddenly dropped her head on her friend\xe2\x80\x99s breast, seized hold\nof her, and shook with smothered laughter. \xe2\x80\x9cYou silly fool!\xe2\x80\x9d she\nexclaimed, quite out of breath. \xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t want to be happy,\xe2\x80\x9d and she\nbegan tickling Mary\xc3\xa1nka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, leave off!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary\xc3\xa1nka, screaming and laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve crushed\nLaz\xc3\xbatka.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHark at those young devils! Quite frisky! Not tired yet!\xe2\x80\x9d came the old\nwoman\xe2\x80\x99s sleepy voice from the wagon.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t want happiness,\xe2\x80\x9d repeated \xc3\x9astenka in a whisper, insistently.\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you are lucky, that you are! How they love you! You are so crusty,\nand yet they love you. Ah, if I were in your place I\xe2\x80\x99d soon turn the\nlodger\xe2\x80\x99s head! I noticed him when you were at our house. He was ready\nto eat you with his eyes. What things Grandad has given me! And yours\nthey say is the richest of the Russians. His orderly says they have\nserfs of their own.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nMary\xc3\xa1nka raised herself, and after thinking a moment, smiled.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know what he once told me: the lodger I mean?\xe2\x80\x9d she said, biting\na bit of grass. \xe2\x80\x9cHe said, \xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99d like to be Luk\xc3\xa1shka the Cossack, or your\nbrother Laz\xc3\xbatka\xe2\x80\x94.\xe2\x80\x99 What do you think he meant?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, just chattering what came into his head,\xe2\x80\x9d answered \xc3\x9astenka. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\ndoes mine not say! Just as if he was possessed!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nMary\xc3\xa1nka dropped her hand on her folded _beshmet_, threw her arm over\n\xc3\x9astenka\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder, and shut her eyes.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe wanted to come and work in the vineyard today: father invited him,\xe2\x80\x9d\nshe said, and after a short silence she fell asleep.\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XXXI\n\n\nThe sun had come out from behind the pear-tree that had shaded the\nwagon, and even through the branches that \xc3\x9astenka had fixed up it\nscorched the faces of the sleeping girls. Mary\xc3\xa1nka woke up and began\narranging the kerchief on her head. Looking about her, beyond the\npear-tree she noticed their lodger, who with his gun on his shoulder\nstood talking to her father. She nudged \xc3\x9astenka and smilingly pointed\nhim out to her.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI went yesterday and didn\xe2\x80\x99t find a single one,\xe2\x80\x9d Ol\xc3\xa9nin was saying as\nhe looked about uneasily, not seeing Mary\xc3\xa1nka through the branches.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you should go out there in that direction, go right as by\ncompasses, there in a disused vineyard denominated as the Waste, hares\nare always to be found,\xe2\x80\x9d said the cornet, having at once changed his\nmanner of speech.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cA fine thing to go looking for hares in these busy times! You had\nbetter come and help us, and do some work with the girls,\xe2\x80\x9d the old\nwoman said merrily. \xe2\x80\x9cNow then, girls, up with you!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried.\n\nMary\xc3\xa1nka and \xc3\x9astenka under the cart were whispering and could hardly\nrestrain their laughter.\n\nSince it had become known that Ol\xc3\xa9nin had given a horse worth fifty\nrubles to Luk\xc3\xa1shka, his hosts had become more amiable and the cornet in\nparticular saw with pleasure his daughter\xe2\x80\x99s growing intimacy with\nOl\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I don\xe2\x80\x99t know how to do the work,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Ol\xc3\xa9nin, trying not to\nlook through the green branches under the wagon where he had now\nnoticed Mary\xc3\xa1nka\xe2\x80\x99s blue smock and red kerchief.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, I\xe2\x80\x99ll give you some peaches,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old woman.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s only according to the ancient Cossack hospitality. It\xe2\x80\x99s her old\nwoman\xe2\x80\x99s silliness,\xe2\x80\x9d said the cornet, explaining and apparently\ncorrecting his wife\xe2\x80\x99s words. \xe2\x80\x9cIn Russia, I expect, it\xe2\x80\x99s not so much\npeaches as pineapple jam and preserves you have been accustomed to eat\nat your pleasure.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you say hares are to be found in the disused vineyard?\xe2\x80\x9d asked\nOl\xc3\xa9nin. \xe2\x80\x9cI will go there,\xe2\x80\x9d and throwing a hasty glance through the\ngreen branches he raised his cap and disappeared between the regular\nrows of green vines.\n\nThe sun had already sunk behind the fence of the vineyards, and its\nbroken rays glittered through the translucent leaves when Ol\xc3\xa9nin\nreturned to his host\xe2\x80\x99s vineyard. The wind was falling and a cool\nfreshness was beginning to spread around. By some instinct Ol\xc3\xa9nin\nrecognized from afar Mary\xc3\xa1nka\xe2\x80\x99s blue smock among the rows of vine, and,\npicking grapes on his way, he approached her. His highly excited dog\nalso now and then seized a low-hanging cluster of grapes in his\nslobbering mouth. Mary\xc3\xa1nka, her face flushed, her sleeves rolled up,\nand her kerchief down below her chin, was rapidly cutting the heavy\nclusters and laying them in a basket. Without letting go of the vine\nshe had hold of, she stopped to smile pleasantly at him and resumed her\nwork. Ol\xc3\xa9nin drew near and threw his gun behind his back to have his\nhands free. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere are your people? May God aid you! Are you alone?\xe2\x80\x9d he\nmeant to say but did not say, and only raised his cap in silence.\n\nHe was ill at ease alone with Mary\xc3\xa1nka, but as if purposely to torment\nhimself he went up to her.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll be shooting the women with your gun like that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary\xc3\xa1nka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I shan\xe2\x80\x99t shoot them.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThey were both silent.\n\nThen after a pause she said: \xe2\x80\x9cYou should help me.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nHe took out his knife and began silently to cut off the clusters. He\nreached from under the leaves low down a thick bunch weighing about\nthree pounds, the grapes of which grew so close that they flattened\neach other for want of space. He showed it to Mary\xc3\xa1nka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMust they all be cut? Isn\xe2\x80\x99t this one too green?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive it here.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nTheir hands touched. Ol\xc3\xa9nin took her hand, and she looked at him\nsmiling.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you going to be married soon?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\n\nShe did not answer, but turned away with a stern look.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you love Luk\xc3\xa1shka?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s that to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI envy him!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery likely!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo really. You are so beautiful!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAnd he suddenly felt terribly ashamed of having said it, so commonplace\ndid the words seem to him. He flushed, lost control of himself, and\nseized both her hands.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhatever I am, I\xe2\x80\x99m not for you. Why do you make fun of me?\xe2\x80\x9d replied\nMary\xc3\xa1nka, but her look showed how certainly she knew he was not making\nfun.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaking fun? If you only knew how I\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe words sounded still more commonplace, they accorded still less with\nwhat he felt, but yet he continued, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what I would not do\nfor you\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cLeave me alone, you pitch!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nBut her face, her shining eyes, her swelling bosom, her shapely legs,\nsaid something quite different. It seemed to him that she understood\nhow petty were all things he had said, but that she was superior to\nsuch considerations. It seemed to him she had long known all he wished\nand was not able to tell her, but wanted to hear how he would say it.\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how can she help knowing,\xe2\x80\x9d he thought, \xe2\x80\x9csince I only want to tell\nher all that she herself is? But she does not wish to understand, does\nnot wish to reply.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHullo!\xe2\x80\x9d suddenly came \xc3\x9astenka\xe2\x80\x99s high voice from behind the vine at no\ngreat distance, followed by her shrill laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cCome and help me, Dm\xc3\xadtri\nAndr\xc3\xa9ich. I am all alone,\xe2\x80\x9d she cried, thrusting her round, na\xc3\xafve little\nface through the vines.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin did not answer nor move from his place.\n\nMary\xc3\xa1nka went on cutting and continually looked up at Ol\xc3\xa9nin. He was\nabout to say something, but stopped, shrugged his shoulders and, having\njerked up his gun, walked out of the vineyard with rapid strides.\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XXXII\n\n\nHe stopped once or twice, listening to the ringing laughter of Mary\xc3\xa1nka\nand \xc3\x9astenka who, having come together, were shouting something. Ol\xc3\xa9nin\nspent the whole evening hunting in the forest and returned home at dusk\nwithout having killed anything. When crossing the road he noticed her\nopen the door of the outhouse, and her blue smock showed through it. He\ncalled to Vany\xc3\xbasha very loud so as to let her know that he was back,\nand then sat down in the porch in his usual place. His hosts now\nreturned from the vineyard; they came out of the outhouse and into\ntheir hut, but did not ask him in. Mary\xc3\xa1nka went twice out of the gate.\nOnce in the twilight it seemed to him that she was looking at him. He\neagerly followed her every movement, but could not make up his mind to\napproach her. When she disappeared into the hut he left the porch and\nbegan pacing up and down the yard, but Mary\xc3\xa1nka did not come out again.\nOl\xc3\xa9nin spent the whole sleepless night out in the yard listening to\nevery sound in his hosts\xe2\x80\x99 hut. He heard them talking early in the\nevening, heard them having their supper and pulling out their cushions,\nand going to bed; he heard Mary\xc3\xa1nka laughing at something, and then\nheard everything growing gradually quiet.\n\nThe cornet and his wife talked a while in whispers, and someone was\nbreathing. Ol\xc3\xa9nin re-entered his hut. Vany\xc3\xbasha lay asleep in his\nclothes. Ol\xc3\xa9nin envied him, and again went out to pace the yard, always\nexpecting something, but no one came, no one moved, and he only heard\nthe regular breathing of three people. He knew Mary\xc3\xa1nka\xe2\x80\x99s breathing and\nlistened to it and to the beating of his own heart. In the village\neverything was quiet. The waning moon rose late, and the deep-breathing\ncattle in the yard became more visible as they lay down and slowly\nrose. Ol\xc3\xa9nin angrily asked himself, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it I want?\xe2\x80\x9d but could not\ntear himself away from the enchantment of the night. Suddenly he\nthought he distinctly heard the floor creak and the sound of footsteps\nin his hosts\xe2\x80\x99 hut. He rushed to the door, but all was silent again\nexcept for the sound of regular breathing, and in the yard the\nbuffalo-cow, after a deep sigh, again moved, rose on her foreknees and\nthen on her feet, swished her tail, and something splashed steadily on\nthe dry clay ground; then she lay down again in the dim moonlight. He\nasked himself: \xe2\x80\x9cWhat am I to do?\xe2\x80\x9d and definitely decided to go to bed,\nbut again he heard a sound, and in his imagination there arose the\nimage of Mary\xc3\xa1nka coming out into this moonlit misty night, and again\nhe rushed to her window and again heard the sound of footsteps. Not\ntill just before dawn did he go up to her window and push at the\nshutter and then run to the door, and this time he really heard\nMary\xc3\xa1nka\xe2\x80\x99s deep breathing and her footsteps. He took hold of the latch\nand knocked. The floor hardly creaked under the bare cautious footsteps\nwhich approached the door. The latch clicked, the door creaked, and he\nnoticed a faint smell of marjoram and pumpkin, and Mary\xc3\xa1nka\xe2\x80\x99s whole\nfigure appeared in the doorway. He saw her only for an instant in the\nmoonlight. She slammed the door and, muttering something, ran lightly\nback again. Ol\xc3\xa9nin began rapping softly but nothing responded. He ran\nto the window and listened. Suddenly he was startled by a shrill,\nsqueaky man\xe2\x80\x99s voice.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cFine!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed a rather small young Cossack in a white cap, coming\nacross the yard close to Ol\xc3\xa9nin. \xe2\x80\x9cI saw ... fine!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin recognized Naz\xc3\xa1rka, and was silent, not knowing what to do or\nsay.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cFine! I\xe2\x80\x99ll go and tell them at the office, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell her father!\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s a fine cornet\xe2\x80\x99s daughter! One\xe2\x80\x99s not enough for her.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you want of me, what are you after?\xe2\x80\x9d uttered Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing; only I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell them at the office.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nNaz\xc3\xa1rka spoke very loud, and evidently did so intentionally, adding:\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust see what a clever cadet!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin trembled and grew pale.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome here, here!\xe2\x80\x9d He seized the Cossack firmly by the arm and drew him\ntowards his hut.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing happened, she did not let me in, and I too mean no harm. She\nis an honest girl\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh, discuss\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but all the same I\xe2\x80\x99ll give you something now. Wait a bit!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nNaz\xc3\xa1rka said nothing. Ol\xc3\xa9nin ran into his hut and brought out ten\nrubles, which he gave to the Cossack.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing happened, but still I was to blame, so I give this!\xe2\x80\x94Only for\nGod\xe2\x80\x99s sake don\xe2\x80\x99t let anyone know, for nothing happened...\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish you joy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Naz\xc3\xa1rka laughing, and went away.\n\nNaz\xc3\xa1rka had come to the village that night at Luk\xc3\xa1shka\xe2\x80\x99s bidding to\nfind a place to hide a stolen horse, and now, passing by on his way\nhome, had heard the sound of footsteps. When he returned next morning\nto his company he bragged to his chum, and told him how cleverly he had\ngot ten rubles. Next morning Ol\xc3\xa9nin met his hosts and they knew nothing\nabout the events of the night. He did not speak to Mary\xc3\xa1nka, and she\nonly laughed a little when she looked at him. Next night he also passed\nwithout sleep, vainly wandering about the yard. The day after he\npurposely spent shooting, and in the evening he went to see Bel\xc3\xa9tski to\nescape from his own thoughts. He was afraid of himself, and promised\nhimself not to go to his hosts\xe2\x80\x99 hut any more.\n\nThat night he was roused by the sergeant-major. His company was ordered\nto start at once on a raid. Ol\xc3\xa9nin was glad this had happened, and\nthought he would not again return to the village.\n\nThe raid lasted four days. The commander, who was a relative of\nOl\xc3\xa9nin\xe2\x80\x99s, wished to see him and offered to let him remain with the\nstaff, but this Ol\xc3\xa9nin declined. He found that he could not live away\nfrom the village, and asked to be allowed to return to it. For having\ntaken part in the raid he received a soldier\xe2\x80\x99s cross, which he had\nformerly greatly desired. Now he was quite indifferent about it, and\neven more indifferent about his promotion, the order for which had\nstill not arrived. Accompanied by Vany\xc3\xbasha he rode back to the cordon\nwithout any accident several hours in advance of the rest of the\ncompany. He spent the whole evening in his porch watching Mary\xc3\xa1nka, and\nhe again walked about the yard, without aim or thought, all night.\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XXXIII\n\n\nIt was late when he awoke the next day. His hosts were no longer in. He\ndid not go shooting, but now took up a book, and now went out into the\nporch, and now again re-entered the hut and lay down on the bed.\nVany\xc3\xbasha thought he was ill.\n\nTowards evening Ol\xc3\xa9nin got up, resolutely began writing, and wrote on\ntill late at night. He wrote a letter, but did not post it because he\nfelt that no one would have understood what he wanted to say, and\nbesides it was not necessary that anyone but himself should understand\nit.\n\nThis is what he wrote:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI receive letters of condolence from Russia. They are afraid that I\nshall perish, buried in these wilds. They say about me: \xe2\x80\x98He will become\ncoarse; he will be behind the times in everything; he will take to\ndrink, and who knows but that he may marry a Cossack girl.\xe2\x80\x99 It was not\nfor nothing, they say, that Erm\xc3\xb3lov declared: \xe2\x80\x98Anyone serving in the\nCaucasus for ten years either becomes a confirmed drunkard or marries a\nloose woman.\xe2\x80\x99 How terrible! Indeed it won\xe2\x80\x99t do for me to ruin myself\nwhen I might have the great happiness of even becoming the Countess\nB\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99s husband, or a Court chamberlain, or a _Mar\xc3\xa9chal de noblesse_ of\nmy district. Oh, how repulsive and pitiable you all seem to me! You do\nnot know what happiness is and what life is! One must taste life once\nin all its natural beauty, must see and understand what I see every day\nbefore me\xe2\x80\x94those eternally unapproachable snowy peaks, and a majestic\nwoman in that primitive beauty in which the first woman must have come\nfrom her creator\xe2\x80\x99s hands\xe2\x80\x94and then it becomes clear who is ruining\nhimself and who is living truly or falsely\xe2\x80\x94you or I. If you only knew\nhow despicable and pitiable you, in your delusions, seem to me! When I\npicture to myself\xe2\x80\x94in place of my hut, my forests, and my love\xe2\x80\x94those\ndrawing-rooms, those women with their pomatum-greased hair eked out\nwith false curls, those unnaturally grimacing lips, those hidden,\nfeeble, distorted limbs, and that chatter of obligatory drawing-room\nconversation which has no right to the name\xe2\x80\x94I feel unendurably\nrevolted. I then see before me those obtuse faces, those rich eligible\ngirls whose looks seem to say: \xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99s all right, you may come near\nthough I am rich and eligible\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94and that arranging and rearranging of\nseats, that shameless match-making and that eternal tittle-tattle and\npretence; those rules\xe2\x80\x94with whom to shake hands, to whom only to nod,\nwith whom to converse (and all this done deliberately with a conviction\nof its inevitability), that continual ennui in the blood passing on\nfrom generation to generation. Try to understand or believe just this\none thing: you need only see and comprehend what truth and beauty are,\nand all that you now say and think and all your wishes for me and for\nyourselves will fly to atoms!\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHappiness is being with nature, seeing her, and conversing with her.\n\xe2\x80\x98He may even (God forbid) marry a common Cossack girl, and be quite\nlost socially\xe2\x80\x99 I can imagine them saying of me with sincere pity! Yet\nthe one thing I desire is to be quite \xe2\x80\x98lost\xe2\x80\x99 in your sense of the word.\nI wish to marry a Cossack girl, and dare not because it would be a\nheight of happiness of which I am unworthy.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree months have passed since I first saw the Cossack girl, Mary\xc3\xa1nka.\nThe views and prejudices of the world I had left were still fresh in\nme. I did not then believe that I could love that woman. I delighted in\nher beauty just as I delighted in the beauty of the mountains and the\nsky, nor could I help delighting in her, for she is as beautiful as\nthey. I found that the sight of her beauty had become a necessity of my\nlife and I began asking myself whether I did not love her. But I could\nfind nothing within myself at all like love as I had imagined it to be.\nMine was not the restlessness of loneliness and desire for marriage,\nnor was it platonic, still less a carnal love such as I have\nexperienced. I needed only to see her, to hear her, to know that she\nwas near\xe2\x80\x94and if I was not happy, I was at peace.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter an evening gathering at which I met her and touched her, I felt\nthat between that woman and myself there existed an indissoluble though\nunacknowledged bond against which I could not struggle, yet I did\nstruggle. I asked myself: \xe2\x80\x98Is it possible to love a woman who will\nnever understand the profoundest interests of my life? Is it possible\nto love a woman simply for her beauty, to love the statue of a woman?\xe2\x80\x99\nBut I was already in love with her, though I did not yet trust to my\nfeelings.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter that evening when I first spoke to her our relations changed.\nBefore that she had been to me an extraneous but majestic object of\nexternal nature: but since then she has become a human being. I began\nto meet her, to talk to her, and sometimes to go to work for her father\nand to spend whole evenings with them, and in this intimate intercourse\nshe remained still in my eyes just as pure, inaccessible, and majestic.\nShe always responded with equal calm, pride, and cheerful equanimity.\nSometimes she was friendly, but generally her every look, every word,\nand every movement expressed equanimity\xe2\x80\x94not contemptuous, but crushing\nand bewitching. Every day with a feigned smile on my lips I tried to\nplay a part, and with torments of passion and desire in my heart I\nspoke banteringly to her. She saw that I was dissembling, but looked\nstraight at me cheerfully and simply. This position became unbearable.\nI wished not to deceive her but to tell her all I thought and felt. I\nwas extremely agitated. We were in the vineyard when I began to tell\nher of my love, in words I am now ashamed to remember. I am ashamed\nbecause I ought not to have dared to speak so to her because she stood\nfar above such words and above the feeling they were meant to express.\nI said no more, but from that day my position has been intolerable. I\ndid not wish to demean myself by continuing our former flippant\nrelations, and at the same time I felt that I had not yet reached the\nlevel of straight and simple relations with her. I asked myself\ndespairingly, \xe2\x80\x98What am I to do?\xe2\x80\x99 In foolish dreams I imagined her now\nas my mistress and now as my wife, but rejected both ideas with\ndisgust. To make her a wanton woman would be dreadful. It would be\nmurder. To turn her into a fine lady, the wife of Dm\xc3\xadtri Andr\xc3\xa9ich\nOl\xc3\xa9nin, like a Cossack woman here who is married to one of our\nofficers, would be still worse. Now could I turn Cossack like Luk\xc3\xa1shka,\nand steal horses, get drunk on _chikhir_, sing rollicking songs, kill\npeople, and when drunk climb in at her window for the night without a\nthought of who and what I am, it would be different: then we might\nunderstand one another and I might be happy.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI tried to throw myself into that kind of life but was still more\nconscious of my own weakness and artificiality. I cannot forget myself\nand my complex, distorted past, and my future appears to me still more\nhopeless. Every day I have before me the distant snowy mountains and\nthis majestic, happy woman. But not for me is the only happiness\npossible in the world; I cannot have this woman! What is most terrible\nand yet sweetest in my condition is that I feel that I understand her\nbut that she will never understand me; not because she is inferior: on\nthe contrary she ought not to understand me. She is happy, she is like\nnature: consistent, calm, and self-contained; and I, a weak distorted\nbeing, want her to understand my deformity and my torments! I have not\nslept at night, but have aimlessly passed under her windows not\nrendering account to myself of what was happening to me. On the 18th\nour company started on a raid, and I spent three days away from the\nvillage. I was sad and apathetic, the usual songs, cards,\ndrinking-bouts, and talk of rewards in the regiment, were more\nrepulsive to me than usual. Yesterday I returned home and saw her, my\nhut. Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka, and the snowy mountains, from my porch, and was\nseized by such a strong, new feeling of joy that I understood it all. I\nlove this woman; I feel real love for the first and only time in my\nlife. I know what has befallen me. I do not fear to be degraded by this\nfeeling, I am not ashamed of my love, I am proud of it. It is not my\nfault that I love. It has come about against my will. I tried to escape\nfrom my love by self-renunciation, and tried to devise a joy in the\nCossack Luk\xc3\xa1shka\xe2\x80\x99s and Mary\xc3\xa1nka\xe2\x80\x99s love, but thereby only stirred up my\nown love and jealousy. This is not the ideal, the so-called exalted\nlove which I have known before; not that sort of attachment in which\nyou admire your own love and feel that the source of your emotion is\nwithin yourself and do everything yourself. I have felt that too. It is\nstill less a desire for enjoyment: it is something different. Perhaps\nin her I love nature: the personification of all that is beautiful in\nnature; but yet I am not acting by my own will, but some elemental\nforce loves through me; the whole of God\xe2\x80\x99s world, all nature, presses\nthis love into my soul and says, \xe2\x80\x98Love her.\xe2\x80\x99 I love her not with my\nmind or my imagination, but with my whole being. Loving her I feel\nmyself to be an integral part of all God\xe2\x80\x99s joyous world. I wrote before\nabout the new convictions to which my solitary life had brought me, but\nno one knows with what labour they shaped themselves within me and with\nwhat joy I realized them and saw a new way of life opening out before\nme; nothing was dearer to me than those convictions... Well! ... love\nhas come and neither they nor any regrets for them remain! It is even\ndifficult for me to believe that I could prize such a one-sided, cold,\nand abstract state of mind. Beauty came and scattered to the winds all\nthat laborious inward toil, and no regret remains for what has\nvanished! Self-renunciation is all nonsense and absurdity! That is\npride, a refuge from well-merited unhappiness, and salvation from the\nenvy of others\xe2\x80\x99 happiness: \xe2\x80\x98Live for others, and do good!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94Why? when in\nmy soul there is only love for myself and the desire to love her and to\nlive her life with her? Not for others, not for Luk\xc3\xa1shka, I now desire\nhappiness. I do not now love those others. Formerly I should have told\nmyself that this is wrong. I should have tormented myself with the\nquestions: What will become of her, of me, and of Luk\xc3\xa1shka? Now I don\xe2\x80\x99t\ncare. I do not live my own life, there is something stronger than me\nwhich directs me. I suffer; but formerly I was dead and only now do I\nlive. Today I will go to their house and tell her everything.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XXXIV\n\n\nLate that evening, after writing this letter, Ol\xc3\xa9nin went to his hosts\xe2\x80\x99\nhut. The old woman was sitting on a bench behind the oven unwinding\ncocoons. Mary\xc3\xa1nka with her head uncovered sat sewing by the light of a\ncandle. On seeing Ol\xc3\xa9nin she jumped up, took her kerchief and stepped\nto the oven.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMary\xc3\xa1nka dear,\xe2\x80\x9d said her mother, \xe2\x80\x9cwon\xe2\x80\x99t you sit here with me a bit?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I\xe2\x80\x99m bareheaded,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied, and sprang up on the oven.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin could only see a knee, and one of her shapely legs hanging down\nfrom the oven. He treated the old woman to tea. She treated her guest\nto clotted cream which she sent Mary\xc3\xa1nka to fetch. But having put a\nplateful on the table Mary\xc3\xa1nka again sprang on the oven from whence\nOl\xc3\xa9nin felt her eyes upon him. They talked about household matters.\nGranny Ul\xc3\xadtka became animated and went into raptures of hospitality.\nShe brought Ol\xc3\xa9nin preserved grapes and a grape tart and some of her\nbest wine, and pressed him to eat and drink with the rough yet proud\nhospitality of country folk, only found among those who produce their\nbread by the labour of their own hands.\n\nThe old woman, who had at first struck Ol\xc3\xa9nin so much by her rudeness,\nnow often touched him by her simple tenderness towards her daughter.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, we need not offend the Lord by grumbling! We have enough of\neverything, thank God. We have pressed sufficient _chikhir_ and have\npreserved and shall sell three or four barrels of grapes and have\nenough left to drink. Don\xe2\x80\x99t be in a hurry to leave us. We will make\nmerry together at the wedding.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd when is the wedding to be?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ol\xc3\xa9nin, feeling his blood\nsuddenly rush to his face while his heart beat irregularly and\npainfully.\n\nHe heard a movement on the oven and the sound of seeds being cracked.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you know, it ought to be next week. We are quite ready,\xe2\x80\x9d replied\nthe old woman, as simply and quietly as though Ol\xc3\xa9nin did not exist. \xe2\x80\x9cI\nhave prepared and have procured everything for Mary\xc3\xa1nka. We will give\nher away properly. Only there\xe2\x80\x99s one thing not quite right. Our Luk\xc3\xa1shka\nhas been running rather wild. He has been too much on the spree! He\xe2\x80\x99s\nup to tricks! The other day a Cossack came here from his company and\nsaid he had been to Nog\xc3\xa1y.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe must mind he does not get caught,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that\xe2\x80\x99s what I tell him. \xe2\x80\x98Mind, Luk\xc3\xa1shka, don\xe2\x80\x99t you get into\nmischief. Well, of course, a young fellow naturally wants to cut a\ndash. But there\xe2\x80\x99s a time for everything. Well, you\xe2\x80\x99ve captured or\nstolen something and killed an _abrek!_ Well, you\xe2\x80\x99re a fine fellow! But\nnow you should live quietly for a bit, or else there\xe2\x80\x99ll be trouble.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I saw him a time or two in the division, he was always\nmerry-making. He has sold another horse,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ol\xc3\xa9nin, and glanced\ntowards the oven. A pair of large, dark, and hostile eyes glittered as\nthey gazed severely at him.\n\nHe became ashamed of what he had said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat of it? He does no one any\nharm,\xe2\x80\x9d suddenly remarked Mary\xc3\xa1nka. \xe2\x80\x9cHe makes merry with his own money,\xe2\x80\x9d\nand lowering her legs she jumped down from the oven and went out\nbanging the door.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin followed her with his eyes as long as she was in the hut, and\nthen looked at the door and waited, understanding nothing of what\nGranny Ul\xc3\xadtka was telling him.\n\nA few minutes later some visitors arrived: an old man, Granny Ul\xc3\xadtka\xe2\x80\x99s\nbrother, with Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka, and following them came Mary\xc3\xa1nka and\n\xc3\x9astenka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood evening,\xe2\x80\x9d squeaked \xc3\x9astenka. \xe2\x80\x9cStill on holiday?\xe2\x80\x9d she added,\nturning to Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, still on holiday,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied, and felt, he did not know why,\nashamed and ill at ease.\n\nHe wished to go away but could not. It also seemed to him impossible to\nremain silent. The old man helped him by asking for a drink, and they\nhad a drink. Ol\xc3\xa9nin drank with Er\xc3\xb3shka, with the other Cossack, and\nagain with Er\xc3\xb3shka, and the more he drank the heavier was his heart.\nBut the two old men grew merry. The girls climbed onto the oven, where\nthey sat whispering and looking at the men, who drank till it was late.\nOl\xc3\xa9nin did not talk, but drank more than the others. The Cossacks were\nshouting. The old woman would not let them have any more _chikhir_, and\nat last turned them out. The girls laughed at Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka, and it was\npast ten when they all went out into the porch. The old men invited\nthemselves to finish their merry-making at Ol\xc3\xa9nin\xe2\x80\x99s. \xc3\x9astenka ran off\nhome and Er\xc3\xb3shka led the old Cossack to Vany\xc3\xbasha. The old woman went\nout to tidy up the shed. Mary\xc3\xa1nka remained alone in the hut. Ol\xc3\xa9nin\nfelt fresh and joyous, as if he had only just woke up. He noticed\neverything, and having let the old men pass ahead he turned back to the\nhut where Mary\xc3\xa1nka was preparing for bed. He went up to her and wished\nto say something, but his voice broke. She moved away from him, sat\ndown cross-legged on her bed in the corner, and looked at him silently\nwith wild and frightened eyes. She was evidently afraid of him. Ol\xc3\xa9nin\nfelt this. He felt sorry and ashamed of himself, and at the same time\nproud and pleased that he aroused even that feeling in her.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMary\xc3\xa1nka!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cWill you never take pity on me? I can\xe2\x80\x99t tell you\nhow I love you.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nShe moved still farther away.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust hear how the wine is speaking! ... You\xe2\x80\x99ll get nothing from me!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, it is not the wine. Don\xe2\x80\x99t marry Luk\xc3\xa1shka. I will marry you.\xe2\x80\x9d\n(\xe2\x80\x9cWhat am I saying,\xe2\x80\x9d he thought as he uttered these words. \xe2\x80\x9cShall I be\nable to say the same tomorrow?\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cYes, I shall, I am sure I shall, and I\nwill repeat them now,\xe2\x80\x9d replied an inner voice.)\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you marry me?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nShe looked at him seriously and her fear seemed to have passed.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMary\xc3\xa1nka, I shall go out of my mind! I am not myself. I will do\nwhatever you command,\xe2\x80\x9d and madly tender words came from his lips of\ntheir own accord.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow then, what are you drivelling about?\xe2\x80\x9d she interrupted, suddenly\nseizing the arm he was stretching towards her. She did not push his arm\naway but pressed it firmly with her strong hard fingers. \xe2\x80\x9cDo gentlemen\nmarry Cossack girls? Go away!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut will you? Everything...\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what shall we do with Luk\xc3\xa1shka?\xe2\x80\x9d said she, laughing.\n\nHe snatched away the arm she was holding and firmly embraced her young\nbody, but she sprang away like a fawn and ran barefoot into the porch:\nOl\xc3\xa9nin came to his senses and was terrified at himself. He again felt\nhimself inexpressibly vile compared to her, yet not repenting for an\ninstant of what he had said he went home, and without even glancing at\nthe old men who were drinking in his room he lay down and fell asleep\nmore soundly than he had done for a long time.\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XXXV\n\n\nThe next day was a holiday. In the evening all the villagers, their\nholiday clothes shining in the sunset, were out in the street. That\nseason more wine than usual had been produced, and the people were now\nfree from their labours. In a month the Cossacks were to start on a\ncampaign and in many families preparations were being made for\nweddings.\n\nMost of the people were standing in the square in front of the Cossack\nGovernment Office and near the two shops, in one of which cakes and\npumpkin seeds were sold, in the other kerchiefs and cotton prints. On\nthe earth-embankment of the office-building sat or stood the old men in\nsober grey, or black coats without gold trimmings or any kind of\nornament. They conversed among themselves quietly in measured tones,\nabout the harvest, about the young folk, about village affairs, and\nabout old times, looking with dignified equanimity at the younger\ngeneration. Passing by them, the women and girls stopped and bent their\nheads. The young Cossacks respectfully slackened their pace and raised\ntheir caps, holding them for a while over their heads. The old men then\nstopped speaking. Some of them watched the passers-by severely, others\nkindly, and in their turn slowly took off their caps and put them on\nagain.\n\nThe Cossack girls had not yet started dancing their _khorov\xc3\xb3ds_, but\nhaving gathered in groups, in their bright coloured _beshmets_ with\nwhite kerchiefs on their heads pulled down to their eyes, they sat\neither on the ground or on the earth-banks about the huts sheltered\nfrom the oblique rays of the sun, and laughed and chattered in their\nringing voices. Little boys and girls playing in the square sent their\nballs high up into the clear sky, and ran about squealing and shouting.\nThe half-grown girls had started dancing their _khorov\xc3\xb3ds_, and were\ntimidly singing in their thin shrill voices. Clerks, lads not in the\nservice, or home for the holiday, bright-faced and wearing smart white\nor new red Circassian gold-trimmed coats, went about arm in arm in twos\nor threes from one group of women or girls to another, and stopped to\njoke and chat with the Cossack girls. The Armenian shopkeeper, in a\ngold-trimmed coat of fine blue cloth, stood at the open door through\nwhich piles of folded bright-coloured kerchiefs were visible and,\nconscious of his own importance and with the pride of an Oriental\ntradesman, waited for customers. Two red-bearded, barefooted Ch\xc3\xa9chens,\nwho had come from beyond the T\xc3\xa9rek to see the f\xc3\xaate, sat on their heels\noutside the house of a friend, negligently smoking their little pipes\nand occasionally spitting, watching the villagers and exchanging\nremarks with one another in their rapid guttural speech. Occasionally a\nworkaday-looking soldier in an old overcoat passed across the square\namong the bright-clad girls. Here and there the songs of tipsy Cossacks\nwho were merry-making could already be heard. All the huts were closed;\nthe porches had been scrubbed clean the day before. Even the old women\nwere out in the street, which was everywhere sprinkled with pumpkin and\nmelon seed-shells. The air was warm and still, the sky deep and clear.\nBeyond the roofs the dead-white mountain range, which seemed very near,\nwas turning rosy in the glow of the evening sun. Now and then from the\nother side of the river came the distant roar of a cannon, but above\nthe village, mingling with one another, floated all sorts of merry\nholiday sounds.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin had been pacing the yard all that morning hoping to see\nMary\xc3\xa1nka. But she, having put on holiday clothes, went to Mass at the\nchapel and afterwards sat with the other girls on an earth-embankment\ncracking seeds; sometimes again, together with her companions, she ran\nhome, and each time gave the lodger a bright and kindly look. Ol\xc3\xa9nin\nfelt afraid to address her playfully or in the presence of others. He\nwished to finish telling her what he had begun to say the night before,\nand to get her to give him a definite answer. He waited for another\nmoment like that of yesterday evening, but the moment did not come, and\nhe felt that he could not remain any longer in this uncertainty. She\nwent out into the street again, and after waiting awhile he too went\nout and without knowing where he was going he followed her. He passed\nby the corner where she was sitting in her shining blue satin\n_beshmet_, and with an aching heart he heard behind him the girls\nlaughing.\n\nBel\xc3\xa9tski\xe2\x80\x99s hut looked out onto the square. As Ol\xc3\xa9nin was passing it he\nheard Bel\xc3\xa9tski\xe2\x80\x99s voice calling to him, \xe2\x80\x9cCome in,\xe2\x80\x9d and in he went.\n\nAfter a short talk they both sat down by the window and were soon\njoined by Er\xc3\xb3shka, who entered dressed in a new _beshmet_ and sat down\non the floor beside them.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere, that\xe2\x80\x99s the aristocratic party,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bel\xc3\xa9tski, pointing with\nhis cigarette to a brightly coloured group at the corner. \xe2\x80\x9cMine is\nthere too. Do you see her? in red. That\xe2\x80\x99s a new _beshmet_. Why don\xe2\x80\x99t\nyou start the _khorov\xc3\xb3d?_\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted, leaning out of the window. \xe2\x80\x9cWait\na bit, and then when it grows dark let us go too. Then we will invite\nthem to \xc3\x9astenka\xe2\x80\x99s. We must arrange a ball for them!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I will come to \xc3\x9astenka\xe2\x80\x99s,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ol\xc3\xa9nin in a decided tone. \xe2\x80\x9cWill\nMary\xc3\xa1nka be there?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, she\xe2\x80\x99ll be there. Do come!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bel\xc3\xa9tski, without the least\nsurprise. \xe2\x80\x9cBut isn\xe2\x80\x99t it a pretty picture?\xe2\x80\x9d he added, pointing to the\nmotley crowds.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, very!\xe2\x80\x9d Ol\xc3\xa9nin assented, trying to appear indifferent.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHolidays of this kind,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, \xe2\x80\x9calways make me wonder why all these\npeople should suddenly be contented and jolly. Today for instance, just\nbecause it happens to be the fifteenth of the month, everything is\nfestive. Eyes and faces and voices and movements and garments, and the\nair and the sun, are all in a holiday mood. And we no longer have any\nholidays!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bel\xc3\xa9tski, who did not like such reflections.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd why are you not drinking, old fellow?\xe2\x80\x9d he said, turning to\nEr\xc3\xb3shka.\n\nEr\xc3\xb3shka winked at Ol\xc3\xa9nin, pointing to Bel\xc3\xa9tski. \xe2\x80\x9cEh, he\xe2\x80\x99s a proud one\nthat _kunak_ of yours,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\n\nBel\xc3\xa9tski raised his glass.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Allah birdy!_\xe2\x80\x9d he said, emptying it. (_Allah birdy_, \xe2\x80\x9cGod has\ngiven!\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the usual greeting of Caucasians when drinking together.)\n\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Sau bul_\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x9cYour health\xe2\x80\x9d), answered Er\xc3\xb3shka smiling, and emptied his\nglass.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpeaking of holidays!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, turning to Ol\xc3\xa9nin as he rose and\nlooked out of the window, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat sort of holiday is that! You should\nhave seen them make merry in the old days! The women used to come out\nin their gold-trimmed _saraf\xc3\xa1ns_. Two rows of gold coins hanging round\ntheir necks and gold-cloth diadems on their heads, and when they passed\nthey made a noise, \xe2\x80\x98flu, flu,\xe2\x80\x99 with their dresses. Every woman looked\nlike a princess. Sometimes they\xe2\x80\x99d come out, a whole herd of them, and\nbegin singing songs so that the air seemed to rumble, and they went on\nmaking merry all night. And the Cossacks would roll out a barrel into\nthe yards and sit down and drink till break of day, or they would go\nhand-in-hand sweeping the village. Whoever they met they seized and\ntook along with them, and went from house to house. Sometimes they used\nto make merry for three days on end. Father used to come home\xe2\x80\x94I still\nremember it\xe2\x80\x94quite red and swollen, without a cap, having lost\neverything: he\xe2\x80\x99d come and lie down. Mother knew what to do: she would\nbring him some fresh caviar and a little _chikhir_ to sober him up, and\nwould herself run about in the village looking for his cap. Then he\xe2\x80\x99d\nsleep for two days! That\xe2\x80\x99s the sort of fellows they were then! But now\nwhat are they?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, and the girls in the _saraf\xc3\xa1ns_, did they make merry all by\nthemselves?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Bel\xc3\xa9tski.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, they did! Sometimes Cossacks would come on foot or on horse and\nsay, \xe2\x80\x98Let\xe2\x80\x99s break up the _khorov\xc3\xb3ds_,\xe2\x80\x99 and they\xe2\x80\x99d go, but the girls\nwould take up cudgels. Carnival week, some young fellow would come\ngalloping up, and they\xe2\x80\x99d cudgel his horse and cudgel him too. But he\xe2\x80\x99d\nbreak through, seize the one he loved, and carry her off. And his\nsweetheart would love him to his heart\xe2\x80\x99s content! Yes, the girls in\nthose days, they were regular queens!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XXXVI\n\n\nJust then two men rode out of the side street into the square. One of\nthem was Naz\xc3\xa1rka. The other, Luk\xc3\xa1shka, sat slightly sideways on his\nwell-fed bay Kabard\xc3\xa1 horse which stepped lightly over the hard road\njerking its beautiful head with its fine glossy mane. The well-adjusted\ngun in its cover, the pistol at his back, and the cloak rolled up\nbehind his saddle showed that Luk\xc3\xa1shka had not come from a peaceful\nplace or from one near by. The smart way in which he sat a little\nsideways on his horse, the careless motion with which he touched the\nhorse under its belly with his whip, and especially his half-closed\nblack eyes, glistening as he looked proudly around him, all expressed\nthe conscious strength and self-confidence of youth. \xe2\x80\x9cEver seen as fine\na lad?\xe2\x80\x9d his eyes, looking from side to side, seemed to say. The elegant\nhorse with its silver ornaments and trappings, the weapons, and the\nhandsome Cossack himself attracted the attention of everyone in the\nsquare. Naz\xc3\xa1rka, lean and short, was much less well dressed. As he rode\npast the old men, Luk\xc3\xa1shka paused and raised his curly white sheepskin\ncap above his closely cropped black head.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, have you carried off many Nog\xc3\xa1y horses?\xe2\x80\x9d asked a lean old man\nwith a frowning, lowering look.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you counted them, Grandad, that you ask?\xe2\x80\x9d replied Luk\xc3\xa1shka,\nturning away.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s all very well, but you need not take my lad along with you,\xe2\x80\x9d\nthe old man muttered with a still darker frown.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust see the old devil, he knows everything,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Luk\xc3\xa1shka to\nhimself, and a worried expression came over his face; but then,\nnoticing a corner where a number of Cossack girls were standing, he\nturned his horse towards them.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood evening, girls!\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted in his powerful, resonant voice,\nsuddenly checking his horse. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve grown old without me, you\nwitches!\xe2\x80\x9d and he laughed.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood evening, Luk\xc3\xa1shka! Good evening, laddie!\xe2\x80\x9d the merry voices\nanswered. \xe2\x80\x9cHave you brought much money? Buy some sweets for the\ngirls!... Have you come for long? True enough, it\xe2\x80\x99s long since we saw\nyou....\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNaz\xc3\xa1rka and I have just flown across to make a night of it,\xe2\x80\x9d replied\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka, raising his whip and riding straight at the girls.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Mary\xc3\xa1nka has quite forgotten you,\xe2\x80\x9d said \xc3\x9astenka, nudging Mary\xc3\xa1nka\nwith her elbow and breaking into a shrill laugh.\n\nMary\xc3\xa1nka moved away from the horse and throwing back her head calmly\nlooked at the Cossack with her large sparkling eyes.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue enough, you have not been home for a long time! Why are you\ntrampling us under your horse?\xe2\x80\x9d she remarked dryly, and turned away.\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka had appeared particularly merry. His face shone with audacity\nand joy. Obviously staggered by Mary\xc3\xa1nka\xe2\x80\x99s cold reply he suddenly\nknitted his brow.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cStep up on my stirrup and I\xe2\x80\x99ll carry you away to the mountains.\nMammy!\xe2\x80\x9d he suddenly exclaimed, and as if to disperse his dark thoughts\nhe caracoled among the girls. Stooping down towards Mary\xc3\xa1nka, he said,\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll kiss, oh, how I\xe2\x80\x99ll kiss you! ...\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nMary\xc3\xa1nka\xe2\x80\x99s eyes met his and she suddenly blushed and stepped back.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, bother you! you\xe2\x80\x99ll crush my feet,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, and bending her head\nlooked at her well-shaped feet in their tightly fitting light blue\nstockings with clocks and her new red slippers trimmed with narrow\nsilver braid.\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka turned towards \xc3\x9astenka, and Mary\xc3\xa1nka sat down next to a woman\nwith a baby in her arms. The baby stretched his plump little hands\ntowards the girl and seized a necklace string that hung down onto her\nblue _beshmet_. Mary\xc3\xa1nka bent towards the child and glanced at Luk\xc3\xa1shka\nfrom the corner of her eyes. Luk\xc3\xa1shka just then was getting out from\nunder his coat, from the pocket of his black _beshmet_, a bundle of\nsweetmeats and seeds.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere, I give them to all of you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, handing the bundle to\n\xc3\x9astenka and smiling at Mary\xc3\xa1nka.\n\nA confused expression again appeared on the girl\xe2\x80\x99s face. It was as\nthough a mist gathered over her beautiful eyes. She drew her kerchief\ndown below her lips, and leaning her head over the fair-skinned face of\nthe baby that still held her by her coin necklace she suddenly began to\nkiss it greedily. The baby pressed his little hands against the girl\xe2\x80\x99s\nhigh breasts, and opening his toothless mouth screamed loudly.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re smothering the boy!\xe2\x80\x9d said the little one\xe2\x80\x99s mother, taking him\naway; and she unfastened her _beshmet_ to give him the breast. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99d\nbetter have a chat with the young fellow.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll only go and put up my horse and then Naz\xc3\xa1rka and I will come\nback; we\xe2\x80\x99ll make merry all night,\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka, touching his horse\nwith his whip and riding away from the girls.\n\nTurning into a side street, he and Naz\xc3\xa1rka rode up to two huts that\nstood side by side.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere we are all right, old fellow! Be quick and come soon!\xe2\x80\x9d called\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka to his comrade, dismounting in front of one of the huts; then\nhe carefully led his horse in at the gate of the wattle fence of his\nown home.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow d\xe2\x80\x99you do, St\xc3\xabpka?\xe2\x80\x9d he said to his dumb sister, who, smartly\ndressed like the others, came in from the street to take his horse; and\nhe made signs to her to take the horse to the hay, but not to unsaddle\nit.\n\nThe dumb girl made her usual humming noise, smacked her lips as she\npointed to the horse and kissed it on the nose, as much as to say that\nshe loved it and that it was a fine horse.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow d\xe2\x80\x99you do, Mother? How is it that you have not gone out yet?\xe2\x80\x9d\nshouted Luk\xc3\xa1shka, holding his gun in place as he mounted the steps of\nthe porch.\n\nHis old mother opened the door.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear me! I never expected, never thought, you\xe2\x80\x99d come,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old\nwoman. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, K\xc3\xadrka said you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be here.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo and bring some _chikhir_, Mother. Naz\xc3\xa1rka is coming here and we\nwill celebrate the feast day.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDirectly, Luk\xc3\xa1shka, directly!\xe2\x80\x9d answered the old woman. \xe2\x80\x9cOur women are\nmaking merry. I expect our dumb one has gone too.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nShe took her keys and hurriedly went to the outhouse. Naz\xc3\xa1rka, after\nputting up his horse and taking the gun off his shoulder, returned to\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka\xe2\x80\x99s house and went in.\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XXXVII\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour health!\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka, taking from his mother\xe2\x80\x99s hands a cup\nfilled to the brim with _chikhir_ and carefully raising it to his bowed\nhead.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cA bad business!\xe2\x80\x9d said Naz\xc3\xa1rka. \xe2\x80\x9cYou heard how Daddy Burl\xc3\xa1k said, \xe2\x80\x98Have\nyou stolen many horses?\xe2\x80\x99 He seems to know!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cA regular wizard!\xe2\x80\x9d Luk\xc3\xa1shka replied shortly. \xe2\x80\x9cBut what of it!\xe2\x80\x9d he\nadded, tossing his head. \xe2\x80\x9cThey are across the river by now. Go and find\nthem!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cStill it\xe2\x80\x99s a bad lookout.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s a bad lookout? Go and take some _chikhir_ to him tomorrow and\nnothing will come of it. Now let\xe2\x80\x99s make merry. Drink!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka, just in the tone in which old Er\xc3\xb3shka uttered the word.\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll go out into the street and make merry with the girls. You go and\nget some honey; or no, I\xe2\x80\x99ll send our dumb wench. We\xe2\x80\x99ll make merry till\nmorning.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nNaz\xc3\xa1rka smiled.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre we stopping here long?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cTill we\xe2\x80\x99ve had a bit of fun. Run and get some vodka. Here\xe2\x80\x99s the\nmoney.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nNaz\xc3\xa1rka ran off obediently to get the vodka from Y\xc3\xa1mka\xe2\x80\x99s.\n\nDaddy Er\xc3\xb3shka and Ergush\xc3\xb3v, like birds of prey, scenting where the\nmerry-making was going on, tumbled into the hut one after the other,\nboth tipsy.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBring us another half-pail,\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Luk\xc3\xa1shka to his mother, by way of\nreply to their greeting.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow then, tell us where did you steal them, you devil?\xe2\x80\x9d shouted\nEr\xc3\xb3shka. \xe2\x80\x9cFine fellow, I\xe2\x80\x99m fond of you!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cFond indeed...\xe2\x80\x9d answered Luk\xc3\xa1shka laughing, \xe2\x80\x9ccarrying sweets from\ncadets to lasses! Eh, you old...\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s not true, not true! ... Oh, Mark,\xe2\x80\x9d and the old man burst out\nlaughing. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd how that devil begged me. \xe2\x80\x98Go,\xe2\x80\x99 he said, \xe2\x80\x98and arrange\nit.\xe2\x80\x99 He offered me a gun! But no. I\xe2\x80\x99d have managed it, but I feel for\nyou. Now tell us where have you been?\xe2\x80\x9d And the old man began speaking\nin Tartar.\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka answered him promptly.\n\nErgush\xc3\xb3v, who did not know much Tartar, only occasionally put in a word\nin Russian: \xe2\x80\x9cWhat I say is he\xe2\x80\x99s driven away the horses. I know it for a\nfact,\xe2\x80\x9d he chimed in.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGir\xc3\xa9y and I went together.\xe2\x80\x9d (His speaking of Gir\xc3\xa9y Khan as \xe2\x80\x9cGir\xc3\xa9y\xe2\x80\x9d\nwas, to the Cossack mind, evidence of his boldness.) \xe2\x80\x9cJust beyond the\nriver he kept bragging that he knew the whole of the steppe and would\nlead the way straight, but we rode on and the night was dark, and my\nGir\xc3\xa9y lost his way and began wandering in a circle without getting\nanywhere: couldn\xe2\x80\x99t find the village, and there we were. We must have\ngone too much to the right. I believe we wandered about well-nigh till\nmidnight. Then, thank goodness, we heard dogs howling.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cFools!\xe2\x80\x9d said Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka. \xe2\x80\x9cThere now, we too used to lose our way in\nthe steppe. (Who the devil can follow it?) But I used to ride up a\nhillock and start howling like the wolves, like this!\xe2\x80\x9d He placed his\nhands before his mouth, and howled like a pack of wolves, all on one\nnote. \xe2\x80\x9cThe dogs would answer at once ... Well, go on\xe2\x80\x94so you found\nthem?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe soon led them away! Naz\xc3\xa1rka was nearly caught by some Nog\xc3\xa1y women,\nhe was!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cCaught indeed,\xe2\x80\x9d Naz\xc3\xa1rka, who had just come back, said in an injured\ntone.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe rode off again, and again Gir\xc3\xa9y lost his way and almost landed us\namong the sand-drifts. We thought we were just getting to the T\xc3\xa9rek but\nwe were riding away from it all the time!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou should have steered by the stars,\xe2\x80\x9d said Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what I say,\xe2\x80\x9d interjected Ergush\xc3\xb3v.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, steer when all is black; I tried and tried all about... and at\nlast I put the bridle on one of the mares and let my own horse go\nfree\xe2\x80\x94thinking he\xe2\x80\x99ll lead us out, and what do you think! he just gave a\nsnort or two with his nose to the ground, galloped ahead, and led us\nstraight to our village. Thank goodness! It was getting quite light. We\nbarely had time to hide them in the forest. Nag\xc3\xadm came across the river\nand took them away.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nErgush\xc3\xb3v shook his head. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s just what I said. Smart. Did you get\nmuch for them?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s all here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka, slapping his pocket.\n\nJust then his mother came into the room, and Luk\xc3\xa1shka did not finish\nwhat he was saying.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDrink!\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe too, G\xc3\xadrich and I, rode out late one night...\xe2\x80\x9d began Er\xc3\xb3shka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh bother, we\xe2\x80\x99ll never hear the end of you!\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka. \xe2\x80\x9cI am\ngoing.\xe2\x80\x9d And having emptied his cup and tightened the strap of his belt\nhe went out.\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XXXVIII\n\n\nIt was already dark when Luk\xc3\xa1shka went out into the street. The autumn\nnight was fresh and calm. The full golden moon floated up behind the\ntall dark poplars that grew on one side of the square. From the\nchimneys of the outhouses smoke rose and spread above the village,\nmingling with the mist. Here and there lights shone through the\nwindows, and the air was laden with the smell of _kisyak_, grape-pulp,\nand mist. The sounds of voices, laughter, songs, and the cracking of\nseeds mingled just as they had done in the daytime, but were now more\ndistinct. Clusters of white kerchiefs and caps gleamed through the\ndarkness near the houses and by the fences.\n\nIn the square, before the shop door which was lit up and open, the\nblack and white figures of Cossack men and maids showed through the\ndarkness, and one heard from afar their loud songs and laughter and\ntalk. The girls, hand in hand, went round and round in a circle\nstepping lightly in the dusty square. A skinny girl, the plainest of\nthem all, set the tune:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom beyond the wood, from the forest dark,\nFrom the garden green and the shady park,\nThere came out one day two young lads so gay.\nYoung bachelors, hey! brave and smart were they!\nAnd they walked and walked, then stood still, each man,\nAnd they talked and soon to dispute began!\nThen a maid came out; as she came along,\nSaid, \xe2\x80\x98To one of you I shall soon belong!\xe2\x80\x99\n\xe2\x80\x99Twas the fair-faced lad got the maiden fair,\nYes, the fair-faced lad with the golden hair!\nHer right hand so white in his own took he,\nAnd he led her round for his mates to see!\nAnd said, \xe2\x80\x98Have you ever in all your life,\nMet a lass as fair as my sweet little wife?\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\nThe old women stood round listening to the songs. The little boys and\ngirls ran about chasing one another in the dark. The men stood by,\ncatching at the girls as the latter moved round, and sometimes breaking\nthe ring and entering it. On the dark side of the doorway stood\nBel\xc3\xa9tski and Ol\xc3\xa9nin, in their Circassian coats and sheepskin caps, and\ntalked together in a style of speech unlike that of the Cossacks, in\nlow but distinct tones, conscious that they were attracting attention.\n\nNext to one another in the _khorov\xc3\xb3d_ circle moved plump little \xc3\x9astenka\nin her red _beshmet_ and the stately Mary\xc3\xa1nka in her new smock and\n_beshmet_. Ol\xc3\xa9nin and Bel\xc3\xa9tski were discussing how to snatch \xc3\x9astenka\nand Mary\xc3\xa1nka out of the ring. Bel\xc3\xa9tski thought that Ol\xc3\xa9nin wished only\nto amuse himself, but Ol\xc3\xa9nin was expecting his fate to be decided. He\nwanted at any cost to see Mary\xc3\xa1nka alone that very day and to tell her\neverything, and ask her whether she could and would be his wife.\nAlthough that question had long been answered in the negative in his\nown mind, he hoped he would be able to tell her all he felt, and that\nshe would understand him.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy did you not tell me sooner?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bel\xc3\xa9tski. \xe2\x80\x9cI would have got\n\xc3\x9astenka to arrange it for you. You are such a queer fellow! ...\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s to be done! ... Some day, very soon, I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you all about\nit. Only now, for Heaven\xe2\x80\x99s sake, arrange so that she should come to\n\xc3\x9astenka\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, that\xe2\x80\x99s easily done! Well, Mary\xc3\xa1nka, will you belong to the\n\xe2\x80\x98fair-faced lad\xe2\x80\x99, and not to Luk\xc3\xa1shka?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bel\xc3\xa9tski, speaking to\nMary\xc3\xa1nka first for propriety\xe2\x80\x99s sake, but having received no reply he\nwent up to \xc3\x9astenka and begged her to bring Mary\xc3\xa1nka home with her. He\nhad hardly time to finish what he was saying before the leader began\nanother song and the girls started pulling each other round in the ring\nby the hand.\n\nThey sang:\n\nPast the garden, by the garden,\nA young man came strolling down,\nUp the street and through the town.\nAnd the first time as he passed\nHe did wave his strong right hand.\nAs the second time he passed\nWaved his hat with silken band.\nBut the third time as he went\nHe stood still: before her bent.\n\nHow is it that thou, my dear,\nMy reproaches dost not fear?\nIn the park don\xe2\x80\x99t come to walk\nThat we there might have a talk?\nCome now, answer me, my dear,\nDost thou hold me in contempt?\nLater on, thou knowest, dear,\nThou\xe2\x80\x99lt get sober and repent.\nSoon to woo thee I will come,\nAnd when we shall married be\nThou wilt weep because of me!\n\nThough I knew what to reply,\nYet I dared not him deny,\nNo, I dared not him deny!\nSo into the park went I,\nIn the park my lad to meet,\nThere my dear one I did greet.\n\nMaiden dear, I bow to thee!\nTake this handkerchief from me.\nIn thy white hand take it, see!\nSay I am beloved by thee.\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t know at all, I fear,\nWhat I am to give thee, dear!\nTo my dear I think I will\nOf a shawl a present make\xe2\x80\x94\nAnd five kisses for it take.\n\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka and Naz\xc3\xa1rka broke into the ring and started walking about\namong the girls. Luk\xc3\xa1shka joined in the singing, taking seconds in his\nclear voice as he walked in the middle of the ring swinging his arms.\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, come in, one of you!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. The other girls pushed Mary\xc3\xa1nka,\nbut she would not enter the ring. The sound of shrill laughter, slaps,\nkisses, and whispers mingled with the singing.\n\nAs he went past Ol\xc3\xa9nin, Luk\xc3\xa1shka gave a friendly nod.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDm\xc3\xadtri Andr\xc3\xa9ich! Have you too come to have a look?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Ol\xc3\xa9nin dryly.\n\nBel\xc3\xa9tski stooped and whispered something into \xc3\x9astenka\xe2\x80\x99s ear. She had\nnot time to reply till she came round again, when she said:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, we\xe2\x80\x99ll come.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd Mary\xc3\xa1nka too?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin stooped towards Mary\xc3\xa1nka. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll come? Please do, if only for a\nminute. I must speak to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf the other girls come, I will.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you answer my question?\xe2\x80\x9d said he, bending towards her. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are\nin good spirits today.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nShe had already moved past him. He went after her.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you answer?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnswer what?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe question I asked you the other day,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ol\xc3\xa9nin, stooping to her\near. \xe2\x80\x9cWill you marry me?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nMary\xc3\xa1nka thought for a moment.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you tonight.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAnd through the darkness her eyes gleamed brightly and kindly at the\nyoung man.\n\nHe still followed her. He enjoyed stooping closer to her. But Luk\xc3\xa1shka,\nwithout ceasing to sing, suddenly seized her firmly by the hand and\npulled her from her place in the ring of girls into the middle. Ol\xc3\xa9nin\nhad only time to say, \xe2\x80\x9cCome to \xc3\x9astenka\xe2\x80\x99s,\xe2\x80\x9d and stepped back to his\ncompanion.\n\nThe song came to an end. Luk\xc3\xa1shka wiped his lips, Mary\xc3\xa1nka did the\nsame, and they kissed. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, kisses five!\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka. Chatter,\nlaughter, and running about, succeeded to the rhythmic movements and\nsound. Luk\xc3\xa1shka, who seemed to have drunk a great deal, began to\ndistribute sweetmeats to the girls.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI offer them to everyone!\xe2\x80\x9d he said with proud, comically pathetic\nself-admiration. \xe2\x80\x9cBut anyone who goes after soldiers goes out of the\nring!\xe2\x80\x9d he suddenly added, with an angry glance at Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\nThe girls grabbed his sweetmeats from him, and, laughing, struggled for\nthem among themselves. Bel\xc3\xa9tski and Ol\xc3\xa9nin stepped aside.\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka, as if ashamed of his generosity, took off his cap and wiping\nhis forehead with his sleeve came up to Mary\xc3\xa1nka and \xc3\x9astenka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnswer me, my dear, dost thou hold me in contempt?\xe2\x80\x9d he said in the\nwords of the song they had just been singing, and turning to Mary\xc3\xa1nka\nhe angrily repeated the words: \xe2\x80\x9cDost thou hold me in contempt? When we\nshall married be thou wilt weep because of me!\xe2\x80\x9d he added, embracing\n\xc3\x9astenka and Mary\xc3\xa1nka both together.\n\n\xc3\x9astenka tore herself away, and swinging her arm gave him such a blow on\nthe back that she hurt her hand.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, are you going to have another turn?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe other girls may if they like,\xe2\x80\x9d answered \xc3\x9astenka, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I am going\nhome and Mary\xc3\xa1nka was coming to our house too.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nWith his arm still round her, Luk\xc3\xa1shka led Mary\xc3\xa1nka away from the crowd\nto the darker corner of a house.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t go, Mary\xc3\xa1nka,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9clet\xe2\x80\x99s have some fun for the last time.\nGo home and I will come to you!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat am I to do at home? Holidays are meant for merrymaking. I am\ngoing to \xc3\x9astenka\xe2\x80\x99s,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Mary\xc3\xa1nka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll marry you all the same, you know!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary\xc3\xa1nka, \xe2\x80\x9cwe shall see when the time comes.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you are going,\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka sternly, and, pressing her close, he\nkissed her on the cheek.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere, leave off! Don\xe2\x80\x99t bother,\xe2\x80\x9d and Mary\xc3\xa1nka, wrenching herself from\nhis arms, moved away.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh my girl, it will turn out badly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka reproachfully and\nstood still, shaking his head. \xe2\x80\x9cThou wilt weep because of me...\xe2\x80\x9d and\nturning away from her he shouted to the other girls:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow then! Play away!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nWhat he had said seemed to have frightened and vexed Mary\xc3\xa1nka. She\nstopped, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat will turn out badly?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, that!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat what?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, that you keep company with a soldier-lodger and no longer care\nfor me!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll care just as long as I choose. You\xe2\x80\x99re not my father, nor my\nmother. What do you want? I\xe2\x80\x99ll care for whom I like!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, all right...\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka, \xe2\x80\x9cbut remember!\xe2\x80\x9d He moved towards\nthe shop. \xe2\x80\x9cGirls!\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted, \xe2\x80\x9cwhy have you stopped? Go on dancing.\nNaz\xc3\xa1rka, fetch some more _chikhir_.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, will they come?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ol\xc3\xa9nin, addressing Bel\xc3\xa9tski.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99ll come directly,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Bel\xc3\xa9tski. \xe2\x80\x9cCome along, we must prepare\nthe ball.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XXXIX\n\n\nIt was already late in the night when Ol\xc3\xa9nin came out of Bel\xc3\xa9tski\xe2\x80\x99s hut\nfollowing Mary\xc3\xa1nka and \xc3\x9astenka. He saw in the dark street before him\nthe gleam of the girl\xe2\x80\x99s white kerchief. The golden moon was descending\ntowards the steppe. A silvery mist hung over the village. All was\nstill; there were no lights anywhere and one heard only the receding\nfootsteps of the young women. Ol\xc3\xa9nin\xe2\x80\x99s heart beat fast. The fresh moist\natmosphere cooled his burning face. He glanced at the sky and turned to\nlook at the hut he had just come out of: the candle was already out.\nThen he again peered through the darkness at the girls\xe2\x80\x99 retreating\nshadows. The white kerchief disappeared in the mist. He was afraid to\nremain alone, he was so happy. He jumped down from the porch and ran\nafter the girls.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBother you, someone may see...\xe2\x80\x9d said \xc3\x9astenka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin ran up to Mary\xc3\xa1nka and embraced her.\n\nMary\xc3\xa1nka did not resist.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHaven\xe2\x80\x99t you kissed enough yet?\xe2\x80\x9d said \xc3\x9astenka. \xe2\x80\x9cMarry and then kiss,\nbut now you\xe2\x80\x99d better wait.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-night, Mary\xc3\xa1nka. Tomorrow I will come to see your father and tell\nhim. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you say anything.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy should I!\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mary\xc3\xa1nka.\n\nBoth the girls started running. Ol\xc3\xa9nin went on by himself thinking over\nall that had happened. He had spent the whole evening alone with her in\na corner by the oven. \xc3\x9astenka had not left the hut for a single moment,\nbut had romped about with the other girls and with Bel\xc3\xa9tski all the\ntime. Ol\xc3\xa9nin had talked in whispers to Mary\xc3\xa1nka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you marry me?\xe2\x80\x9d he had asked.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99d deceive me and not have me,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied cheerfully and calmly.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut do you love me? Tell me for God\xe2\x80\x99s sake!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t I love you? You don\xe2\x80\x99t squint,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mary\xc3\xa1nka,\nlaughing and with her hard hands squeezing his....\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat whi-ite, whi-i-ite, soft hands you\xe2\x80\x99ve got\xe2\x80\x94so like clotted cream,\xe2\x80\x9d\nshe said.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am in earnest. Tell me, will you marry me?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not, if father gives me to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell then remember, I shall go mad if you deceive me. Tomorrow I will\ntell your mother and father. I shall come and propose.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nMary\xc3\xa1nka suddenly burst out laughing.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems so funny!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s true! I will buy a vineyard and a house and will enroll myself as\na Cossack.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMind you don\xe2\x80\x99t go after other women then. I am severe about that.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin joyfully repeated all these words to himself. The memory of them\nnow gave him pain and now such joy that it took away his breath. The\npain was because she had remained as calm as usual while talking to\nhim. She did not seem at all agitated by these new conditions. It was\nas if she did not trust him and did not think of the future. It seemed\nto him that she only loved him for the present moment, and that in her\nmind there was no future with him. He was happy because her words\nsounded to him true, and she had consented to be his. \xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d thought he\nto himself, \xe2\x80\x9cwe shall only understand one another when she is quite\nmine. For such love there are no words. It needs life\xe2\x80\x94the whole of\nlife. Tomorrow everything will be cleared up. I cannot live like this\nany longer; tomorrow I will tell everything to her father, to Bel\xc3\xa9tski,\nand to the whole village.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka, after two sleepless nights, had drunk so much at the f\xc3\xaate\nthat for the first time in his life his feet would not carry him, and\nhe slept in Y\xc3\xa1mka\xe2\x80\x99s house.\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XL\n\n\nThe next day Ol\xc3\xa9nin awoke earlier than usual, and immediately\nremembered what lay before him, and he joyfully recalled her kisses,\nthe pressure of her hard hands, and her words, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat white hands you\nhave!\xe2\x80\x9d He jumped up and wished to go at once to his hosts\xe2\x80\x99 hut to ask\nfor their consent to his marriage with Mary\xc3\xa1nka. The sun had not yet\nrisen, but it seemed that there was an unusual bustle in the street and\nside-street: people were moving about on foot and on horseback, and\ntalking. He threw on his Circassian coat and hastened out into the\nporch. His hosts were not yet up. Five Cossacks were riding past and\ntalking loudly together. In front rode Luk\xc3\xa1shka on his broad-backed\nKabard\xc3\xa1 horse.\n\nThe Cossacks were all speaking and shouting so that it was impossible\nto make out exactly what they were saying.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cRide to the Upper Post,\xe2\x80\x9d shouted one.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSaddle and catch us up, be quick,\xe2\x80\x9d said another.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s nearer through the other gate!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you talking about?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Luk\xc3\xa1shka. \xe2\x80\x9cWe must go through the\nmiddle gates, of course.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo we must, it\xe2\x80\x99s nearer that way,\xe2\x80\x9d said one of the Cossacks who was\ncovered with dust and rode a perspiring horse. Luk\xc3\xa1shka\xe2\x80\x99s face was red\nand swollen after the drinking of the previous night and his cap was\npushed to the back of his head. He was calling out with authority as\nthough he were an officer.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the matter?
