Where are you going?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ol\xc3\xa9nin, with\ndifficulty attracting the Cossacks\xe2\x80\x99 attention.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are off to catch _abreks_. They\xe2\x80\x99re hiding among the sand-drifts. We\nare just off, but there are not enough of us yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAnd the Cossacks continued to shout, more and more of them joining as\nthey rode down the street. It occurred to Ol\xc3\xa9nin that it would not look\nwell for him to stay behind; besides he thought he could soon come\nback. He dressed, loaded his gun with bullets, jumped onto his horse\nwhich Vany\xc3\xbasha had saddled more or less well, and overtook the Cossacks\nat the village gates. The Cossacks had dismounted, and filling a wooden\nbowl with _chikhir_ from a little cask which they had brought with\nthem, they passed the bowl round to one another and drank to the\nsuccess of their expedition. Among them was a smartly dressed young\ncornet, who happened to be in the village and who took command of the\ngroup of nine Cossacks who had joined for the expedition. All these\nCossacks were privates, and although the cornet assumed the airs of a\ncommanding officer, they only obeyed Luk\xc3\xa1shka. Of Ol\xc3\xa9nin they took no\nnotice at all, and when they had all mounted and started, and Ol\xc3\xa9nin\nrode up to the cornet and began asking him what was taking place, the\ncornet, who was usually quite friendly, treated him with marked\ncondescension. It was with great difficulty that Ol\xc3\xa9nin managed to find\nout from him what was happening. Scouts who had been sent out to search\nfor _abreks_ had come upon several hillsmen some six miles from the\nvillage. These _abreks_ had taken shelter in pits and had fired at the\nscouts, declaring they would not surrender. A corporal who had been\nscouting with two Cossacks had remained to watch the _abreks_, and had\nsent one Cossack back to get help.\n\nThe sun was just rising. Three miles beyond the village the steppe\nspread out and nothing was visible except the dry, monotonous, sandy,\ndismal plain covered with the footmarks of cattle, and here and there\nwith tufts of withered grass, with low reeds in the flats, and rare,\nlittle-trodden footpaths, and the camps of the nomad Nog\xc3\xa1y tribe just\nvisible far away. The absence of shade and the austere aspect of the\nplace were striking. The sun always rises and sets red in the steppe.\nWhen it is windy whole hills of sand are carried by the wind from place\nto place.\n\nWhen it is calm, as it was that morning, the silence, uninterrupted by\nany movement or sound, is peculiarly striking. That morning in the\nsteppe it was quiet and dull, though the sun had already risen. It all\nseemed specially soft and desolate. The air was hushed, the footfalls\nand the snorting of the horses were the only sounds to be heard, and\neven they quickly died away.\n\nThe men rode almost silently. A Cossack always carries his weapons so\nthat they neither jingle nor rattle. Jingling weapons are a terrible\ndisgrace to a Cossack. Two other Cossacks from the village caught the\nparty up and exchanged a few words. Luk\xc3\xa1shka\xe2\x80\x99s horse either stumbled or\ncaught its foot in some grass, and became restive\xe2\x80\x94which is a sign of\nbad luck among the Cossacks, and at such a time was of special\nimportance. The others exchanged glances and turned away, trying not to\nnotice what had happened. Lukaskha pulled at the reins, frowned\nsternly, set his teeth, and flourished his whip above his head. His\ngood Kabard\xc3\xa1 horse, prancing from one foot to another not knowing with\nwhich to start, seemed to wish to fly upwards on wings. But Luk\xc3\xa1shka\nhit its well-fed sides with his whip once, then again, and a third\ntime, and the horse, showing its teeth and spreading out its tail,\nsnorted and reared and stepped on its hind legs a few paces away from\nthe others.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, a good steed that!\xe2\x80\x9d said the cornet.\n\nThat he said _steed_ instead of _horse_ indicated special praise.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cA lion of a horse,\xe2\x80\x9d assented one of the others, an old Cossack.\n\nThe Cossacks rode forward silently, now at a footpace, then at a trot,\nand these changes were the only incidents that interrupted for a moment\nthe stillness and solemnity of their movements.\n\nRiding through the steppe for about six miles, they passed nothing but\none Nog\xc3\xa1y tent, placed on a cart and moving slowly along at a distance\nof about a mile from them. A Nog\xc3\xa1y family was moving from one part of\nthe steppe to another. Afterwards they met two tattered Nog\xc3\xa1y women\nwith high cheekbones, who with baskets on their backs were gathering\ndung left by the cattle that wandered over the steppe. The cornet, who\ndid not know their language well, tried to question them, but they did\nnot understand him and, obviously frightened, looked at one another.\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka rode up to them both, stopped his horse, and promptly uttered\nthe usual greeting. The Nog\xc3\xa1y women were evidently relieved, and began\nspeaking to him quite freely as to a brother.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Ay-ay, kop abrek!_\xe2\x80\x9d they said plaintively, pointing in the direction\nin which the Cossacks were going. Ol\xc3\xa9nin understood that they were\nsaying, \xe2\x80\x9cMany _abreks_.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nNever having seen an engagement of that kind, and having formed an idea\nof them only from Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka\xe2\x80\x99s tales, Ol\xc3\xa9nin wished not to be left\nbehind by the Cossacks, but wanted to see it all. He admired the\nCossacks, and was on the watch, looking and listening and making his\nown observations. Though he had brought his sword and a loaded gun with\nhim, when he noticed that the Cossacks avoided him he decided to take\nno part in the action, as in his opinion his courage had already been\nsufficiently proved when he was with his detachment, and also because\nhe was very happy.\n\nSuddenly a shot was heard in the distance.\n\nThe cornet became excited, and began giving orders to the Cossacks as\nto how they should divide and from which side they should approach. But\nthe Cossacks did not appear to pay any attention to these orders,\nlistening only to what Luk\xc3\xa1shka said and looking to him alone.\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka\xe2\x80\x99s face and figure were expressive of calm solemnity. He put\nhis horse to a trot with which the others were unable to keep pace, and\nscrewing up his eyes kept looking ahead.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s a man on horseback,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, reining in his horse and keeping\nin line with the others.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin looked intently, but could not see anything. The Cossacks soon\ndistinguished two riders and quietly rode straight towards them.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre those the _abreks?_\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\nThe Cossacks did not answer his question, which appeared quite\nmeaningless to them. The _abreks_ would have been fools to venture\nacross the river on horseback.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s friend R\xc3\xb3dka waving to us, I do believe,\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka,\npointing to the two mounted men who were now clearly visible. \xe2\x80\x9cLook,\nhe\xe2\x80\x99s coming to us.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nA few minutes later it became plain that the two horsemen were the\nCossack scouts. The corporal rode up to Luk\xc3\xa1shka.\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XLI\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre they far?\xe2\x80\x9d was all Luk\xc3\xa1shka said.\n\nJust then they heard a sharp shot some thirty paces off. The corporal\nsmiled slightly.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOur G\xc3\xbarka is having shots at them,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, nodding in the direction\nof the shot.\n\nHaving gone a few paces farther they saw G\xc3\xbarka sitting behind a\nsand-hillock and loading his gun. To while away the time he was\nexchanging shots with the _abreks_, who were behind another sand-heap.\nA bullet came whistling from their side.\n\nThe cornet was pale and grew confused. Luk\xc3\xa1shka dismounted from his\nhorse, threw the reins to one of the other Cossacks, and went up to\nG\xc3\xbarka. Ol\xc3\xa9nin also dismounted and, bending down, followed Luk\xc3\xa1shka.\nThey had hardly reached G\xc3\xbarka when two bullets whistled above them.\n\nLuk\xc3\xa1shka looked around laughing at Ol\xc3\xa9nin and stooped a little.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook out or they will kill you, Dm\xc3\xadtri Andr\xc3\xa9ich,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99d\nbetter go away\xe2\x80\x94you have no business here.\xe2\x80\x9d But Ol\xc3\xa9nin wanted absolutely\nto see the _abreks_.\n\nFrom behind the mound he saw caps and muskets some two hundred paces\noff. Suddenly a little cloud of smoke appeared from thence, and again a\nbullet whistled past. The _abreks_ were hiding in a marsh at the foot\nof the hill. Ol\xc3\xa9nin was much impressed by the place in which they sat.\nIn reality it was very much like the rest of the steppe, but because\nthe _abreks_ sat there it seemed to detach itself from all the rest and\nto have become distinguished. Indeed it appeared to Ol\xc3\xa9nin that it was\nthe very spot for _abreks_ to occupy. Luk\xc3\xa1shka went back to his horse\nand Ol\xc3\xa9nin followed him.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must get a hay-cart,\xe2\x80\x9d said Luk\xc3\xa1shka, \xe2\x80\x9cor they will be killing some\nof us. There behind that mound is a Nog\xc3\xa1y cart with a load of hay.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nThe cornet listened to him and the corporal agreed. The cart of hay was\nfetched, and the Cossacks, hiding behind it, pushed it forward. Ol\xc3\xa9nin\nrode up a hillock from whence he could see everything. The hay-cart\nmoved on and the Cossacks crowded together behind it. The Cossacks\nadvanced, but the Ch\xc3\xa9chens, of whom there were nine, sat with their\nknees in a row and did not fire.\n\nAll was quiet. Suddenly from the Ch\xc3\xa9chens arose the sound of a mournful\nsong, something like Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cAy day, dalalay.\xe2\x80\x9d The Ch\xc3\xa9chens\nknew that they could not escape, and to prevent themselves from being\ntempted to take to flight they had strapped themselves together, knee\nto knee, had got their guns ready, and were singing their death-song.\n\nThe Cossacks with their hay-cart drew closer and closer, and Ol\xc3\xa9nin\nexpected the firing to begin at any moment, but the silence was only\nbroken by the _abreks_\xe2\x80\x99 mournful song. Suddenly the song ceased; there\nwas a sharp report, a bullet struck the front of the cart, and Ch\xc3\xa9chen\ncurses and yells broke the silence and shot followed on shot and one\nbullet after another struck the cart. The Cossacks did not fire and\nwere now only five paces distant.\n\nAnother moment passed and the Cossacks with a whoop rushed out on both\nsides from behind the cart\xe2\x80\x94Luk\xc3\xa1shka in front of them. Ol\xc3\xa9nin heard only\na few shots, then shouting and moans. He thought he saw smoke and\nblood, and abandoning his horse and quite beside himself he ran towards\nthe Cossacks. Horror seemed to blind him. He could not make out\nanything, but understood that all was over. Luk\xc3\xa1shka, pale as death,\nwas holding a wounded Ch\xc3\xa9chen by the arms and shouting, \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t kill\nhim. I\xe2\x80\x99ll take him alive!\xe2\x80\x9d The Ch\xc3\xa9chen was the red-haired man who had\nfetched his brother\xe2\x80\x99s body away after Luk\xc3\xa1shka had killed him. Luk\xc3\xa1shka\nwas twisting his arms. Suddenly the Ch\xc3\xa9chen wrenched himself free and\nfired his pistol. Luk\xc3\xa1shka fell, and blood began to flow from his\nstomach. He jumped up, but fell again, swearing in Russian and in\nTartar. More and more blood appeared on his clothes and under him. Some\nCossacks approached him and began loosening his girdle. One of them,\nNaz\xc3\xa1rka, before beginning to help, fumbled for some time, unable to put\nhis sword in its sheath: it would not go the right way. The blade of\nthe sword was blood-stained.\n\nThe Ch\xc3\xa9chens with their red hair and clipped moustaches lay dead and\nhacked about. Only the one we know of, who had fired at Luk\xc3\xa1shka,\nthough wounded in many places was still alive. Like a wounded hawk all\ncovered with blood (blood was flowing from a wound under his right\neye), pale and gloomy, he looked about him with wide-open excited eyes\nand clenched teeth as he crouched, dagger in hand, still prepared to\ndefend himself. The cornet went up to him as if intending to pass by,\nand with a quick movement shot him in the ear. The Ch\xc3\xa9chen started up,\nbut it was too late, and he fell.\n\nThe Cossacks, quite out of breath, dragged the bodies aside and took\nthe weapons from them. Each of the red-haired Ch\xc3\xa9chens had been a man,\nand each one had his own individual expression. Luk\xc3\xa1shka was carried to\nthe cart. He continued to swear in Russian and in Tartar.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo fear, I\xe2\x80\x99ll strangle him with my hands. _Anna seni!_\xe2\x80\x9d he cried,\nstruggling. But he soon became quiet from weakness.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin rode home. In the evening he was told that Luk\xc3\xa1shka was at\ndeath\xe2\x80\x99s door, but that a Tartar from beyond the river had undertaken to\ncure him with herbs.\n\nThe bodies were brought to the village office. The women and the little\nboys hastened to look at them.\n\nIt was growing dark when Ol\xc3\xa9nin returned, and he could not collect\nhimself after what he had seen. But towards night memories of the\nevening before came rushing to his mind. He looked out of the window,\nMary\xc3\xa1nka was passing to and fro from the house to the cowshed, putting\nthings straight. Her mother had gone to the vineyard and her father to\nthe office. Ol\xc3\xa9nin could not wait till she had quite finished her work,\nbut went out to meet her. She was in the hut standing with her back\ntowards him. Ol\xc3\xa9nin thought she felt shy.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMary\xc3\xa1nka,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cI say, Mary\xc3\xa1nka! May I come in?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nShe suddenly turned. There was a scarcely perceptible trace of tears in\nher eyes and her face was beautiful in its sadness. She looked at him\nin silent dignity.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin again said:\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMary\xc3\xa1nka, I have come\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cLeave me alone!\xe2\x80\x9d she said. Her face did not change but the tears ran\ndown her cheeks.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you crying for? What is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d she repeated in a rough voice. \xe2\x80\x9cCossacks have been killed,\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s what for.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cLuk\xc3\xa1shka?\xe2\x80\x9d said Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo away! What do you want?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMary\xc3\xa1nka!\xe2\x80\x9d said Ol\xc3\xa9nin, approaching her.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will never get anything from me!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cMary\xc3\xa1nka, don\xe2\x80\x99t speak like that,\xe2\x80\x9d Ol\xc3\xa9nin entreated.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGet away. I\xe2\x80\x99m sick of you!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted the girl, stamping her foot, and\nmoved threateningly towards him. And her face expressed such\nabhorrence, such contempt, and such anger that Ol\xc3\xa9nin suddenly\nunderstood that there was no hope for him, and that his first\nimpression of this woman\xe2\x80\x99s inaccessibility had been perfectly correct.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin said nothing more, but ran out of the hut.\n\n\n\n\n Chapter XLII\n\n\nFor two hours after returning home he lay on his bed motionless. Then\nhe went to his company commander and obtained leave to visit the staff.\nWithout taking leave of anyone, and sending Vany\xc3\xbasha to settle his\naccounts with his landlord, he prepared to leave for the fort where his\nregiment was stationed. Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka was the only one to see him off.\nThey had a drink, and then a second, and then yet another. Again as on\nthe night of his departure from Moscow, a three-horsed conveyance stood\nwaiting at the door. But Ol\xc3\xa9nin did not confer with himself as he had\ndone then, and did not say to himself that all he had thought and done\nhere was \xe2\x80\x9cnot it\xe2\x80\x9d. He did not promise himself a new life. He loved\nMary\xc3\xa1nka more than ever, and knew that he could never be loved by her.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, good-bye, my lad!\xe2\x80\x9d said Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen you go on an\nexpedition, be wise and listen to my words\xe2\x80\x94the words of an old man.\nWhen you are out on a raid or the like (you know I\xe2\x80\x99m an old wolf and\nhave seen things), and when they begin firing, don\xe2\x80\x99t get into a crowd\nwhere there are many men. When you fellows get frightened you always\ntry to get close together with a lot of others. You think it is merrier\nto be with others, but that\xe2\x80\x99s where it is worst of all! They always aim\nat a crowd. Now I used to keep farther away from the others and went\nalone, and I\xe2\x80\x99ve never been wounded. Yet what things haven\xe2\x80\x99t I seen in\nmy day?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you\xe2\x80\x99ve got a bullet in your back,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Vany\xc3\xbasha, who was\nclearing up the room.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was the Cossacks fooling about,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Er\xc3\xb3shka.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cCossacks? How was that?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, just so. We were drinking. V\xc3\xa1nka S\xc3\xadtkin, one of the Cossacks, got\nmerry, and puff! he gave me one from his pistol just here.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and did it hurt?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ol\xc3\xa9nin. \xe2\x80\x9cVany\xc3\xbasha, will you soon be\nready?\xe2\x80\x9d he added.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, where\xe2\x80\x99s the hurry! Let me tell you. When he banged into me, the\nbullet did not break the bone but remained here. And I say: \xe2\x80\x98You\xe2\x80\x99ve\nkilled me, brother. Eh! What have you done to me? I won\xe2\x80\x99t let you off!\nYou\xe2\x80\x99ll have to stand me a pailful!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, but did it hurt?\xe2\x80\x9d Ol\xc3\xa9nin asked again, scarcely listening to the\ntale.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me finish. He stood a pailful, and we drank it, but the blood went\non flowing. The whole room was drenched and covered with blood. Grandad\nBurl\xc3\xa1k, he says, \xe2\x80\x98The lad will give up the ghost. Stand a bottle of the\nsweet sort, or we shall have you taken up!\xe2\x80\x99 They bought more drink, and\nboozed and boozed\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but did it hurt you much?\xe2\x80\x9d Ol\xc3\xa9nin asked once more.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHurt, indeed! Don\xe2\x80\x99t interrupt: I don\xe2\x80\x99t like it. Let me finish. We\nboozed and boozed till morning, and I fell asleep on the top of the\noven, drunk. When I woke in the morning I could not unbend myself\nanyhow\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas it very painful?\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Ol\xc3\xa9nin, thinking that now he would at\nlast get an answer to his question.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid I tell you it was painful? I did not say it was painful, but I\ncould not bend and could not walk.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd then it healed up?\xe2\x80\x9d said Ol\xc3\xa9nin, not even laughing, so heavy was\nhis heart.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt healed up, but the bullet is still there. Just feel it!\xe2\x80\x9d And\nlifting his shirt he showed his powerful back, where just near the bone\na bullet could be felt and rolled about.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cFeel how it rolls,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, evidently amusing himself with the bullet\nas with a toy. \xe2\x80\x9cThere now, it has rolled to the back.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd Luk\xc3\xa1shka, will he recover?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHeaven only knows! There\xe2\x80\x99s no doctor. They\xe2\x80\x99ve gone for one.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere will they get one? From Gr\xc3\xb3znoe?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ol\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, my lad. Were I the Tsar I\xe2\x80\x99d have hung all your Russian doctors\nlong ago. Cutting is all they know! There\xe2\x80\x99s our Cossack Bakl\xc3\xa1shka, no\nlonger a real man now that they\xe2\x80\x99ve cut off his leg! That shows they\xe2\x80\x99re\nfools. What\xe2\x80\x99s Bakl\xc3\xa1shka good for now? No, my lad, in the mountains\nthere are real doctors. There was my chum, V\xc3\xb3rchik, he was on an\nexpedition and was wounded just here in the chest. Well, your doctors\ngave him up, but one of theirs came from the mountains and cured him!\nThey understand herbs, my lad!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, stop talking rubbish,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ol\xc3\xa9nin. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d better send a doctor\nfrom head-quarters.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cRubbish!\xe2\x80\x9d the old man said mockingly. \xe2\x80\x9cFool, fool! Rubbish. You\xe2\x80\x99ll\nsend a doctor!\xe2\x80\x94If yours cured people, Cossacks and Ch\xc3\xa9chens would go to\nyou for treatment, but as it is your officers and colonels send to the\nmountains for doctors. Yours are all humbugs, all humbugs.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin did not answer. He agreed only too fully that all was humbug in\nthe world in which he had lived and to which he was now returning.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow is Luk\xc3\xa1shka? You\xe2\x80\x99ve been to see him?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe just lies as if he were dead. He does not eat nor drink. Vodka is\nthe only thing his soul accepts. But as long as he drinks vodka it\xe2\x80\x99s\nwell. I\xe2\x80\x99d be sorry to lose the lad. A fine lad\xe2\x80\x94a brave, like me. I too\nlay dying like that once. The old women were already wailing. My head\nwas burning. They had already laid me out under the holy icons. So I\nlay there, and above me on the oven little drummers, no bigger than\nthis, beat the tattoo. I shout at them and they drum all the harder.\xe2\x80\x9d\n(The old man laughed.) \xe2\x80\x9cThe women brought our church elder. They were\ngetting ready to bury me. They said, \xe2\x80\x98He defiled himself with worldly\nunbelievers; he made merry with women; he ruined people; he did not\nfast, and he played the _balal\xc3\xa1yka_. Confess,\xe2\x80\x99 they said. So I began to\nconfess. \xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99ve sinned!\xe2\x80\x99 I said. Whatever the priest said, I always\nanswered \xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99ve sinned.\xe2\x80\x99 He began to ask me about the _balal\xc3\xa1yka_.\n\xe2\x80\x98Where is the accursed thing,\xe2\x80\x99 he says. \xe2\x80\x98Show it me and smash it.\xe2\x80\x99 But\nI say, \xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99ve not got it.\xe2\x80\x99 I\xe2\x80\x99d hidden it myself in a net in the\nouthouse. I knew they could not find it. So they left me. Yet after all\nI recovered. When I went for my _balal\xc3\xa1yka_\xe2\x80\x94What was I saying?\xe2\x80\x9d he\ncontinued. \xe2\x80\x9cListen to me, and keep farther away from the other men or\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll get killed foolishly. I feel for you, truly: you are a drinker\xe2\x80\x94I\nlove you! And fellows like you like riding up the mounds. There was one\nwho lived here who had come from Russia, he always would ride up the\nmounds (he called the mounds so funnily, \xe2\x80\x98hillocks\xe2\x80\x99). Whenever he saw a\nmound, off he\xe2\x80\x99d gallop. Once he galloped off that way and rode to the\ntop quite pleased, but a Ch\xc3\xa9chen fired at him and killed him! Ah, how\nwell they shoot from their gun-rests, those Ch\xc3\xa9chens! Some of them\nshoot even better than I do. I don\xe2\x80\x99t like it when a fellow gets killed\nso foolishly! Sometimes I used to look at your soldiers and wonder at\nthem. There\xe2\x80\x99s foolishness for you! They go, the poor fellows, all in a\nclump, and even sew red collars to their coats! How can they help being\nhit! One gets killed, they drag him away and another takes his place!\nWhat foolishness!\xe2\x80\x9d the old man repeated, shaking his head. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy not\nscatter, and go one by one? So you just go like that and they won\xe2\x80\x99t\nnotice you. That\xe2\x80\x99s what you must do.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, thank you! Good-bye, Daddy. God willing we may meet again,\xe2\x80\x9d said\nOl\xc3\xa9nin, getting up and moving towards the passage.\n\nThe old man, who was sitting on the floor, did not rise.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that the way one says \xe2\x80\x98Good-bye\xe2\x80\x99? Fool, fool!\xe2\x80\x9d he began. \xe2\x80\x9cOh dear,\nwhat has come to people? We\xe2\x80\x99ve kept company, kept company for well-nigh\na year, and now \xe2\x80\x98Good-bye!\xe2\x80\x99 and off he goes! Why, I love you, and how I\npity you! You are so forlorn, always alone, always alone. You\xe2\x80\x99re\nsomehow so unsociable. At times I can\xe2\x80\x99t sleep for thinking about you. I\nam so sorry for you. As the song has it:\n\nIt is very hard, dear brother,\nIn a foreign land to live.\n\n\nSo it is with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, good-bye,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ol\xc3\xa9nin again.\n\nThe old man rose and held out his hand. Ol\xc3\xa9nin pressed it and turned to\ngo.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive us your mug, your mug!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nAnd the old man took Ol\xc3\xa9nin by the head with both hands and kissed him\nthree times with wet moustaches and lips, and began to cry.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI love you, good-bye!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin got into the cart.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, is that how you\xe2\x80\x99re going? You might give me something for a\nremembrance. Give me a gun! What do you want two for?\xe2\x80\x9d said the old\nman, sobbing quite sincerely.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin got out a musket and gave it to him.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a lot you\xe2\x80\x99ve given the old fellow,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Vany\xc3\xbasha, \xe2\x80\x9che\xe2\x80\x99ll\nnever have enough! A regular old beggar. They are all such irregular\npeople,\xe2\x80\x9d he remarked, as he wrapped himself in his overcoat and took\nhis seat on the box.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHold your tongue, swine!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the old man, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat a\nstingy fellow!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\nMary\xc3\xa1nka came out of the cowshed, glanced indifferently at the cart,\nbowed and went towards the hut.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9c_La fille!_\xe2\x80\x9d said Vany\xc3\xbasha, with a wink, and burst out into a silly\nlaugh.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDrive on!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Ol\xc3\xa9nin, angrily.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-bye, my lad! Good-bye. I won\xe2\x80\x99t forget you!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Er\xc3\xb3shka.\n\nOl\xc3\xa9nin turned round. Daddy Er\xc3\xb3shka was talking to Mary\xc3\xa1nka, evidently\nabout his own affairs, and neither the old man nor the girl looked at\nOl\xc3\xa9nin.\n\n\n\n'
